HEADLINE: Development for New York City;
The West Side Jets

 BODY:
Mayor Rudolph Giuliani's proposal to build a football stadium on the West Side of Manhattan, perhaps for the New York Jets, has suddenly taken on new life. It has also generated understandable skepticism from New Yorkers. Nevertheless, the idea is more promising than it might seem at first. The Jets are said to be extremely interested. The earlier idea of moving Yankee Stadium to the West Side is effectively dead, especially now that Gov. George Pataki has come out against it. More important, City Hall and the governor's office are mulling some financial arrangements that might work and also speed the redevelopment of a poorly served and underutilized area of Manhattan.
Mr. Giuliani and Mr. Pataki are both clearly thinking about their legacies, but that is no reason not to look searchingly at the stadium proposal being studied by their staffs. Three criteria should be applied in making a judgment about its worthiness. First, a stadium should have multiple uses and serve as an available annex to the Javits Convention Center, which needs to expand and construct a large meeting space if it is to lure the big meetings that increasingly are going elsewhere. Second, to avoid traffic congestion and air pollution, the project has to be served primarily by mass transit rather than automobiles. Third, it must pay for itself without large public subsidies beyond valid infrastructure projects. The surprise is that it may be possible to meet all three of these criteria. 
 George Steinbrenner is said to retain his dream of moving to the West Side. But the time has come for everyone to recognize that this idea no longer makes sense, if it ever did. A few years ago, the Yankees' owner said box-office receipts in the Bronx were lagging. Today they are breaking records. If Yankee Stadium is rebuilt at its current site, the fans will come. The Yankees can have their luxury skyboxes, and the city and state can upgrade the subway, car, train and even water links in an economical fashion that would improve the surrounding area. The same can be done for the Mets in Queens.
 The West Side stadium idea is being pushed most aggressively by the organization that hopes to lure the Olympics to New York City in 2012. Obviously, a stadium south of the Javits Center has to rise and fall on its own merits, irrespective of what might happen with the Olympics. Enter the New York Jets, who currently play in Giants Stadium at the Meadowlands, the only professional football team that has to share a stadium with another professional team. The Jets' new owner, Robert Wood Johnson IV, evidently wants to move. The team ought to pay for the West Side stadium itself if the city and state help finance the cost of a platform over the railroad yards next to the Hudson River.
What is most intriguing about the stadium proposal is its potential to redevelop the West Side south of 42nd Street, an area that now includes bus and truck parking lots, warehouses and other operations that could be moved to a less central location. As Charles Bagli reported last week in The Times, New York City is in desperate need of places to build new office space and housing. This page has called for the city to open up this area to commercial and residential development, a topic to which we will return from time to time. A transit station linking the stadium and the area to Amtrak, the Long Island Rail Road, Metro North and an extension of the No. 7 subway line is not an unrealistic idea. Public investment in such infrastructure would almost certainly generate new construction of offices, apartments and hotels. The use of incremental tax revenues from such projects could help finance the entire cost of construction.
The stadium idea is drawing fire from community groups, elected officials and others worried about congestion, expense and pollution. Their concerns cannot be dismissed. But it would also be a mistake to dismiss the stadium proposal as just another wild idea. It deserves to be looked at skeptically but also with an open mind.

HEADLINE: Rich Nations and Some Oil Producers Call for Price Cuts

BYLINE: By JOSEPH KAHN 

DATELINE: PRAGUE, Sept. 24

 BODY:
Rich nations claimed a breakthrough today in their battle against high oil prices after persuading several oil-producing countries to join them in calling for cheaper crude. But their intensive campaign to stabilize commodity and currency markets awaited the verdict of traders on Monday.
Saudi Arabia, Algeria, Russia and the United Arab Emirates joined the Group of 7 industrialized nations and other members of the committee that governs the International Monetary Fund in condemning current oil prices as harmful. Their agreement did not constitute a binding commitment, but financial officials presented it as tangible progress before OPEC holds a meeting in Caracas, Venezuela, this week. 
 "The committee is concerned that current oil prices, if sustained, could hamper global growth, add to inflationary pressures and adversely affect prospects for many countries," its communique said.
Finance ministers who are gathered here for the annual meetings of the monetary fund and the World Bank hope that their sustained oratorical campaign against a $30-plus barrel price for oil will have a marked impact on a volatile commodity market that is sometimes moved by psychology and speculation.
But they are financial officials, not energy ministers, and they have so far been unable to announce concrete steps that would lead to a greater supply of oil by winter. Today's communique, like one released Saturday, praised the United States decision last week to tap its substantial strategic oil reserves to help dampen prices. Though the statement noted that other nations would also consider using emergency reserves to increase supply, none had made such a commitment by today.
Finance ministers once expected that their meetings would be overshadowed by anti-globalization protests. So far, those protests have proven mostly peaceful and far smaller than anticipated. But the high price of oil and the falling euro -- what the ministers have called twin threats to the global economy -- have overtaken the agenda. The big test will come Monday, when markets reopen and traders vote on whether official promises to corral prices are credible.
Central bankers from the United States, Britain, Japan and Canada joined the European Central Bank on Friday in a concerted intervention to prop up the battered euro, which had fallen almost 30 percent since it went into use at the start of last year.
In delicately worded statements, they have effectively promised to buy euros and sell their own currencies again if the market does not get the message. They could be dragged into defending their stance as early as Monday if the euro slumps.
Nations that control the monetary fund gave Horst Kohler, the former German finance ministry official who now runs the Washington-based International Monetary Fund, the chance to present his vision for the global lending agency.
The fund has come under sustained attack for practicing bad economics and imposing too much on poor nations, though the ministers gathered here are strong supporters of the fund because they see its as a bulwark against financial panic.
"I have not come to the fund to turn it upside down," Mr. Kohler said before the meeting began. "But there is a further need for change, and even accelerated change."
As expected, the nations that control the fund backed a plan to provide debt relief to 20 nations by the end of this year, up from 10 that have already been granted relief. The effort, intended at least in part to head off demands from protesters, requires that the monetary fund and the World Bank ease some conditions they have imposed on debt relief in the past.
The nations also approved changes to the fund's lending programs intended to preserve the fund's capital for crises and wean it away from some long-term lending to more financially stable nations. The overhaul was pushed by Treasury Secretary Lawrence H. Summers of the United States.
But finance ministers did not endorse specific changes to the way the fund imposes capitalist economic and financial conditions on nations that borrow its money. Though Mr. Kohler has spoken of his vision of streamlining and even eliminating some of those conditions, the committee's communique today warned against any severing of strings attached to loans. It did back a review of the matter, however.
Protesters favoring debt relief held a mock funeral today for children they say die needlessly because their countries are so poor, attracting hundreds of people to central Prague. Organized by Jubilee 2000, a London-based umbrella group that advocates debt forgiveness, the march was significantly smaller than similar events held recently in Seattle, in Washington, D.C., and in Cologne, Germany.
Other protest groups have vowed to blockade World Bank and International Monetary Fund delegates around town on Tuesday. But some organizers are worried that the number of activists arriving in Prague will fall short of the expected 20,000.
Some protesters are blaming overly zealous authorities for the low turnout. The police today halted an Italian train carrying protesters at the Czech Republic's southern border and refused to let it pass until four blacklisted activists got off.
Lee Sustar, an American who said he writes for a socialist newspaper in Chicago and had come to Prague to cover the demonstrations, said by telephone from the Prague airport that he had been stopped there by border authorities and refused entry. He said the police told him that he was on a blacklist given to them by American officials. He added that he had been detained during anti-globalization protests in Seattle last year.

HEADLINE: Faltering Euro Faces Test in Markets Today

BYLINE: By EDMUND L. ANDREWS 

DATELINE: PRAGUE, Sept. 24

 BODY:
Having won pledges from the world's major central banks to support the euro, Europe's downtrodden single currency, the European Central Bank will have its toughness and its credibility tested on Monday by foreign exchange markets.
On Friday, the European bank bought up billions of dollars worth of euros and was joined by the United States Federal Reserve as well as the central banks of Japan, Britain and Canada. 
 Catching the markets by surprise, the central banks announced their "shared concern" about the euro's precipitous decline and suggested that they would intervene again if the euro's value against the dollar did not increase.
For the European Central Bank, which assumed power when the euro was born on Jan. 1, 1999, a successful intervention is crucial for its credibility and that of the currency itself.
Financial officials from Europe, the United States and Japan, who were here in Prague this weekend for meetings of the International Monetary Fund, spent much of the weekend talking up their commitment in the hope of jolting currency markets when they open for business on Monday.
"Markets understand that there is a clear will not to accept an undervalued euro," said Horst Kohler, managing director of the fund.
Ernst Welteke, president of Germany's central bank, the Bundesbank, said he was pleased at how well the intervention has gone so far.
But experts and currency traders are uncertain. The intervention on Friday appears to have involved only a few billion dollars and may have little long-term impact if it is not followed up by more waves of euro-buying and dollar-selling.
If the intervention has little effect and if central bankers do not intervene again, experts say, the damage to the currency will be much worse than if they had not decided to intervene at all.
Few people are more aware of the risks than the president of the European Central Bank, Wim Duisenberg of the Netherlands. Like many other central bankers, he is skeptical about the value of intervention. In December, when the euro sank below parity against the dollar for the first time, he was determined to wait for the markets to correct themselves.
When the euro's value fell to 88.4 cents in early May, Mr. Duisenberg came under increasing pressure from political leaders to intervene. Prime Minister Lionel Jospin of France called for "joint action" to shore up the euro and even proposed strengthening the role of finance ministers of countries using the euro in monetary policy.
Mr. Duisenberg did not budge, though he did issue a formal statement expressing sympathy with those who were alarmed by the euro's decline.
Mr. Duisenberg has been careful not to spell out his goals or to suggest how much money the central banks are willing to commit to rescuing the euro. But he seems determined to push its value up rather than simply to stabilize it, and anything short of an increase will be taken by markets as an even greater proof of the euro's dim prospects.

HEADLINE: THE 2000 CAMPAIGN: THE VICE PRESIDENT;
Differences on Medicare Take Center Stage in Gore Campaign

BYLINE: By KEVIN SACK 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 24

 BODY:
Vice President Al Gore opened a week dedicated to highlighting his differences with Gov. George W. Bush over Medicare by asserting today that Mr. Bush's plan for providing prescription drug benefits would force some low-income elderly people to go to welfare offices to qualify. Mr. Bush's aides called that contention "pure fantasy" and "a classic scare tactic."
The Bush campaign, meanwhile, vowed to wage a war against America's "education recession" in the week before the 2000 campaign becomes consumed by the long-awaited first debate between the Democratic and Republican presidential contenders. Aides at Mr. Bush's headquarters in Austin, Tex., buoyed by a polling rebound that accompanied the Texas governor's first good week in a month, also disparaged Mr. Gore for what they portrayed as his recent misstatements. 
 Mr. Gore's comments came in a 25-minute conference telephone call with reporters this afternoon from his official residence at the Naval Observatory here. The vice president departs on Monday for Florida, where he hopes to solidify his position among the state's pivotal elderly voters by releasing a new booklet containing his proposals for strengthening and expanding Medicare. He also plans to campaign this week in Michigan and Iowa before devoting the weekend to preparing for the first of three scheduled debates with Mr. Bush, on Oct. 3.
Mr. Bush, who spent the day in Texas and had no public schedule, embarks on a western swing to Oregon, Washington and California on Monday. By focusing on education, his campaign hopes to win over women, closing the gender gap that favors Mr. Gore. Bush aides also believe that the issue allows them to point to concrete accomplishments and statistics in Texas that demonstrate Mr. Bush's effectiveness in a given area of public policy.
The week dawns with a series of polls showing the race tightening into a dead heat, underscoring the importance of the debates, on Oct. 3 in Boston, Oct. 11 in Winston-Salem, N.C., and Oct. 17 in St. Louis.
A survey by Newsweek magazine gave Mr. Gore a lead of 3 percentage points, a gap within the margin of sampling error. Mr. Gore led by 14 percentage points in a Newsweek poll a week ago. A daily Gallup tracking poll showed Mr. Bush with a lead of 1 percentage point, while a Fox TV poll had the candidates even.
The polls were taken during a week in which Mr. Bush seemed to regain his footing after nearly a month on the defensive. He did his best to keep attention focused on a series of Gore campaign missteps, charged Mr. Gore with political opportunism for calling for the release of oil from the country's strategic petroleum reserve, and accused his rival of "misleading Americans."
Mr. Gore said today that he would remain focused on the issues, and said he would devote the week to discussing Medicare policy as a way to draw contrasts with Mr. Bush.
"We have some real and very significant differences," Mr. Gore said. "The other side treats the Medicare surplus as a piggy bank they can use for a tax cut that primarily benefits the wealthiest Americans. I'll veto the use of any money from Medicare for anything other than Medicare."
Mr. Gore has proposed a plan to extend Medicare's solvency by five years, from 2025 to 2030, by placing the anticipated $400 billion, 10-year Medicare surplus in a secure fund that could be used to reduce the federal debt. That, he projects, would save $75 billion in interest payments that would be used, in turn, to shore up Medicare.
In addition, Mr. Gore would spend $338 billion over 10 years to provide a universal prescription drug benefit under Medicare. Individuals making about $12,000 or less would have their drugs covered without charge, while those with higher incomes would pay premiums on a sliding scale. The Gore plan also would spend $40 billion to increase funding for nursing homes, teaching hospitals, rural hospitals, home health agencies and rehabilitative services, all areas that suffered deep Medicare cuts under the balanced budget act of 1997.
Mr. Bush's Medicare plan, which would cost a total of $198 billion, would create a system where private insurers compete with the government to provide coverage.
His market-based approach would essentially provide subsidies to older people who could spend the money on a variety of government-approved plans, some of which would include prescription drug coverage. For those with the lowest incomes, the government would provide drug coverage free. During the four years Mr. Bush estimates it would take to implement his plan, he would spend $48 billion on a temporary series of grants to states to subsidize coverage for the low-income elderly.
"For the first five years, they would force many seniors to go to welfare offices to sign up for state programs," Mr. Gore said, "and middle-class seniors would be left out completely."
Ari Fleischer, a spokesman for Mr. Bush, said that the welfare office accusation was wrong and that Mr. Bush's plan would expand on an existing program in 23 states where applicants return forms by mail.
"Al Gore spent last week making things up and today he's continued," Mr. Fleischer said.
Mr. Bush's assertion of an "education recession," a phrase that aides said tested well in internal campaign polling with women and independent voters, will coincide with the release of a Republican National Committee advertisement that makes the same point. The campaign will attempt to back up the charge by discussing declining test scores, safety concerns, and widening achievement gaps between rich and poor.
Mr. Fleischer used the phrase several times in a conference call with reporters today, saying the recession had "been present for the last 10 years," a span that dates to President George Bush's administration.

HEADLINE: Police Arrest 25 in Connection With Indonesia Bombings

BYLINE: By CALVIN SIMS 

DATELINE: JAKARTA, Indonesia, Sept. 24

 BODY:
The Indonesian police said today that they had arrested 25 people in connection with a recent series of bombings in Jakarta, including one that killed 15 people at the stock exchange. The police said that the suspects admitted carrying out the bombings and indicated that their next target was to be the American Embassy.
They said the suspects were arrested in Jakarta on Friday and Saturday based on tips and information gathered by police investigators. One suspect, armed with a grenade, indicated after he was picked up that he had been on his way to attack the American Embassy and a nearby crowded department store, they said. 
 "The suspects have all admitted to being involved in recent bomb attacks, and one of them said that he intended to blow up the United States Embassy," said Superintendent Nur Usman, a spokesman for the Jakarta police.
American Embassy officials said they were aware of the arrests but declined to comment on them. Last week, the State Department warned United States citizens that American companies and interests in Indonesia might be the target of such attacks.
In the last week, there have been numerous anti-American demonstrations across Indonesia, during which American flags were burned. The protests followed a visit by Defense Secretary William S. Cohen, who warned Indonesia that it could face economic sanctions if it does not disband violent militia groups that are accused of killing three United Nations aid workers on the divided island of Timor. The militias are linked to the Indonesian military.
The police spokesman said three of the people detained are believed to be the ringleaders, while the others either knew about the plot or played minor roles. Mr. Nur declined to provide any details on the suspects or a motive for the bombing campaign, which included attacks on the Indonesian attorney general's office, the Philippine ambassador's residence and the Malaysian Embassy.
Although the investigation is continuing, Mr. Usman said there was so far no indication that the suspects were connected with the military or the police. There had been widespread speculation that the blasts were the work of disaffected members of the armed forces loyal to former President Suharto.
The bombings have coincided with major developments in the government's corruption case against Mr. Suharto, the former longtime dictator, and law enforcement officials have said attacks were meant to derail President Abdurrahman Wahid's democratic reforms.
The arrests brought a sigh of relief to residents of this bustling capital city of 11 million people, who had grown apprehensive in recent weeks as the government appeared helpless to solve the bomb attacks. Last week, the president, frustrated that not a single arrest had been made in the case, replaced the national police chief and ordered the arrest of Mr. Suharto's son, whom police questioned, then released for lack of evidence.
With Mr. Suharto's trial scheduled to resume this week, Jakarta had been bracing for perhaps another round of bomb attacks. Mr. Suharto's lawyers contend that he is too sick after three strokes to attend his trial.
In a related development, the Indonesian government, facing mounting pressure from other nations to contain the violence on Timor, began an ambitious campaign this weekend to disarm the militia groups that are accused of killing the three United Nations aid workers in West Timor early this month.
The police in West Timor, which is controlled by Indonesia, said that by this evening the militias had surrendered 34 automatic rifles, 9 grenade launchers, about 1,000 homemade guns and an equal number of rounds of ammunition. The government has said it will use force to disarm the militias if they do not hand over all their weapons by Tuesday.
The militias, which are infamous for decapitating their prey, went on a rampage in East Timor last year after the territory voted to secede from Indonesian rule. The vicious gangs continue to cause havoc on both sides of the border.
"So far, it appears that the militias are being very cooperative in turning over their arms," said Simatupang, the police superintendent in the West Timor border town of Atambua, where the three aid workers were killed. Mr. Simatupang said in a telephone interview that since Saturday at least 18 militia groups had brought weapons to the Atambua police station and that he expected more would be turned in.
But almost no one expects the crackdown to bring an end to the violence. Authorities have conducted several campaigns to disarm the militias in the past, collecting hundreds of weapons. But the gangs, which are friendly with the military, continue to resurface with guns.
Moreover, the situation has become critical along the West Timor border, where 120,000 refugees from East Timor are trapped in refugee camps with food supplies running out.

HEADLINE: Back Home, President Evokes Pride and Pain

BYLINE: By RICK BRAGG 

DATELINE: LITTLE ROCK, Ark., Sept. 24

 BODY:
Jason Maynard loves his grandfather and wishes he were still alive. But sometimes he thinks it may be just as well that his grandfather, Marion Crank, died in 1994, before Bill Clinton's presidency had so many dents.
His grandfather, a 20-year veteran of the Arkansas Legislature, had a fondness for Mr. Clinton. 
 "Grandfather was around to see him elected president in 1992, but not the rest of it," said Mr. Maynard, a marketing manager for a publishing company who recently left Little Rock to work in Chicago. "In a way I'm glad he never saw what happened."
But his grandfather, who died at 79 and has a bridge named for him in Little River County, would have found good in Mr. Clinton's flawed presidency and would not have been surprised that it endured, Mr. Maynard said.
"Knock him down, and he keeps coming back," his grandfather said of Mr. Clinton at a 1981 Democratic fund-raiser, as Mr. Clinton was trying to rebuild his political career after losing re-election as governor in 1980.
When Mr. Crank died, Mr. Clinton called his widow to offer his sympathies -- from the Oval Office.
This past week, as the long-running Whitewater investigation concluded that there was insufficient evidence to bring charges against Mr. Clinton, so, too, evaporated the threat of one more blemish on his battered presidency.
Perhaps it is the final one, say some in his home state, as his presidency nears its end and Arkansans here and around the country form his legacy in bars, cafes, boardrooms, church pews and construction sites.
But there is more to it than the usual breakdown of likes or dislikes, divided by party lines. His strength was always said to be his personal touch, Arkansans say, so it is only natural that they take his life personally.
"He had tremendous talents and abilities, and they were misguided," said Edward M. Penick Sr., who was born in Little Rock in 1922 and has lived here all his life.
Like many here, Mr. Penick speaks of Mr. Clinton with a mix of sadness and weariness.
"I'll remember him as a person who could have done a lot for this country, but somewhere in his makeup he lost character and integrity, and that's tragic," said Mr. Penick, a retired banker.
"He's still trying to establish his legacy, and I don't think it will be what he thinks it will be. The power of the presidency fed his ego, and he thought no one could do anything to him."
He does not hate Mr. Clinton, he is only disappointed in him.
"In Arkansas, he's remembered as a great embarrassment," Mr. Penick said.
Others said they were tired of the ribald jokes and the talk show jabs that are finally beginning to subside now that Vice President Al Gore and Gov. George W. Bush of Texas are providing fresh material.
"It gave us a black eye, but not a lasting one," said Martin J. Borchert, an affluent retired contractor who was Little Rock's mayor from 1967 to 1968. "We'll get over it, if we haven't already."
Others said Mr. Clinton never did much for the state that was his springboard to the presidency, that he did not deliver even one interstate highway, a sore point here.
But as easy as it is to find someone who regrets this state's connection to Mr. Clinton, it is just as easy to find someone who would vote for him again -- for president, for governor, for anything.
In the dust of a construction site on the northwestern edge of downtown, Robert J. Keeling wiped the grime and sweat from his face and thought back on a quarter century of Bill Clinton-style politics.
The sex scandals did not turn Mr. Keeling, a carpenter with a master's degree in education, from Mr. Clinton.
"It didn't bother me much," Mr. Keeling, 45, said. "If you did a roll call in Congress on who'd had an affair, hands would go up all over the place" if the lawmakers were honest about it, he said.
Whitewater meant nothing to him.
The economy stayed strong, and there was plenty of construction work. On Friday, he and other workers labored to transform an old Oldsmobile dealership on the edge of downtown into a temporary warehouse for Mr. Clinton's papers. A permanent library is planned.
"He didn't start with much," said Mr. Keeling, with admiration.
"He wasn't born rich. Look where he got. He did us proud," he said, and then paused, thinking. "Proud enough."
Mr. Maynard said there was no pure right or pure wrong in the legacy.
"He survived Monica," Mr. Maynard said. "He survived Whitewater. And now Al is looking pretty good," he said, weighing Mr. Gore's chances of winning the presidency.
Mr. Maynard said he believed Mr. Clinton was not the pure political animal he had been called. "He does care about people," he said. That, he said, is not an act.
Elsewhere in the city, people say Mr. Clinton's legacy will be one of neither great pride nor great embarrassment.
"I think history will treat him well, when it's all said and done," said Joe D. Bell, the son of a state senator and a senior partner in the influential law firm Friday, Eldredge & Clark here.
"He may not be a superstar president, but he won't be remembered as a Herbert Hoover," Mr. Bell said.
Mr. Bell said that reaction from outsiders to his being from Mr. Clinton's state is neither always good nor always bad.
"I get a mixed reaction when I travel out of state," he said. "Some people ask if it's as bad as they say it is, but others say he's a sharp guy, and we're glad you gave him to us."
In a way, Mr. Bell said, Mr. Clinton's success -- and then his survival -- may have actually improved the way some Arkansans feel about themselves.
"Clinton's enduring strength is that he never quits," Mr. Bell said. "It's just another example of the true grit of Arkansans."
Mr. Clinton's legacy is formed in Mr. Maynard's mind.
Mr. Maynard says he will have regrets about Mr. Clinton, of unrealized potential, of a potentially great but flawed man.
But he says that when he thinks about Mr. Clinton, whom he has voted for as long as he has been old enough to do so, he thinks about the day his grandfather died and how the phone rang in his grandmother's house, and Mr. Clinton's words.
"I'm sitting here in the Oval Office, thinking about the good times Marion and I had," Mr. Maynard recalled Mr. Clinton saying.
Mr. Maynard said he would never regret giving that man his vote.
"He is an impressive person," he said. "Anybody who doubts that should just be introduced to him, and after two minutes of conversation, they would know that."

HEADLINE: Leery of the Web, Olympic Officials Set Limits on News

BYLINE: By FELICITY BARRINGER 

 BODY:
Christie Ambrosi, the hard-hitting outfielder for the United States Olympic women's softball team, has long had friendly dealings with her hometown newspaper, The Kansas City Star. So, when asked, she readily agreed to write an Olympic diary for The Star's World Wide Web site.
But shortly before she left for Sydney, Australia, in August, she sent an e-mail message to the newspaper, saying, "As far as the diary entry, we aren't allowed to do them during the Olympics." The International Olympic Committee was putting teeth into its often-ignored rule against publication of athletes' Olympic diaries during the Games. 
 Ms. Ambrosi was not alone. Siri Mullinix, the North Carolina-born goalkeeper of the women's soccer team, stopped her online chats with The Greensboro News & Record and said she could not contribute a diary during the Games. The Hartford Courant got the same message from Karen Scavotto, the 18-year-old archer from Enfield, Conn.
The 27th Olympic Games may be remembered for many things -- world records, peacetime logistical planning -- but it will surely be a benchmark in the struggle between those who hold intellectual property rights in sporting events and those seeking to cover sports, especially for the ever-expanding universe of news outlets on the Internet.
Concerned that the power of the Internet could eventually undermine the economic foundation of the modern Olympic movement, the Olympic committee is going to great lengths to control how and where the images and accounts of the Sydney extravaganza reach the public. The committee's actions echo what is happening in other areas of sports, as both amateur and professional teams and leagues aggressively invoke their property rights to control how and by whom events are covered.
Most of the committee's attention is focused on the Web. Here, streaming video -- still in its infancy -- may eventually attain both the technical quality and reach to cut into television audiences. This could, in turn, dilute the value of the most valuable asset of the Olympics: broadcasting rights. The Web could also spawn a horde of virtual storefronts where those with no rights to Olympic trademarks piggyback on the Olympic name to hawk their wares without returning anything to the nonprofit committee, which supports the athletes and the Games.
The prospects are worrisome enough to make Olympic officials take a hard line now. No athlete diaries or online chats. No streaming video of events -- even months-old replays of the Australian Olympic swimming trials, as the Australian site www.ninemsn.com.au discovered. No use of the Olympic rings to promote news organizations' own Olympic coverage.
Two months before the Games began, the committee filed a lawsuit in federal court in northern Virginia to dislodge 1,800 so-called cybersquatters, who had registered domain names using terms owned by the I.O.C. Names of the defendants took up 40 pages. The lawsuit is pending.
More than 20 private Web monitors on three continents are using the latest search engine technology to monitor possible violations of the I.O.C.'s property rights.
The strict enforcement of the diary rule hit local newspapers hard. Mike Fannin, the sports editor of The Kansas City Star, said, "I would assume that it is their ball and if you want to play, you play by their rules." But, he added: "The readers miss that connection with their local athlete. Christie Ambrosi's a softball player in the Kansas City suburbs. When she walks into a bar, people know who she is. The Olympics -- it's all about representing your hometown, your city, your country."
Franklin Servan-Schreiber, the director of communications and new media for the International Olympic Committee, is keenly aware of the tension between property rights and unfettered coverage of the Games. But, he said, "I don't think the I.O.C. can be seen in any way limiting speech about the Olympics." He pointed out that 20,000 journalists are in Sydney -- nearly twice as many media people as athletes, all free to cover the Games as they see fit.
It is also true that the most important pieces of news, the results, are released instantaneously and sent immediately by wire services to all publications, online and off. And any journalist can interview the athletes, he added. "It's the most written-about event every four years," he said. "We're very far removed from any notion of limiting speech."
The aggressive enforcement effort, he said, is meant to ensure that "the association between the athlete, the sport, the values and the symbol that represents all this is clear."
The Internet has offered little revenue to the Olympics so far. There are concerns that the Web could cut into television audiences and undermine the economic underpinnings of the Olympic movement. The committee took in about $1.3 billion for television rights to the Sydney games alone. NBC paid $705 million for United States rights.
And though great technological improvements are needed before the Internet can offer high-quality video to large audiences, the Web's reach still expands the power of once-small publications. The existing rule against diaries was never enforced against "small newspapers," Mr. Servan-Schreiber said. Now, on the Web, "nothing is small."
(At least one newspaper, however, has printed an athlete's diary without repercussions. The Newark Star-Ledger ran an account on Thursday from Joetta Clark Diggs, an 800-meter runner. An editor there said no sports officials had objected.)
Still, the new emphasis on restrictions like the diary ban is at least puzzling to some.
Floyd Abrams, a constitutional lawyer with Cahill, Gordon & Reindel, said it was odd that "the increased availability of a means of communication leads to a ruling seeking to assure that less is said." But he said the I.O.C. was not a United States agency and so was not bound by the First Amendment.
The gradual fencing off of information and imagery by sports franchises was well under way before the Olympics. In the last few years, debates have been joined over whether game developments can be distributed instantly by pager (they can, a federal appeals court has ruled).
Earlier this year, Nascar, the stock-car racing league, backed off an attempt to make reporters agree that Nascar was sole owner of "images, sounds and data arising from and during any Nascar event" -- including the racing times and scoring information.
"They were saying, 'If it happens during the event, we own the intellectual property rights to it,' " said Michael Persinger, the sports editor of The Charlotte Observer in North Carolina, the epicenter of the Nascar universe. "You can't own the news."
For the International Olympic Committee -- a nonprofit organization that gives more than 90 percent of its revenue to sports federations, local Olympic committees, organizing committees of host cities and athletes -- the stakes in controlling the Web loom increasingly large, particularly in four, six or eight years, when streaming video on the Web may be far superior to the current fuzzy, jumpy images.
All the issues raised by the Web will be discussed at an Olympic committee meeting in December.
Thus far in the Games, The Associated Press's sports editor said, all scores had been sent out to news organizations instantaneously. "It's been going fine," the editor, Terry Taylor, said.
Given the 15-hour to 18-hour time difference between Sydney and the United States, many dedicated fans are turning to the Web for results. Traffic on the sports sections of all major sites, like sports.yahoo.com, msnbc.com and cnnsi.com, surged in the last week.
Allen Weiner, NetRatings's vice president for analytic services, said he thinks the Web has not cut into the broadcast audience. "People have been using it to determine what they want to watch on TV," he said. "If you were a basketball fan and heard they had a close game against Lithuania, you might be interested in watching."
Still, Mr. Fannin of The Star said, "All these leagues are getting more proprietary about their rights and what's theirs and what's theirs exclusively -- especially when it pertains to the Internet." And when it comes to sports, "it's a seller's market."

HEADLINE: Fear Persists About the Price of Oil

BYLINE: By WINNIE HU 

DATELINE: WHITE PLAINS, Sept. 24

 BODY:
For months, Richard Nannarello has been girding his three-story Tudor here for a cold and costly winter. He invested in thermal drapes, removed the radiator covers for more warmth, and recently paid a $129 fee to get a long-term contract with an oil company that caps the price of his heating oil at $1.39 a gallon.
So it came as little relief to Mr. Nannarello when President Clinton ordered the release of 30 million barrels of oil from the Strategic Petroleum Reserve on Friday to help control heating oil prices. Indeed, more than anything, Mr. Nannarello saw it as a temporary measure to win voters for Vice President Al Gore in the November election. 
 "After the election, it may all be forgotten," said Mr. Nannarello, 44, a human resources assistant at the United Nations who spent $1,200 last year on heating bills.
Like Mr. Nannarello, many residents in the New York metropolitan area say their worries have not dissipated even though oil from the strategic reserve is expected to keep their heating bills in check, at least for now.
Energy experts and government officials, including Senator Charles E. Schumer, Democrat of New York, have warned that the price of heating oil could reach its highest level in years because of growing demand worldwide in a market of limited supplies. On Thursday, Mr. Gore joined them in calling for the release of oil from the strategic reserve.
The price of crude oil for November delivery fell by $1.32 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange after the president's announcement on Friday, closing at $32.68. Heating oil for delivery in October fell by 4.41 cents a gallon, to 95.48 cents.
In a telephone interview today, Senator Schumer praised the president's decision to release the oil and predicted that it would have a major impact. "I believe that the price of oil will come down considerably," he said. "My guess is it'll settle at below $30 a barrel." He added, "This is going to make a huge difference for New York families."
For weeks, anxious residents have been taking steps to insulate their homes -- and shield their wallets -- from cold weather and expensive oil. They have run through the stock of weather-stripping and insulation supplies at some hardware stores, rushed to fill their heating tanks before prices rose again, and signed up for contracts that guarantee a steady supply of oil at a fixed cost. Some homeowners are even switching to cheaper heating sources, like wood- and coal-burning stoves.
"Why wait for Jack Frost to come knocking?" said Richard Sanders, 65, a retired machinist from Port Chester, N.Y., who installed a wood stove in August and stocked up on five cords of split wood. "The summer was gone before it started, and now we have a long, cold winter ahead. It'll be a tough one."
Mr. Sanders said he would have bought the $1,500 stove even if he had known that the government would increase the supply of heating oil and thereby lower prices a month later. In the last five years, he said, his bills have doubled to $1,200 a year. "I certainly appreciate what they've done, but I don't know how much it will help," he said. "No matter what you do, it just seems like prices go up every year."
Minaj Patel, 43, who owns a dry-cleaning store in Englewood, N.J., said he had planned to raise his prices next month to offset the growing cost of heating oil, which is used to run his store's presses and machines. Now that may not be necessary. "It's supposed to help us, right?" he said of the release from the strategic oil reserve. "Whatever I can save this winter will be very good, because I'm not a rich man. If I can maintain the same kind of service for my customers without having to pay too much for oil and gas, that would be great."
But many residents and business owners have already decided not to take any chances on what could be a long, cold winter. At the Home Depot in New Rochelle, so many customers have requested caulking and weather-stripping that winter inventories were placed on the shelves in August, two months earlier than usual. The most popular items have since been reordered.
Paul Gencarelli, 28, a construction worker in New Rochelle, emerged from the Home Depot on Thursday afternoon with a 20-foot-long plastic sheet rolled up in his shopping cart. After spending $1,600 for heating oil last winter, he decided to insulate his home by wrapping plastic around the screened-in porch. "It's getting ridiculous," he said. "It's so expensive that I'm doing everything I can to tighten up the windows and everything else."
Other cost-conscious homeowners have been shopping around for the lowest heating oil prices. Since June, for instance, more than 500 people have joined a fuel buyers' program organized by the New York Public Interest Research Group, which negotiates discounts of at least 10 to 30 cents a gallon off retail prices for its 14,000 participants from Albany to New York City and Long Island. "I think consumers should be concerned, and this should be a wake-up call to the amount of energy we are using," said Cathleen Breen, director of the fuel buyers' group.
Hundreds of customers of the Almeida Oil Company, based in Scarsdale, have signed up for the company's program to cap oil prices between $1.39 and $1.59 per gallon (depending on the contract) until June 30, 2001. In past years, it offered a fixed price of 99.5 cents per gallon. "This was the first year I've not fixed the price," said Robert Almeida, the company's owner, who is also an economist, "because the market went up so much that I couldn't buy oil at reasonable enough prices for the customers."
Mr. Nannarello, for his part, said the $129 that he spent for the price cap would ease his mind through the winter. Early Thursday, an Almeida Oil worker arrived at his home to top off the 275-gallon heating tank in his basement. It was the first of 25 stops that day for the worker, Jimmy Pyrch. "Morning, sir," Mr. Pyrch said as he unfastened a hose from his truck and fit it around a pipe on the side of the house. "What do you think, we going to have a cold winter?"
As his customer nodded, Mr. Pyrch twisted a valve to release the flow of heating oil. "I hope so," he said with a broad smile. "For the boss's sake anyway."

HEADLINE: THE 2000 CAMPAIGN: THE VOTERS;
With Its Independent Streak, Wisconsin Remains a Tossup

SERIES: BATTLEGROUNDS: Wisconsin

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr. 

DATELINE: GREEN BAY, Wis., Sept. 22

 BODY:
After almost 14 years in office, more than any other governor in the state's history, Tommy G. Thompson bestrides Wisconsin politics like a colossus. He has built a national reputation as a reformer and innovator, and this summer he headed the committee that drafted the Republican platform in Philadelphia.
But he has never managed to carry his state for his party's presidential nominee. Not for Bob Dole four years ago, and not for George Bush in 1988 or 1992. He tried hard. On Halloween Day, 1992, he whistle-stopped across the state with Mr. Bush, from Mukwonago and Waukesha to Oshkosh and Chippewa Falls, a made-for-the-cameras trip on a train called the Spirit of America. 
 This time, the governor recently proclaimed, "I'm not worried."
If not, he is the only politician in Wisconsin who is not. The race between Gov. George W. Bush of Texas and Vice President Al Gore is breathlessly close here, in the estimation of political pros in both parties. Like several other Great Lakes states, this is one of the year's main presidential battlegrounds, although it is a bit smaller than most, with only 11 electoral votes.
Some recent private polls, like one taken for a big Wisconsin union, show an inconclusive edge for Mr. Gore; others show the race even.
This is a quirky state, politically speaking, hard to read, full of ticket-splitters and independents. It produced both the LaFollettes, the great clan of Progressive reformers, and the right-wing zealot Joseph R. McCarthy. Both of its senators, Herb Kohl and Russell D. Feingold, are Democrats, but both take offbeat positions on some issues and work closely with Republicans.
Ross Perot pulled 16 percent of the Wisconsin vote in 1992.
"We're 35 percent Republican and 35 percent Democratic and 35 percent independent," said Bill Dixon of Madison, a Democrat who managed Gary Hart's 1988 presidential campaign. "It doesn't add up, but then, neither do we."
Wisconsin is made to order, one might think, for Ralph Nader, the Green Party candidate, and he campaigned here on Wednesday, pounding away at "the concentration of power in the hands of a few." Mr. Nader sounded a bit like Fighting Bob LaFollette, and he drew sizable crowds -- more than 1,000 at a rally in Milwaukee and about 1,800 in Madison. In a poll taken in June, he had 16 percent of the vote in traditionally liberal Dane County (Madison), and some Gore backers have warned that he could hurt Mr. Gore in that area.
The vice president's daughter Karenna Gore Schiff, who campaigned here on Tuesday, and other surrogates argue that a Nader vote is a Bush vote.
"I don't think they have much to fear," said John Bibby, a retired political scientist at the University of Wisconsin-Milwaukee. "The closer we get to Election Day, the more people will worry about throwing their vote away. He'll do well in Madison, but not so well elsewhere. Obviously, if it turns out to be really close, even a few percentage points could be decisive."
Mr. Nader is handicapped, of course, by a shortage of money and a resultant inability to match the major parties' television spending.
Mr. Bush and Mr. Gore have been saturating the state with commercials for months. According to a continuing national study by Kenneth Goldstein, a professor of political science at the University of Wisconsin at Madison, they have spent $2.6 million in Green Bay and Milwaukee alone since June 1.
Those cities are two of the places where Mr. Gore has advertised most intensively; this summer, only Flint, Mich., Scranton, Pa., and Pittsburgh saw more Gore commercials than Green Bay and Milwaukee. This month, Green Bay has seen more Bush commercials than any other city except Scranton. This week, the Republicans were trumpeting the governor's new slogan, "Real plans for real people," and the Democrats were replying with the taunt, "George Bush's real plans hurt real people."
Nor have the candidates missed opportunities to campaign here. Mr. Bush spoke to the American Legion national convention in Milwaukee; both nominees spoke to the Veterans of Foreign Wars in August. Mr. Gore's first postconvention event was a riverboat trip down the Mississippi, complete with a Mark Twain impersonator, starting from La Crosse in southwest Wisconsin. Perhaps with an eye on possible defectors to Mr. Nader, Mr. Gore arranged for his erstwhile rival, former Senator Bill Bradley of New Jersey, to issue a belated endorsement here in Green Bay.
"Wisconsin," said Professor Goldstein, whose study is underwritten by the Pew Charitable Trusts, "is seeing action like it's never had before."
Jeff Mayers, a political reporter in Madison who runs a Web site that has been covering the campaign intensively (http://wispolitics.com), sees the election as a fight, in essence, between Governor Thompson's organization and the state's most powerful union, the Wisconsin Education Association Council, a teachers' group with 90,000 members.
The union's president, Terry Craney, was one of the few Gore delegates at the 1988 Democratic convention, and his organization has helped to set up the vice president's major Wisconsin appearances in the last eight years. In 1996, it turned out a crowd of 30,000 for a Gore rally, one of the biggest political gatherings in state history.
Governor Thompson, who attributed the elder George Bush's 1988 defeat in Wisconsin to "just mistake after mistake" by Mr. Bush and his strategists, has helped Governor Bush raise money in the state. Several politicians here said they thought that in the event of a Republican victory in November, Mr. Thompson hoped to be named United States ambassador to Vatican City.
Karl Rove, Mr. Bush's top national strategist, said in Wisconsin on Thursday that Mr. Bush would return to the state three or four times before Election Day. Mr. Thompson's support, he asserted, will not transfer directly to Mr. Bush but will ensure that those who like the governor and his policies "give us a better hearing."
At the moment, Governor Bush is "a couple points up, a couple points down" in the state, Mr. Rove said.
Darren Schmitz, Mr. Thompson's former press secretary, is running the Bush operation in Wisconsin. He said the organizational foundation laid down over the years by Mr. Thompson would inevitably benefit Mr. Bush.
"But more important," Mr. Schmitz said, "education is the crucial issue in this race. We are an education state, and Governor Thompson is identified with education. He has poured $3 billion into education during his time in office. He has supported the charter school movement and Milwaukee's school voucher program, which is the most comprehensive one in the whole country."
National Democrats and the teachers' union oppose the voucher program, of course, but it is backed by Mayor John O. Norquist of Milwaukee, a four-term Democrat, and it finally triumphed in 1999 when voters ousted antivoucher members of the school board.
Still, Teresa Vilmain, Mr. Gore's top Wisconsin operative, thinks the vice president can win on education and such other issues as health care, the economy and the environment. In this state, Ms. Vilmain said, "we're fighting on our turf." Even so, she added: "It's going to be very tight. We only won here by a few points four years ago. It's totally challenging for us, because we are up against a Republican governor who is totally engaged in this fight."
Mr. Schmitz also sees a struggle to the end. Asked how things stand now, he replied, "I've seen private polls that show it dead even."
Milwaukee remains a major manufacturing city, and Wisconsin has a dozen or more smaller cities with sizable employers, like Wausau and Oshkosh. It also has a significant farm population, which supports the state's noted dairy industry. But here as elsewhere, the suburbs seem to hold the political key this year. Not coincidentally, one of the main Bush offices is in Wauwatosa, west of Milwaukee, a town with a goodly number of Reagan Democrats. Ronald Reagan carried Wisconsin twice, people here recall, and Richard M. Nixon carried it three times -- in each case with a lot of help from Democrats.
Any number of things could tip the race one way or the other.
Low prices for farm products, for instance. Or high oil prices. This is one of the states that is paying runaway sums for gasoline, and that is a topic mentioned by many voters when asked what issues are on their minds. But so far, they do not seem to have decided whom to blame for the increasingly large share of their budgets taken up by heating their houses and operating their cars.
Mr. Gore made a pre-emptive strike this week by calling on President Clinton to release some of the nation's Strategic Petroleum Reserve, and Mr. Bush replied at once, accusing him of playing politics with a major element of national security.
Another imponderable is the possible crosscurrents that Senator Kohl's re-election race could generate. A popular figure, he is expected to win easily, but he is not much of a team player -- his slogan is "nobody's senator but yours" -- and many of Wisconsin's ticket-splitters might find a Bush-Kohl combination attractive. Or so some Gore senior strategists speculate.

This is the third article in a series about four swing states that are likely to be critical to the outcome of the presidential race. The last article will look at Michigan. Earlier articles looked at Pennsylvania and Ohio.

HEADLINE: Cost Hinders Effort To Reclaim a Castle;
After $7 Million, Newark Wearies Of Restoring a Looted Landmark

BYLINE: By ANDREW JACOBS 

DATELINE: NEWARK

 BODY:
Rising like a Bavarian castle on a knoll overlooking the skyscrapers downtown, the Krueger-Scott Mansion has long been one of this city's architectural jewels, a relic of the gilded age when Newark was a proud industrial powerhouse and High Street was its boulevard of nouveau-riche extravagance.
The 26-room house was built by Gottfried Krueger, a German immigrant who rose from poverty to become Newark's most flamboyant beer baron. Clad in salmon sandstone, its slate roof punctuated by six chimneys and a soaring turret, it was the largest residence in town when it was completed in 1888. It was also the first in New Jersey to have an elevator. 
 The house was later the home of Louise Scott Roundtree, a South Carolina-born former maid who had become a beauty-parlor magnate. Many believe she was the first black woman in the city to become a millionaire.
While $7 million worth of work has restored the exterior, the house is a ruin inside. Years of municipal neglect and repeated looting have robbed the house of its crystal chandeliers and pearl-inlaid wainscoting. After a decade of work -- and, some say, wasteful spending -- plans to turn the house into an African-American cultural center have been frozen by the Municipal Council, which refuses to allocate any more money for a project that was originally scheduled for completion in 1993.
"Looking at that place, there's no way anyone could say $7 million was spent on that building," said Donald Tucker, a Council member whose recent vote helped defeat a proposal that would have allowed the Newark Housing Authority to finish the job. "It's an abysmal failure, a total waste of taxpayer money."
The Council's refusal to spend more money puts it at odds with Mayor Sharpe James, who has vowed to finish the project, at a cost of $5 million more. "If I have to, I'm going to move into that building myself and force the city to provide me with heat, electricity and plumbing," he said in an interview. "We've got to keep the legacy of Louise Scott alive."
For a city struggling to rebuild its shattered ego, the Krueger-Scott project has become an outsized symbol of Newark's faded glories, bungled opportunities, and aspirations for a more glorious future. As the home of a self-made black businesswoman, the house is also freighted with issues of race and black pride.
Although Newark is enjoying a long-awaited revival, it remains one of the nation's poorest cities, one that many say can ill afford to spend millions renovating a Victorian estate. Although he wants to see the building rehabilitated, Mr. James no longer supports public financing for a historically accurate restoration. He simply wants the house brought into public use, even if that means a gut renovation or a deal with a private corporation that would get office and meeting space in return for its investment.
"We are not a cash cow," Mr. James said. "The citizens won't support open-ended spending."
In many ways, the fortunes and misfortunes of the Krueger-Scott Mansion mirror those of this battered city, which was one of the nation's wealthiest until the riots of 1967 and the ensuing depopulation drained Newark of its prosperity.
It takes some imagination to picture the days when High Street was lined with opulent estates, each one bigger and more outlandish than the last.
Now known as Martin Luther King Jr. Boulevard, the street has become a hodgepodge of weatherworn stately homes, empty lots and synagogues that have been recycled into churches. Next door to the Krueger-Scott Mansion is a 21-story apartment building, Hill Manor, that is awaiting demolition, part of the city's effort to replace its high-rise housing projects with town houses.
In building his fantasy home, Mr. Krueger is said to have been inspired by the baroque castles of his native Baden.
"People considered it the most garish thing they'd ever seen," said Charles F. Cummings, the city historian, who has written about High Street's genteel past. "But he loved it. He also loved to rub people's noses in the fact that he was a penniless German immigrant who came to own one of the biggest breweries in town."
Dressed in Louis XIV decor, the house was outfitted with rosewood floors, stained-glass windows, embossed leather walls and a five-story atrium capped with an elaborate copper-filigreed dome.
The Kruegers moved out at the end of World War I, and the house became the headquarters of a construction company. In 1925, it was bought by the Valley of Newark Scottish Rite, a Masonic organization that left its painted gold insignia on the dining room walls.
Mrs. Roundtree, who owned a hotel and a chain of beauty schools, bought the building in 1958 and converted it back into a residence. Over the years, she used the rambling house as a nursery school, a neighborhood cultural center and offices for the Scott College of Beauty Culture.
By the time Mrs. Roundtree died in 1982, her fortune had dwindled and the city, saying she owed back taxes, seized the house and evicted her family. Left unattended, the house was stripped of its furnishings, its massive organ and the hand-carved baroque columns that supported a staircase.
"It's been horribly cannibalized," said Douglas Eldridge, executive director of the Newark Preservation and Landmarks Committee, who toured the house before Ms. Scott's death. "If the city had secured it when it took it over, things wouldn't have gotten so bad."
By the late 1980's, a group of preservationists were rallying around the house. In 1990 they won a $625,800 grant from the New Jersey Historic Trust and a spot for the house on the National Register of Historic Places. The city of Newark matched that amount, and over the years devoted more than $4 million to turning the home into a center focusing on the black contribution to Newark's development. The federal government contributed an additional $1.5 million.
In 1993, Mayor James recruited a curator from the Schomburg Center for Research in Black Culture in Harlem, Catherine J. Lenix-Hooker, to raise money, resurrect the building and get the cultural program off the ground. Although her book parties, concerts and poetry readings were deemed a success, the restoration effort, critics say, fell victim to runaway overspending.
Work stopped for a year while Ms. Lenix-Hooker looked for a company to reproduce 23,000 thin "Lincoln Red" bricks. Specialized craftsmen were hired to replicate the terra-cotta tiles, the coachmen's portico, and the carved caryatids that hold up the massive front porch. All 140 windows were replaced, and structural work was done to prop up listing walls.
When Ms. Lenix-Hooker came back to City Hall two years ago looking for more money, the Council balked. The mayor, facing criticism and negative press, pulled her off the job. "I think the project was beyond her talents," he said.
But Ms. Lenix-Hooker has plenty of defenders, who say she was sacrificed to satisfy the Council's mounting discontent. Historic restoration, they point out, is a costly endeavor, something that many elected officials failed to appreciate.
"The work called for a high level of craftsmanship and high quality materials that will last for decades," said Harriette Hawkins, director of the New Jersey Historic Trust. "For a new building of that scale and bravado, you couldn't even put a price tag on it."
Ms. Lenix-Hooker, now in charge of marketing for Newark Symphony Hall, defends her work, saying the house was in worse shape than anyone had expected. "They don't understand that this was not about patching up an old building," she said. Once the press labeled the house a white elephant, she said, raising private money became impossible.
She added that her fund-raising efforts were eclipsed by those for the New Jersey Center for the Performing Arts, then under construction, which had dozens of staff members devoted to raising money. "I was the only one doing this, and in the end, I got terribly bashed," she said.
Since work stopped two years ago, the site has been ringed by a chain-link fence and guarded round the clock. Despite Council opposition, the housing authority is still considering its own rehabilitation plan, although it has yet to say what the building's final use would be.
Seeking a better assessment of the project's potential costs, the agency hired a team of engineers this month to survey the moldering interior and probe its underlying structure. Although hints of the building's former grandeur are still evident, including heavy oak doors and gilded rococo plasterwork, much of the house is a wreck. The debris-filled elevator slumps in its shaft, the fruitwood parquet floors have been damaged by years of rain, and the elaborate Craftsman-style banister has been mangled by looters.
As he inspected the decay recently, Peter Meyer, president of PPE, an engineering firm, shook his head with a mix of awe and despair. "It's definitely doable," he said. "But it's clearly a major, major job." The housing authority has said it is willing to spend upward of $5 million to make the building useful again.
Although the Krueger-Scott Mansion may get another chance at life, it seems increasingly doubtful that the home's interior will get the exacting restoration that preservationists and others have long expected. And some worry that in its haste to finish the job, the city will jettison its original purpose, to create a cultural center devoted to black history.
Among those eager to see the house brought back to life is Genevieve Tobin Scranton, one of Gottfried Krueger's granddaughters. Mrs. Scranton, 76, who lives in Vermont, would like to see the house turned into a museum of Newark's brewery past. "We all feel so helpless," she said. "When I think of what the house was, and what it has become, I feel sick."

HEADLINE: High-Speed Web Access Creeping Along

BYLINE: By JAYSON BLAIR 

 BODY:
Kevin Newman knew that his small Chelsea advertising company could not be competitive while relying on slow-speed computer modems. The company designs Web pages that often include streaming video and animated graphics. But its dial-up modems, which received data over regular telephone lines, were so slow that it often took minutes to get online access to some of the Web pages his employees had created.
So last year, Mr. Newman ordered four high-speed Internet digital subscriber lines, or D.S.L.'s, from Bell Atlantic for his ad agency, the William H. Brown Company. Sometimes, he wishes he had not. "There would be days I would come in when I had no phone lines and no Internet access at all," he said last week. 
 That happened after he waited nearly 10 weeks for installation after placing the order. Once the high-bandwidth lines were in place, he added, the service crashed often and there were software malfunctions, and it was often difficult to reach a Bell Atlantic technician.
Mr. Newman, who is the company's president, is not alone in his frustration with the D.S.L. service from Bell Atlantic, which recently merged with GTE to become to Verizon Communications.
In dozens of interviews, Silicon Alley executives listed things like Manhattan's high rents and onerous city taxes as the greatest long-term obstacles to the growth of high-technology businesses in the city. But when asked what was the greatest short-term threat to their ability to compete with top technology companies in other regions, the executives uniformly placed what they called Verizon's sluggish response to demand and its attempts to frustrate D.S.L. competitors at the top of their lists.
Andrew Brust, vice chairman of the New York Software Industry Association, a trade group, said, "The time it takes to get a data line and the effort it takes to keep it working is really important to New York and the growth of Silicon Alley."
Technology industries are driving the economies of several regions, from Silicon Valley in California to the Route 128 corridor outside Boston to Montgomery County, Md., where biotechnology firms have clustered.
Executives of Silicon Alley businesses believe that one of the main factors in recruiting and retaining good companies is being able to assure them that they and their employees can get high-speed Internet connections at home and at work. But it has not been easy guaranteeing that with Verizon, one of the largest local providers of high-speed Internet connections.
Jack Hidary, president of EarthWeb, one of the first Silicon Alley companies, said he considered Verizon's problems with its D.S.L. service to be an impediment to the industry's expansion in the metropolitan region. Mr. Hidary said it was "insane" that Time Warner Cable's high-speed Internet service, called Road Runner, is doing so well competing against Verizon D.S.L. Cable modems can be faster than D.S.L., but the connections are generally less secure and their speed can be less reliable, which is why small businesses often choose D.S.L.
"We are not seeing what we need when it comes to the whole process of wiring the city up for high-speed, secure access," Mr. Hidary added, referring to D.S.L. "Road Runner is simply taking over because you can get an appointment faster."
Cable modems use the same type of wire that brings cable television programming into a building. In general, cable downloading speeds average about 20 times as fast as a standard 56 kilobits-per-second telephone modem, while D.S.L. lines run about 11 times as fast as a 56k connection. But cable modems run on shared lines that can slow significantly if many people in the same area have the service.
Silicon Alley business executives said Verizon's missteps started around 1996, during D.S.L.'s infancy, when the company did not offer D.S.L. service in New York at the same time it was offering it in other parts of the country. At the time, one Verizon official compared it to testing a show on the road before taking it to Broadway. They say the show never really opened on Broadway because Verizon did not invest the money needed to upgrade its network and wiring or to train its sales and installation workers.
Verizon officials acknowledge that there have been problems with D.S.L., which they attribute to New York's complicated and aged wiring and the difficulties of being at the forefront of a new industry.
"This industry is only two years old" in New York City, said Larry Plumb, a spokesman for Verizon. "It is moving very fast, and you have a lot of demands, with a lot of people trying to work together."
But some industry leaders, including the New York Software Industry Association and the New York New Media Association, a trade group, have claimed that Verizon problems go beyond the complicated nature of the D.S.L. business. They complained that Verizon's decision to delay D.S.L. service in New York and the way it treated competing D.S.L. providers have slowed the growth of D.S.L. service in the city.
And they also charge that Verizon made it difficult for the other providers to flourish, because it ran its own D.S.L. service over telephone wires that were already installed, but forced competitors to put in their own lines, driving up those companies' costs and the costs to consumers. Verizon officials have denied trying to stifle competition.
Under pressure from the Federal Communications Commission and the New York State Public Service Commission, Verizon promised in May to open up its network of existing lines to D.S.L. competitors, a move that regulators say will lower costs and reduce installation delays.
Mr. Plumb, the Verizon spokesman, said D.S.L. service should improve. "It is difficult in New York because of the existing infrastructure," he said. "In other parts of the country, it is not quite as hard."
On that point, at least, Verizon and its critics are in agreement.
