HEADLINE: POVERTY TOUR REVISITED/American Voices;
Forget Washington. The Poor Cope Alone.

BYLINE: By EVELYN NIEVES 

DATELINE: CLARKSDALE, Miss.

 BODY:
In Eastern Kentucky, a 15-year-old boy dreams of quitting school to make real money working at a pizza parlor. Here in Clarksdale, a 68-year-old widow thanks God that she can manage the $200-a-month rent on a house one good rain could wash away. In East St. Louis, Ill., a 20-year-old mother of six wishes she could find a public housing project where the broken glass on the stoop is swept away.
Poor people learn to keep hopes small. So when President Clinton came calling on some of the poorest places in the country last year with ideas to make things better, it was like rain in the desert. 
 The president's six-state, four-day poverty tour, highlighting for the first time in his two terms the places that remain untouched by the longest economic boom in this nation's history, caused an excitement that bordered on the spiritual. He was like a charismatic preacher, stirring the congregation to believe.
A year later, the president's modest proposals for tax incentives to bring businesses to impoverished communities are still awaiting Senate approval. The idea that anything big might happen to places that have been down longer than they were ever up has long faded. And so has the notion, if it ever existed, that the federal government is the answer to the empty downtowns, idle laborers and the dilapidated buildings all too common in these communities.
In a presidential election season, when the two major-party candidates are suggesting that everyone should benefit from the nation's prosperity, the people who might be buoyed by the words have little use for national politics. In dozens of interviews in communities the president visited -- Clarksdale, East St. Louis and the Eastern Kentucky towns of Hazard and Annville -- what was clear was that Democrats are Democrats, Republicans are Republicans and people are more concerned with how to fix their own communities than in the state of the country.
Unlike the extensively reported tour of poor communities by Robert F. Kennedy in 1968, when he was a presidential candidate, Mr. Clinton's tour was a day's headlines. And perhaps because this is the age of welfare changes, the places Mr. Clinton visited expected little beyond the visit. East St. Louis, which looks leftover from a bombing, is bent on proving that it is not the urban war zone it became known as a decade ago. Appalachia, which is humming along, in parts, is concerned with telling the world that it is not a haven for barefoot hillbillies in shotgun shacks.
 
Clarksdale
Facing Adversity, Showing Resilience
Clarksdale, in the heart of the Mississippi Delta, is determined to prove that with grit, it will not only endure, but prevail.
It is, as the mayor says, "resilient in the face of adversity."
The morning of the day the president came to town, Shirley Fair opened Ooo So Pretty flowers and gifts at the regular time, 9 a.m. Ms. Fair nodded to the Secret Service agents on the roof, said hey to the ones who had been hanging around the store for 10 days and managed to take a few orders for bouquets.
Her store, on the corner of a nearly abandoned strip of downtown Issaquena Avenue, had been chosen for a meeting between the president and local small-business owners. Word was that he was bringing ways to lure businesses that had fled to Highway 61 back to town, besides enticing new ones.
But Ms. Fair, barely getting by after 21 years in business, would bet on just two things that day. It would be hotter than heck, and Mr. Clinton's visit would make great pictures.
She was right. When President Clinton strolled through the tired, broken streets of downtown, sweating enough for two men in the 105-degree heat, it was the most exciting thing to happen to Clarksdale (population 22,000) in a long time. Maybe ever. He passed the boarded Roxy Theater, the closed-down Pac-Man lounge and the falling-down wooden houses as if he were right at home. Then he came into Ooo So Pretty and listened to people sing the business blues for about an hour, a lot longer than his schedulers had planned.
And then, he left.
"It was a great Kodak moment," Ms. Fair said.
As she expected, nothing has really changed on Issaquena Avenue. Like so many downtowns abandoned for highway strips, the Roxy (the mayor pointed it out to the president as a place that might make a great cultural center with some kind of grant or loan), is still boarded. The Pac-Man lounge is still closed. The falling down houses look ready to give up completely.
Johnny Newson is still putting in the long hours at Newson's Auto Parts, in a blocklong building, inherited from his dad, across the street from Ooo So Pretty. Mr. Newson opens at 7 and stays until 7 or 8 to compete with the Wal-Mart and Auto Zone on Highway 61. "Right now," he said, "it's just like the president never came."
A lot of people say the same thing. Hardly anyone blames the president, though. In Clarksdale, people pay as little attention to Washington politics as Washington politics pays to them. No one faults any of the presidential candidates for not coming around; they are used to being ignored.
They tend to blame Wal-Mart for their problems, for stealing business from downtown. Or Memphis, 75 miles away, for stealing brain power. Or the local politicians, for letting downtown slip into a coma without a fight.
They blame themselves, too.
"People got to want to improve," said Ethel Mae Clark, sitting on her mother's porch on downtown Yazoo Street. Next door was a boarded shack of a house. Across the street, where houses used to be, was a lot overrun with weeds.
Ms. Clark, who lives nearby, has diabetes and a failing kidney, and her world is framed by the $400 Social Security check she receives each month. Her mother, Martha Riley, who was sitting on the porch sewing a quilt under a sign for "Pigee's Beauty Salon" -- a remnant of her landlord's defunct business -- is in similar straits. Ms. Riley pays $200 a month rent for the house and wishes she could afford better. She blames not being able to read or write for her lot. "Here," she said, "Things just get worser."
Still, Clarksdale is a proud old place. Some of the biggest names in the blues walked these streets. Muddy Waters and John Lee Hooker grew up in town. Bessie Smith died here.
The Delta Blues Museum, which is fighting for financing from the Clarksdale Board of Commissioners, attracts visitors from all over the world. Tony Czech, the director, showed off the guest book to prove it.
"Look at these," Mr. Czech said, leafing through a few pages that revealed addresses from Paris, the Netherlands, Sweden, Germany and Alaska. "And these are just over a couple of days."
But after these visitors tour the museum, they have nowhere else in Clarksdale to go.
For the really poor people -- and that means nearly 40 percent of Clarksdale residents, who live below the poverty level -- shopping means taking a long walk along a busy road. Local bus service is nonexistent.
Richard M. Webster Jr., the mayor, had hoped the president's visit would lead to a real change. But Mr. Webster is not surprised that it did not. "We Deltans have learned to be resilient," he said. "Resilient in the face of adversity. Just the fact that we are able to live and work here is a sign of that."
Yet there are occasional cries of desperation. Dave Williams, a part-time carpenter, started shouting, "Factories! We need some factories here!" when he saw a reporter and photographer walking around town.
He has lived here all his life, 42 years, but cannot see sticking it out much longer. "I would like to have a full-time job," Mr. Williams said. "So would a lot of people. I know a friend of mine, he's living in one of those cattle boxes that sit on the tracks. So are a lot of people. See for yourself."
On a scorching day, there was no one in the metal cars. Hardly anyone walked the streets, either, except for a twig-skinny woman who asked for $5 for a fix, and a man gathering cans in a plastic grocery bag.
 
East St. Louis
Self-Help Is Seen As the Solution
It was Sunday, the sun shining like a halo, and the streets of East St. Louis were virtually empty, as though the city had been warned of an air raid.
This made the abandoned buildings all over town stand out in bold relief. From boarded churches to closed-down gas stations and burned-out houses and storefronts, the rotting buildings, more than 1,500 in all, mocked the city's pride, marring every neighborhood.
Businesses, even on State Street, the main drag, are few and far between. The city's only places to eat are the kind that measure customers by the millions. So many businesses have left that when the president stopped by on his poverty tour, the best example of a positive economic growth that the city could come up with was a new Walgreen's.
East St. Louis, with about 32,000 residents, 99 percent of them African-American, is clearly one very poor city. About 40 percent of the residents live below the federal poverty line of $16,600 for a family of four. A three-bedroom house can be bought for $10,000. The unemployment rate is more than 10 percent, twice the national average.
President Clinton, with an entourage that included the Rev. Jesse Jackson and several cabinet members, promised that East St. Louis would not be forgotten. But while his brief visit has yielded little concrete change, residents overwhelmingly vote Democratic, all the time. Few people have anything but praise for the president's gestures toward their city.
"The visit shed a lot of light on the things that we have to offer," said Greg Lewis, one of a dozen members of the local Democratic club who was spending his Sunday afternoon renovating a building the club recently bought for its headquarters. "A lot of it," Mr. Lewis added, to a chorus of yeahs, "depends on us."
Like other members of the committee, Mr. Lewis, who is 38, the father of two boys, 12 and 15, and a relocation supervisor in nearby St. Louis, could not say enough good things about his hometown.
"My wife and I have a six-figure income between us," he said. "We live in this city because we believe in it. The stigma is something we're trying to get away from."
Told that the city looks grim, Mr. Lewis and other residents talk about how much it has improved from 10 years ago. The police cars did not have working radios and the fire engines would not run. Trash was piled high in the streets because the city had no money to hire haulers. In 1990, a man who was physically abused while in jail sued East St. Louis and won, forcing the city to turn over the deed to City Hall to him to satisfy the court judgment. And when things seemed as if they could not get any worse, they did. In 1991, the city posted 68 homicides, a record.
Now, with tax revenue from the Casino Queen riverboat, which opened here in 1993, just across the Mississippi River from St. Louis's Gateway Arch, and the new $15 million waterfront hotel adjacent to it, the city can afford at least basic services. Still, many of the casino's 1,200 employees, even those who come from East St. Louis, live elsewhere, where the schools are better and there are neighborhoods where houses appreciate in value.
For the very poor in East St. Louis, prone to not voting or participating in civic life, the city still looks very bleak. Andrea Mitchell, a 20-year-old mother of six, moved to East St. Louis from Washington Park, Ill., less than two years ago. With local schools too overcrowded to accept her 5-year-old and 4-year-old, Ms. Mitchell said she was about ready to move again. She lives in the Normandy Houses, a barrackslike public housing complex, and despite help from neighbors, she cannot manage her young family in her two-bedroom apartment and police her neighborhood as well.
"All these empty lots and empty houses are terrible," Ms. Mitchell said. "The streets need to be fixed. There really ain't no businesses here. People should all pitch in and clean this place up."
Since the president's visit, a Blockbuster video store and Save-A-Lot grocery store that had already been in the works have opened by the Walgreen's, creating a small but bona fide shopping center in the heart of town. They represent the first major retail development in the city since the 1950's.
But with so much abandonment, and such seemingly intractable poverty, longtime residents and business owners say it will take more than a few new chain stores to lift the city off its knees.
Fredericka Nash and her two sisters run the Nash Funeral Home, which their parents opened more than 70 years ago. Ms. Nash, 80, remembers when East St. Louis was "a bustling city."
"To look around, you certainly wouldn't see it," Ms. Nash said. "Sometimes, it looks like a battle zone. The people want better. But how are you going to achieve that?"
Like others, she brushed off the possibility of a federal solution. "With the proper city administration," she said sternly, "possibly it may be able to stem the tide."
The mayor, Debra Powell, did not keep a scheduled appointment to talk about her city, and was busy the next day as well. Her assistant said Ms. Powell was too busy planning to meet the actors Harry Belafonte and Danny Glover, who were considering investing in the city.
 
Appalachia
Anger at Being Part Of Poverty Tour
Of all the jobs in Jackson County, Ky., those on the assembly line at the Mid-South Electronics Company in Annville are among the most coveted.
Mid-South, a contract manufacturer for several multinational companies, has nearly 900 employees, up from 40 workers 10 years ago. And the nonunion shop keeps hiring more workers, while few leave. Employees say the pay, which starts at $5.75 an hour, is pretty good, especially considering medical benefits.
Kevin Dunn, who has worked at Mid-South for 7 years, after 11 years at a textile plant in London, a town 22 miles away, considers himself lucky. "I'd say it's one of the better jobs around," said Mr. Dunn, 50, the single father of two boys.
It was at Mid-South that President Clinton stopped on his poverty tour last year of the Appalachian region of Eastern Kentucky to prove his point that companies can park themselves anywhere and do well.
Folks at Mid-South say it was a big thrill to have the president walk through the plant, even though many workers are Republicans through and through.
"I voted for him, but I'd better not say that too loud," said Mr. Dunn, giggling, as he sat on a picnic table outside the plant on his lunch break.
Della Dell, a quality control supervisor who has been at Mid-South for 16 years, said she was excited to meet the president, even though she is a Republican.
"He stopped in here because he cared about jobs," Ms. Dell said, "even if we were doing fine already."
There is a lot of that kind of talk in Eastern Kentucky. People are weary of the image of Appalachian hillbillies in miners' hats and rope belts. Time and again they said it would be wrong to take pictures or interview the rural poor without including the rest of the people. Some were insulted that the president's visit was part of a "poverty tour."
As poor as the region is -- nearly 35 percent of the people in Jackson County, which includes Annville, live below the poverty line, as do 30 percent of those in Perry County, where the president addressed a rally in Hazard -- the emphasis is on telling the world that encouraging private investment is the way to go.
This is God's country. From Annville to Hazard, people grow patches of tobacco in the backyard and post the Ten Commandments on their lawns. Signs on lampposts read: "God is coming soon. Are you ready?"
People time and again said they were blessed to live in a place as beautiful as Kentucky, where the mountains are green and lush and the trees look as old as time.
William Gorman, the mayor of Hazard, invited the president to visit his town months earlier, largely to show how well Appalachia is doing.
"I'm a Republican, and I really think he did us a great honor," said Mr. Gorman, who traces his family's roots in the region to the Revolutionary War. "The greatest problem I've seen with people is the lack of hope. You give them hope and they'll conquer the world."
Mr. Clinton talked about his "new markets initiative," to entice businesses to invest here, but for Mr. Gorman, who has been mayor for 23 years, Hazard (population 5,416) is already benefiting from the new economy.
"Appalachia has a lot of problems," he said, taking the time on a Saturday with his assistant and friend of 50 years, Charlie Hammonds, to give reporters a tour of the town. All traffic pointed to the Wal-Mart, and the grand opening of a new off-track betting center, the U-Bet.
"But the quality of life here is no different than it is anywhere in the country," Mr. Hammonds piped in. "We're just the same as most places."
Mr. Hammonds was worried that the president spent a little too much time in places where the old stereotype of Appalachia persists -- on rural roads, out where families have little education or work.
But the fact remains that very poor people still live along the back roads of Appalachia, in houses that look like abandoned barns. Some of them even live right on the roads for all the world to see.
Take Jesse McWhorter and his family, who live off a county road in Burning Springs (population about 500), up the road from Annville.
Jesse, 15, lives with his mother and two sisters, 11 and 8, in a house with so many dogs and puppies and cats and kittens that no one can say exactly how many they own.
He owns cocks, too, beautiful bantam roosters that he has spared from death despite his belief, once he was born again last year, that raising cocks is the Devil's business.
"I kept two cocks because they're so beautiful," Jesse said. "The other ones I just took and went like this," he said, twisting his hands to show how he snapped their necks.
When the school van dropped him off recently, his mother, Linda, was on the porch with his sister Chrissie, home from school because she did not feel like going. An old friend, Martha Couch, was on the porch, too.
Ms. McWhorter and Ms. Couch vaguely remembered the president's visit, though they said they did not vote or pay much attention to politics.
Ms. Couch, who recently learned from a social worker that she was 75 years old ("I thought I was 72") said there were not many people like herself and the McWhorters left, families who have lived in the area for generations, mining or growing tobacco, minding no one's business but their own.
But Jesse, chewing tobacco and spitting it, awkwardly, said he was going to live a different life.
"I can't wait to start making some money," he said. "I can drive a truck. Or I can make pizzas."
He said he was not even thinking of college. "I can't afford it," he said, dismissing the notion of scholarships or loans. "I can afford to find my own way, though. I can definitely afford that."

HEADLINE: Debate on Need for New Power Plants Ignores Conservation

BYLINE: By KIRK JOHNSON 

 BODY:
New Yorkers heard a lot this summer about why their electricity bills were going up and why a new generation of power plants was needed, and the message centered on one word: demand.
Millions of new air-conditioners, computers and other gadgets are humming, blinking and draining electricity from the power pool, utility executives and state energy officials said over and over again. The 40 percent jump in electricity rates that many in the region were assessed in August was only a hint of what could lie ahead, those officials suggested, if residents did not accept more power plants in their neighborhoods. 
 What the public was not told was that the people framing the argument for building power plants have an abiding interest in selling more electricity. Nor was the public told that the means already existed to dampen demand and reduce the amount of new power needed, if the political will could be exerted.
Before New York's energy markets were deregulated, beginning in 1998, the state required companies like Consolidated Edison to reduce demand in the system -- through energy efficiency measures, for example -- if that choice was cheaper than building a new plant.
Under deregulation, that requirement has been lost. In an open market, every participant wants to sell more energy, and the government agencies in New York and other deregulated states that once compelled companies to think of alternatives have become silent or powerless.
"The reliability problems and price spikes in the last two years have led to a simplistic set of solutions: build more power plants," said Richard Cowart, director of the Regulatory Assistance Project, a nonprofit energy policy institute in Montpelier, Vt., that works with state regulators and utilities.
On the new playing field, Mr. Cowart and other experts said, the incentives are all one-sided.
Power generating companies have no motive to encourage people to use less energy or to use it more efficiently because the most money is to be made when demand is at its peak. And electricity delivery companies make more money by sending more electricity through their wires. "The incentive to sell more electricity is overwhelming," Mr. Cowart said.
Certainly, demand for electricity is rising in New York and across the country, and most experts agree that some added generating capacity is needed. And New York State's top electricity official, Maureen O. Helmer, chairwoman of the Public Service Commission, has emphasized in her recent speeches that conservation and efficiency measures must be part of the solution to rising demand.
The problem, environmental groups and energy analysts say, is that in an arena in which government has taken a step back through deregulation, it is harder for regulators to make those opinions count.
Given the tone of the debate in New York and the sharp jump in prices this summer, it would be easy for the public to think that the region's energy situation is worse than it actually is. Most long-term projections for the region say that demand will increase less in New York State and New York City than in the nation as a whole during the next 10 to 15 years. New York, it turns out, is not an energy hog.
The most recent official long-term projection for New York, called the State Energy Plan, issued in late 1998, said the state could get by with only a very modest expansion in generating capacity for the next 10 years, or no expansion at all if the levels of electricity that utilities are required to hold in reserve could be modified.
The rate of growth in demand is also slowing down, not accelerating. Within the territory served by Consolidated Edison, which includes all of New York City and its northern suburbs, peak demand, the highest single moment of energy use on the heaviest day of the year, rose by 1.7 percent a year from the mid-1990's to the end of the decade. In the new century, that growth rate is expected to be 1.2 percent a year.
"It will be more modest," one senior Con Ed official said.
The main implication of those projections, economists and energy analysts say, is that most of the increased capacity proposed for New York City may not be needed at all, at least to meet new demand. In the Con Ed service area, meeting a 1.2 percent growth in demand through new supply alone would require an increased capacity of 500 to 750 megawatts over the next five years. Developers and power plant builders have proposed a total increase six to eight times greater than that in the next three years alone.
"No one plant can make the argument that it's really needed," said Ashok Gupta, an energy economist at the Natural Resources Defense Council, a private conservation group. "Like malls, power plants are now being built for market share."
Predicting energy demand is notoriously difficult. In the early 1970's, for example, the accepted wisdom was that energy use would continue to grow at the rapid pace that it had in the 1960's. Academics and utility industry officials said the country might need 1,000 nuclear plants by the turn of the century.
But the oil shocks of the mid-1970's and early 1980's forced the introduction of energy-efficiency measures that made those forecasts worthless. Energy use as a percentage of the nation's economy, called energy intensity, began declining and has done so ever since.
Some energy officials have said the problem of rising prices and the overwhelming focus on supply is the result of a still-new electricity market that is not yet functioning as intended.
Theorists who had envisioned competition as the door to a golden age of consumer choice had predicted that companies specializing in demand reduction -- they sell ways to cut energy use through technology or better management -- would compete with companies selling supply. But that has not happened yet. Part of Con Ed's prediction of more modest growth in demand in coming years is based on the expectation that demand-management companies will emerge within the next few years, company officials said.
But state regulatory officials also made the political decision in the late 1990's to reduce spending for conservation and efficiency measures. The state, which once led the region in financing for alternatives to new supply -- an effort that included television commercials urging people to think about saving energy -- now spends about a third as much per capita as most states in the Northeast, including Connecticut and New Jersey, according to figures from the New York State Energy Research and Development Authority.
Last month, a report filed with the State Public Service Commission recommended an increase in financing for the efficiency program. The report was produced by representatives of the electricity industry, working alongside consumer and environmental groups. But the state's utilities, including New York State Electric and Gas, Niagara Mohawk and Con Ed, refused to support the final report because they were unhappy with its conclusions.
"Deregulation undermined the role of utilities in promoting energy efficiency," said Edward A. Smeloff, the executive director of the Pace Law School Energy Project, a research and advocacy group that helped write the report. As electricity companies have become more conscious of the bottom line under deregulation, Mr. Smeloff said, demand-reduction programs have become a threat to profits.
For utilities that buy most of their power from other companies, moving more electricity through the wires has become the primary way to increase profits, Mr. Smeloff said. "That's the business," he added.
Utility officials say that much of their demand problems come down to one hour each year, that moment of peak demand when consumers are using the most electricity. To a great degree, planning revolves around the need to have adequate supply for that crucial moment so as to avoid blackouts. But experts like Mr. Smeloff say the tools already exist to dampen those peaks, and reduce prices to consumers as well, if only a constituency existed to push for the changes.
Many business and residential energy uses are routine and could be postponed for a few hours to dampen load on peak days, a plan that efficiency experts say would be particularly easy in New York City commercial buildings where energy use is centrally controlled and monitored. In England, one electricity provider created lower rates for consumers who ran appliances like washing machines and dishwashers in off-peak hours. The program became so popular that power use in the middle of the night went up significantly during the discount-pricing window.
Environmentalists also worry that increasing power capacity beyond the real demand in New York could create a kind of self-fueling spiral. If too many new plants are built, they say, prices will probably fall, encouraging people to use more electricity, while at the same time reducing incentives to save energy or use more efficient products.
In New York and elsewhere in the country, much of the growth in demand is being driven by the spread of air-conditioning, especially from people buying extra units to cool more parts of their homes. Last year, Con Ed began a kind of visual history project in which employees began regularly photographing 90 apartment buildings in New York City and counting the number of window air-conditioners. In one year, the total went up by 5 percent.
Some energy experts say more air-conditioning could further increase the spurts in demand that could in turn lead to more power plants, which many scientists say are prime sources of the greenhouse gases blamed for global warming.
"We have hotter summers because of greenhouse gases and more greenhouse gases because of more air-conditioning," said Joseph Romm, the executive director of the Center for Energy and Climate Solutions, a nonprofit consulting institute based in Virginia. "It would certainly be ironic if people decided that the solution was more power plants."

HEADLINE: THE 2000 CAMPAIGN: THE VICE PRESIDENT;
Bush Would Use Medicare For Tax Cuts, Gore Says

BYLINE: By KEVIN SACK 

DATELINE: CORAL GABLES, Fla., Sept. 25

 BODY:
Campaigning in Florida, where as many as 40 percent of this November's voters may be 60 or older, Vice President Al Gore promised today to strengthen sanctions against health maintenance organizations that pull out of the Medicare market and to eliminate Medicare copayments and deductibles for a variety of preventive tests.
Mr. Gore unveiled those proposals in a speech in St. Petersburg that opened a weeklong discussion of Medicare policy, an issue that is particularly potent in Florida. Before flying here to attend several fund-raisers, Mr. Gore used his speech to convey his stark differences on the issue with Gov. George W. Bush of Texas, a comparison that illustrates the broader philosophical differences between the two campaigns. Along those lines, Mr. Gore also drew attention to a five-year-old interview in which Mr. Bush expressed support for the deep Medicare cuts championed by former House Speaker Newt Gingrich. 
 Mr. Gore's aides provided reporters with a videotape of the interview on Oct. 19, 1995, by a television station in Austin, Tex., in which Mr. Bush commended that year's House Republican plan to cut $270 billion over seven years from Medicare, the federal health insurance program for the elderly. The proposal died after a prolonged battle with Congressional Democrats and the White House that prompted two government shutdowns.
"Elderly people will not suffer as a result of this plan," Mr. Bush said in the interview with KXAN. "It's going to make the plan solvent. And Republicans will be heralded, not only for saving Medicare, but at the same time for having the political courage to balance the budget."
Mr. Gore's aides then made him available for an interview with The New York Times, in which he said that Mr. Bush's comments were "very revealing of his basic attitude toward Medicare" and belied his assertion that he was a new kind of Republican.
"He doesn't like it," Mr. Gore said. "He disagrees with its basic premise and he is looking for any politically viable opportunity to gut it."
Mr. Gore then said Mr. Bush would do "virtually the same thing to Medicare that Gingrich proposed: raising premiums, taking money out of Medicare for a tax cut that goes mainly to the wealthy, forcing seniors into H.M.O.'s, privatizing Medicare."
Dan Bartlett, a Bush spokesman, said Mr. Bush had made it clear with his own 10-year plan to restructure Medicare, at a cost of $198 billion, that he was committed to the program.
Mr. Bush's proposal, issued earlier this month, would allow private insurers to compete with the government to provide a variety of coverage options, some of which could include prescription drug benefits.
"His proposals will strengthen Medicare so it will be just as successful in the future as it has been in the past for millions of seniors," Mr. Bartlett said.
As for Mr. Bush's comments in 1995, Mr. Bartlett said, "What is important here is not what Governor Bush said about a plan five years ago that wasn't even his. What's important is what he says about his plan today."
Today's trip was Mr. Gore's fourth to Florida since the Democratic convention last month, and his ninth since early March. But despite that sign that Mr. Gore is competing aggressively for the state's 25 electoral votes, his campaign admits that it is being badly outspent here on television advertising.
Karl Koch, a senior consultant to the campaign in Florida, said that the Bush campaign and the Republican National Committee had spent an estimated $2.2 million on advertising from Sept. 11-19, compared with only $330,000 by the Gore campaign. The Democratic National Committee, which had been advertising in three Florida media markets, is currently off the air but plans to return, Mr. Koch said.
A senior campaign official said today that either Mr. Gore or his running mate, Senator Joseph I. Lieberman, would travel to Florida each week between now and Election Day. The official also said the campaign would increase its advertising and ground troops in the state.
The Gore campaign considers Medicare a major issue partly because it plays well in key states like Florida that have large elderly populations. But Mr. Gore's strategists also know that with 39 million beneficiaries nationwide, a number that is projected to more than double by 2035, Medicare is a significant concern for a large cross-section of voters. Indeed, the vice president also used the Medicare issue to batter his opponent in the Democratic primaries, former Senator Bill Bradley.
In his speech in St. Petersburg, delivered to a crowd of several thousand people, most of them elderly, Mr. Gore vowed that "the heart of my agenda is a rock-solid commitment to Medicare."
Reading from a teleprompter, Mr. Gore explained the key elements of his Medicare proposals: to protect the projected $360 billion, 10-year Medicare surplus by placing it in a secure fund; to add a voluntary prescription drug benefit at a cost of $338 billion; and to invest $40 billion in reimbursement for nursing homes, teaching hospitals, rural hospitals, home health agencies and rehabilitative services.
Those were among the proposals that were repackaged in a 74-page booklet on Medicare that was released by Mr. Gore today, three weeks after he issued a 191-page synopsis of his economic plan.
But the booklet also outlined Mr. Gore's new proposals for an array of sanctions against H.M.O.'s that pull out of the Medicare market, a growing problem. Mr. Gore would prevent H.M.O.'s that drop coverage of Medicare recipients from returning to that market for four years, double the existing sanction of two years.
He also would require H.M.O.'s to sign up Medicare beneficiaries for two years instead of the current one year. And the vice president would ban H.M.O.'s from the Medicare market if they showed a persistent pattern of cherry-picking the least expensive recipients by offering coverage in relatively healthy communities while not offering it in less healthy ones.
"We will no longer just accept the rising wave of H.M.O.'s dropping seniors and denying them coverage, all to enhance their bottom line," Mr. Gore said.
Another new proposal would eliminate existing Medicare copayments and deductibles for preventive services like hepatitis B vaccinations, colorectal and prostate cancer screenings, mammographies, pelvic exams and bone mass measurements. Gore aides said those changes would cost $8 billion over 10 years.
"No seniors should be priced out of tests that could save their lives," Mr. Gore said.

INTERNATIONAL A4-12
 
Opposition Candidate Claims to Defeat Milosevic
Vojislav Kostunica, the opposition candidate in the Yugoslav presidential election, claimed victory over President Slobodan Milosevic in the elections Sunday. Mr. Kostunica's supporters said their tally showed him well on his way to a first-round victory. But Mr. Milosevic's ruling coalition said the president was ahead, though the government suspended vote counting; the legal deadline for announcing results is Thursday, and the opposition is concerned about Mr. Milosevic's willingness to acknowledge defeat. A1
 
A Verdict on Bosnia
A federal jury in Manhattan ordered the Bosnian Serb wartime leader Radovan Karadzic to pay $4.5 billion to victims of rape, torture and genocide in the Bosnian war. Even though Dr. Karadzic is at large and the award may never be collected, the jury foreman said he and other members of the jury felt the need to express their outrage over the abuses. A10 

Barak Is Host to Arafat
Prime Minister Ehud Barak of Israel and Yasir Arafat, the Palestinian leader, met for the first time since the Camp David peace talks ended two months ago. Officials on both sides do not expect that the deadlock in the peace negotiations will be broken, but see the meeting, in Mr. Barak's home, as progress in itself. A12
 
U.S. Defends Action in Peru
American officials defended their role in pressuring Panama to give refuge to Valdimiro Montesinos, Peru's ousted intelligence chief, asserting that his departure was necessary to avoid a military coup. Human rights groups criticized the United States for seeking to arrange a soft landing for Mr. Montesinos outside of Peru, where it could be more difficult to prosecute him for abuses. A10
 
Talks on Korean Border Tension
The South Korean defense minister, Cho Seong Tae, right, and Kim Il Chol, minister of the People's Army of North Korea, met in South Korea to discuss lowering tensions along their heavily armed border. A10
 
New Player in French Scandal
Dominique Strauss-Kahn, a former Socialist cabinet official in France, acknowledged that he was given a copy of a videotape that accuses President Jacques Chirac of illegal activities, but that he did not notify the authorities during the two years he had it. Mr. Chirac, who denies the accusations made on the tape, called for an investigation into how Mr. Strauss-Kahn had obtained it. A8

NATIONAL A14-21
 
Senate Approves Measure To Restore Everglades
The Senate voted overwhelmingly to approve a $7.8 billion plan to restore the Everglades and its ecosystem, a project supporters say is the most ambitious environmental restoration effort ever. The plan, drafted by the Army Corps of Engineers, will revamp South Florida's water supply by capturing and storing more rainwater, then redirecting its flow into the Everglades. The House has not set a vote for the bill. A1
 
Removal of Barricades Urged
Mayor Anthony A. Williams of the District of Columbia, backed by Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan and Eleanor Holmes Norton, the city's nonvoting Congressional delegate, urged the reopening of Pennsylvania Avenue in front of the White House. It was closed after the Oklahoma City bombing in 1995. The Secret Service is cool to the proposal. A14
 
Determining Drunken Driving
A dispute in Congress over setting a national standard for what constitutes drunken driving is delaying a transportation spending bill. Advocates of a tougher standard want all states to adopt a blood alcohol level of 0.08, which a 170-pound man would expect to have after drinking five beers in two hours; 30 states currently use 0.10 as the level, which allows that man to drink six beers. A1

Congress is close to passing legislation that the Justice Department says will legalize cruise ships' dumping of some toxic chemicals. But the measure's Senate sponsor, Frank H. HEADLINE: ON BASEBALL;
The White Sox Are Showing That Sometimes Less Can Be More

BYLINE: By Murray Chass 

 BODY:
No greater irony could impose itself on the playoffs than the presence of the Chicago White Sox. No. 26 in the Major League Baseball payroll standing for most of the season, the White Sox have the majors' best won-lost record and are the newly crowned champions of the American League Central.
That the White Sox could go so far on so little is a tribute to every facet of their organization, beginning with Jerry Reinsdorf, the chairman. But that's where irony rears its delicious head. 
 As the players association's Enemy No. 1, as an owner who would fight the players to the catastrophic end over control of players salaries, Reinsdorf has produced a team that undermines every argument the owners make about salaries and payroll disparity. At a time when officials and owners have identified payroll disparity as the game's most pressing problem, a team that pays its players one-third the amount the Yankees pay theirs has already clinched its spot at the postseason party, while the Yankees still plod on to secure their place.
Not only that, but the Cleveland Indians, with a payroll twice that of the White Sox, have been bumped from their perennial place in the A.L. Central and may not even earn an invitation to the party.
But then, in terms of economic impact, this has been a very different season from last. Last season the playoffs were populated by teams with 8 of the 11 highest payrolls. The trend was painfully clear, officials and owners lamented, and would never be reversed without some drastic change in the system.
A big payroll did not guarantee postseason play, they said in a frequent refrain, but a team could not reach postseason play without a big payroll. But this is a new year. Times have changed quickly. This season only four or five of the top 14 teams in payroll will play games after next Sunday. They are the Yankees, the Mets, Atlanta, St. Louis and maybe the Indians.
The Los Angeles Dodgers and the Baltimore Orioles once again lead the big spenders who have gained the least for their money. One wonders if they'll ever get it right. The Arizona Diamondbacks, who won a division championship last season, added about $10 million to their payroll this season, but may as well spend this October in the swimming pool beyond the right-center-field fence at their ballpark. Those three teams began the season among the top five in payroll.
Seattle, on the other hand, was 15th and San Francisco 17th. Close to the bottom were the White Sox and just ahead of them the Oakland Athletics. Three or all four of those teams will play postseason games.
In the interest of having Reinsdorf, the most militant of owners, explain how his poorly paid White Sox could outplay far wealthier teams, a telephone call was placed to his office in Chicago yesterday and then a request for a telephone interview was placed with the team's public relations department. The subject of the interview was provided.
"No, thanks," came the reply from Reinsdorf via a club spokesman. Maybe he's saving his secrets for a book. But is this any way to persuade the players something has to be done to adjust payrolls?
Billy Beane, the Athletics' general manager, was not as reticent about addressing the subject.
"Certainly we'd like to make that an antiquated argument," he said, referring to the payroll-disparity issue. "But the realism of it all is I think this is a pretty unique situation in Oakland. It's a testament to the talent of the young players who are here, but the system hasn't allowed any guarantee that we can retain our best players in the prime of their careers. These young players are not going go be young and inexpensive for long."
Beane's last comment was one of the points Commissioner Bud Selig made. "I won't deny there's a potential for an aberration once in a while," Selig said. "But the fact is the cost of keeping the White Sox up there will be so high. When the Reds almost made it last year, Jim Bowden said don't think this aberration makes the system right," he said referring to the Cincinnati general manager.
The White Sox and the Athletics aside, Selig said, disparity remains a problem.
"I think you definitely have overwhelming evidence of a correlation between high payroll and winning," he said. "See me next year. I'm confident of that. Remember, you still have the same teams winning, the Yankees, Atlanta, the Mets. Let's see what happens in the playoffs. If there's an aberration, it's a quick short aberration."
He recalled what Drayton McLane Jr., the Houston owner, said of the Astros' three division titles: "Someone had to win the National League Central, but we got banged out in the first round each time."
Not surprisingly, the people in the union office offered a different view of this season's developments.
"I think at a certain point you have to have some sympathy for the owners," Gene Orza, the union's No. 2 man, said, then proceeded to cite examples of dire projections some owners have presented during labor negotiations in the last 15 years.
"In 1985 it was Oakland going out of business and they turned around and won three division championships in a row," Orza said. "Then they picked Pittsburgh, and Pittsburgh won a few division championships. Then it was San Diego, but San Diego was in the World Series. It's been a string of bad luck for them, almost Kafkaesque bad luck."
If Reinsdorf shows up at any of the negotiating sessions next year after talks for a new collective bargaining agreement begin, the dialogue between him and Orza would probably be worth the price of a playoff ticket.

HEADLINE: THE 2000 CAMPAIGN: THE TEXAS GOVERNOR;
Bush Expands on Education Theme, Saying a Reading Crisis Endangers the Economy

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO 

DATELINE: SPOKANE, Wash., Sept. 25

 BODY:
Sounding his new slogan intended to raise anxieties about the nation's well-being, Gov. George W. Bush accused the Clinton administration today of helping create an "education recession" in which minority and low-income students were routinely allowed to fail.
Seeking to draw a clear link between the nation's future economic health and the quality of its schools, Mr. Bush asserted that the nation faced a reading crisis that had contributed to crime and caused American students to fall behind their peers in other industrialized nations. 
 "This is a leading indicator of troubles to come," Mr. Bush told 500 supporters early today at an elementary school in Beaverton, Ore., 10 miles west of Portland. "Children who never master reading can never master learning. As uneducated adults, they face a life of struggle on the fringes of our society. Some will turn to crime and end up in prison."
Mr. Bush, the Republican nominee for president, outlined his proposals to improve grade school reading programs, expand scholarship programs for math and science students and encourage annual testing of students in grades three through eight. He accused his Democratic opponent, Vice President Al Gore, of promising change but instead protecting a failed status quo.
"They call for new spending without real reform or accountability," the Texas governor said. "It's like pumping gas into a flooded engine."
Kym Spell, a spokeswoman for the Gore campaign, countered that Mr. Bush would not be able to afford his education proposals because he would need the entire federal surplus to pay for his military programs and tax cuts. Mr. Bush denies that.
Mr. Bush's school event kicked off a three-day swing on the West Coast that today took him through Washington and Oregon, states where Democrats have carried recent presidential elections but where polls show Mr. Bush running neck-and-neck with Mr. Gore.
In both states, Ralph Nader, the Green Party candidate, has recently drawn enthusiastic crowds of more than 10,000 people and is considered a threat to draw disaffected Democrats away from Mr. Gore.
Later today, Mr. Bush told several hundred supporters at an airport rally here that he would oppose breaching hydroelectric dams on the Snake River, as some environmentalists had proposed, to improve salmon habitats. He also attacked Mr. Gore for having said he wanted to study the issue further.
"He's worried about some kind of group's not liking him," Mr. Bush said. "He wants to study it as way of not taking a position."
Mr. Bush also plans to spend two days in California, where polls show Mr. Gore holding a comfortable lead. Part of Mr. Bush's goal is to tap into the state's wealth: he plans to raise more than $1.4 million for the state Republican Party at Silicon Valley and Los Angeles fund-raisers.
But Mr. Bush's communications director, Karen P. Hughes, asserted that the governor also wanted to show that he planned to contest California until Election Day.
"He wouldn't be going there, at a critical point in the campaign, if we didn't think we could compete in California," Ms. Hughes said.
Coming about a week before what will probably be among the most important moments of the fall campaign -- a nationally televised debate with Mr. Gore -- today's event in Beaverton also gave Mr. Bush an opportunity to fine-tune his message on a signature issue before a friendly audience.
Mr. Bush did not hold a news conference but instead fielded gently worded questions from the largely partisan audience. With each response, Mr. Bush expounded on his plans to cut taxes, increase military spending and improve education.
While Mr. Bush has sought to lay blame for the nation's education problems squarely on the administration's doorstep, many educators contend that the federal government plays a relatively minor role in primary and secondary education. The most important decisions -- on curriculum, standards, testing, personnel and construction -- are made by state and local officials.
Mr. Bush himself acknowledged the limitations of federal action today, noting that Washington provides only 7 percent of public education spending.
"It's not as if the federal government is going to solve 100 percent of the problems," he said. "The best education is that where it's focused at the state and local government."
The focus of Mr. Bush's education plan is to ensure that all children read proficiently by the fourth grade. He would provide aid to help primary schools expand reading programs and reward schools that administered standardized tests annually to help educators identify learning problems early. For those and other proposals, Mr. Bush has called for increasing federal education spending by $47 billion over 10 years.
Schools that failed to reduce the gap in test scores between low-income students and their more affluent peers in three years would lose some of their federal Title I funds, which are earmarked for low-income children. That money, $1,500 a student, would be given directly to parents of children in failing schools, who could use it for other educational purposes, like hiring tutors or paying private-school tuition.
Mr. Gore has called for increasing federal education spending by $115 billion over 10 years to help districts build new schools, provide preschool for most students, hire more teachers and raise teacher salaries.
Like Mr. Bush, he has proposed withholding federal aid from states where test scores do not improve. But unlike his opponent, he would redirect that money to the local schools with the greatest problems, rather than give it to parents.
Toward the end of the education event in Beaverton, Mr. Bush was asked what could be done to help students reconnect with politics, and he recommended that candidates not make decisions based on polls or focus groups.
But the Bush campaign is not completely averse to such tools; the phrase "education recession" was carefully poll-tested before Mr. Bush first deployed it today.
While Mr. Bush was pitching his education ideas, his running mate, Dick Cheney, continued his attacks on Mr. Gore, accusing him of having a conflict of interest involving stock and oil prices.
Mr. Cheney told reporters in Springfield, Ohio, that Mr. Gore supported extending a moratorium on royalties paid by United States companies drilling for natural gas in the Gulf of Mexico. That position, Mr. Cheney said, would benefit Occidental Petroleum -- a company whose stock is held in a trust belonging to Mr. Gore's mother.
"Gore continues to be the prospective beneficiary of holdings of stock in Occidental Petroleum in the family trust," Mr. Cheney said, "and I think that that does constitute a conflict of interest."
Mr. Cheney said the vice president should either recuse himself from participating in decisions about energy policy or ask his mother to divest the family trust of oil stocks.
Ms. Spell, the spokeswoman for Mr. Gore, said that the vice president neither owned nor controlled any stock in Occidental and that there was no way of knowing what stocks would be in the family trust when he inherited them.

HEADLINE: Metro Business;
Schumer Urges Steps To Aid Technology

BYLINE: By Jayson Blair 

 BODY:
Senator Charles E. Schumer told members of one of Silicon Alley's largest trade groups yesterday that if the city is to remain competitive in the technology industry it must create more options for less-expensive commercial space, force competition into the high-speed Internet field and train more skilled workers.
Senator Schumer, Democrat of New York, told about 1,000 people gathered in a ballroom at the Brooklyn Marriott for the annual New York Software Summit that a decade ago the city was reeling and that it was "surely at the bottom of any company's list as a prospective business site" until Silicon Alley began to revive the economy of Lower Manhattan in the mid-1990's. The event was sponsored by the New York Software Industry Association, a trade group. 
 There are now about 138,000 technology jobs in New York City, compared with about 27,000 in 1993, according to a New York New Media Association study conducted by PricewaterhouseCoopers and released in March.
Mr. Schumer said the hiring by Silicon Alley companies "exceeds the number employed in banking, construction, legal services and the apparel business" and has helped diversify the city's economy.
He said that the city had taken a step in the right direction by offering incentives to technology companies interested in moving outside Lower Manhattan and into places like Harlem, the Brooklyn Navy Yard and Long Island City, Queens. But without guarantees that the companies will be able to receive high-speed Internet access in those locations, there is no way New York will be able to compete, he added.
"If we don't, we will suffocate the new-media industry or drive them into the arms of New Jersey industrial park developers," Mr. Schumer said. Jayson Blair

HEADLINE: Reckonings;
A Drop in the Barrel?

BYLINE: By PAUL KRUGMAN 

 BODY:
The decision to release part of our Strategic Petroleum Reserve has been widely criticized. Even many commentators with no ax to grind seem convinced that there is something irresponsible about the move.
But they're wrong. We should be tapping our oil reserves; in fact, the big mistake was not using them months ago. 
 Put it this way: Why has the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries, derided as irrelevant only two years ago, suddenly become so effective again? The answer is that now, as in the oil crises of 1973-4 and 1979-80, circumstances have given OPEC what amounts to a temporary corner on the world oil market. Our long-run policy should be to encourage production and discourage consumption, so this doesn't happen again. But in the meantime we should try to prevent OPEC from taking full advantage of that corner. Releasing oil reserves to set a cap on prices -- and making it clear that we are prepared to release more -- will do exactly that.
Successful attempts to corner markets are rare, but they happen. A Japanese company managed to corner the entire world copper market in the mid-1990's (though it lost it all by overplaying its hand). The standard procedure is to surreptitiously buy up a large part of the supply of your chosen commodity, then pull some of that supply off the market, causing prices to soar for the rest. In effect, the market manipulator creates a temporary monopoly position for himself -- the market corner -- and exploits that temporary monopoly by selling some but not all of his stockpile at very high prices.
OPEC did not follow the classic procedure, but events have produced much the same result. Very low oil prices a few years ago discouraged independent producers; oil exploration fell off sharply. Then demand for oil surged as Asia recovered from its financial crisis and Americans bought ever more S.U.V.'s. The result is that for the time being, even with non-OPEC production at maximum, a few major exporting nations know that they have enormous market power. By producing a few hundred thousand barrels a day less than they could, they can drive prices on the oil they do produce to levels not seen in many years.
This situation won't last indefinitely. As long as we don't do something foolish like encourage consumption by cutting taxes on gasoline, new supplies of oil, together with falling demand in response to high prices, will eventually eliminate that market power. Until then the oil exporters have us, yes, over a barrel, and are exploiting their temporary advantage with gusto.
But if withholding a few hundred thousand barrels a day from the market can drive prices sky-high, putting a similar amount back in can bring them back down to earth -- as demonstrated by the sharp drop in oil prices that followed the announcement of plans to tap U.S. strategic reserves. And Western governments have more than a billion barrels in reserve. Why not use those reserves to break the market corner, or at least to limit its effectiveness?
Some warn that if we supply more oil, OPEC will supply less. Indeed, yesterday Libya's oil minister made that threat explicit. But the logic of the situation suggests that this threat isn't credible. Oil producers know that they are getting higher prices for their oil now than they will in a year or two; the only reason they are not pumping as much as they can is that they believe that holding back will keep prices high. But if they know that attempts to drive up prices by restricting production will be offset by increased releases from Western reserves, they will have less, not more, reason to keep oil off the market. A credible promise (threat?) to use our petroleum reserves to prevent prices from going too high might well actually persuade OPEC to produce more than it otherwise would.
Remember that we're not talking about fundamental market forces here. This market is already being manipulated by a handful of exporting-nation governments -- so why shouldn't the importing-nation governments also enter the game? We have a lot of influence over this market, if we choose to use it. And it would be not just a shame, but positively shameful, if we allow ourselves to be deterred from acting in our own interest because we're afraid to annoy the oil cartel.

HEADLINE: BOOKS OF THE TIMES;
Finding the Seeds of Hope In a Society of Paradoxes

BYLINE: By IRA LAPIDUS 

 BODY:

PERSIAN MIRRORS
The Elusive Face of Iran
By Elaine Sciolino
Illustrated. 401 pages. The Free Press. $26.
For many Americans, Iran conjures up the hostage crisis, terrorism, the crowds chanting "death to America" and the fierce Ayatollah Ruhollah Khomeini denouncing the United States and Israel. 
 Elaine Sciolino shows a different Iran. More than 20 years of visits, interviews, encounters and analyses have given Ms. Sciolino, a senior correspondent in the Washington bureau of The New York Times, her deep and wide-ranging insights. Her perceptive book "Persian Mirrors: The Elusive Face of Iran" conveys the diversity of Iranians and the subtleties, dilemmas and contradictions of their society today.
Iran is not easy to know well, but Ms. Sciolino knows it intimately. Its people are warm and welcoming, but do not reveal themselves readily. Conversation, she reports, is full of politeness, self-abnegation, hypocrisy and lying, all to avoid offense and loss of face. What happens can be accepted, but talking about it is taboo if it strips away dignity and honor. Ms. Sciolino succeeds because she has unraveled a difficult code of cultural expectations.
She is particularly sensitive to Iranian women. "There is an unspoken bond among us that transcends culture, history, nationality and language," she writes. She shows both their helplessness and their power. In public life Iran's women must not be visible. Their heads and bodies must be covered. They may be beaten for violations of these rules.
In private and family matters, Islamic law puts them at a disadvantage. Although it is easy for a man to obtain a divorce, a woman can get one only in extreme circumstances, and the husband is given custody of all but the youngest children. Husbands can also take second wives or arrange "temporary marriages," which are religiously acceptable.
Nonetheless, women fill almost all the roles of a modern society. More than half of Iranian university students are women. Women work, drive, own property, have access to birth control and vote. Moreover, the book notes, women "are experts in finding ways around the constraints of the male-dominated system." Ms. Sciolino found everything from gender-segregated parties to beauty salons to a woman who ran a gambling business in her apartment, where women in low-cut dresses drank and danced to heavy-metal music.
This activity is very dangerous. There is always the possibility of an unexpected intrusion by the morals police, perhaps just to extort a bribe, perhaps to arrest the participants. The insecurity is deeply resented.
Women also show extraordinary courage in fighting the system. Azam Taleghani, the publisher of the weekly newspaper Payam-e Hajar, is committed to Islam and the revolution but challenges clerical ideas of male supremacy and promotes a more feminist interpretation of the Koran. Another activist, Faezeh Hashemi, published a newspaper until it was banned and now promotes sports programs to combat depression among women and to encourage them to fight for their rights.
Minorities do not fare well at all, but are nonetheless loyal to the Iran of their ideals. Zoroastrians and Christians are barely tolerated. Jews are accepted in business, medicine, engineering and law, but anti-Semitism is widespread, and the government maintains incessant anti-Israeli propaganda. Bahais are persecuted relentlessly. Their marriages are not recognized by the state. Their property has been confiscated. They have been expelled from the universities and many have been executed.
But even under this pressure, the religious minorities are loyal. Jews -- Persian is their native language -- feel profoundly, truly Iranian. And a Bahai engineer says: "I am Iranian. I love this country."
Courage and love are essential because the authorities are powerful and oppressive. The clerical establishment, headed by the supreme leader, now Ayatollah Ali Khamenei, controls the judiciary and large sectors of the economy. A vast apparatus of military, police, intelligence officers, morals enforcers and organized vigilantes is used to crush drugs, gambling, homosexuality, prostitution, rape, murder, spying, counter-revolutionary activities and "sowing corruption on earth." Above all, it punishes women for being improperly dressed in public. Recently Iranian journalists and students have been beaten and killed.
Still, the authorities have not been able to check the demand for a transformation of the Islamic Republic into an Islamic democracy. There is a vigorous though embattled press. Guardedly, young people, women and intellectuals, including many liberal clerics, struggle for the future.
Recent elections gave 70 percent of the seats in parliament to liberals, but they are still paralyzed by the powers of the conservatives, and it is not yet clear who will win. Although the struggle is sometimes framed as a conflict between Persian and Islamic identities, religion and popular culture, clerical rule and freedom, dictatorship and democracy, in this supple and sophisticated country even liberals accept the Islamic state. Looking beneath the surface, Ms. Sciolino makes us aware of deeper currents flowing toward political compromise and synthesis.
Iranians, she points out, have a love-hate relationship with the United States. In politics it is the Satan that opposed Iran in the war with Iraq, shot down a civilian airliner, orchestrated an embargo and sides with Israel. Yet America, avidly consumed on television, audio and videocassettes and computer software, is the country of Iranian dreams. It embodies their fantasies of a good life. American relations with this fascinating nation hold unanticipated possibilities.
Through the eyes of Ms. Sciolino, we see a culture of paradoxes: a nation that is open and welcoming but remains hidden and mysterious; a clerical dictatorship but one of the Middle East's liveliest democracies; a puritanical regime but a people who love everyday life; a severe orthodoxy but an expressive cinema and an argumentative press; a state that makes control of women its first concern but whose women are powerful as personalities and even subversive; a revolution that has rejected secularism but a nation heading toward a fusion of Islamic and Persian identities.
First and foremost, Ms. Sciolino shows Iranians as human beings trying to cope with an unusual and very difficult situation. For this wise perspective the reader is grateful.

HEADLINE: The New Kings of Soft Money

BYLINE: By William Kristol and Jeffrey Bell; William Kristol is publisher of The Weekly Standard. Jeffrey Bell, a principal in Capital City Partners, a public policy consulting firm, was an aide to the presidential campaign of Gary Bauer.

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
Republicans and conservatives around the country desperately want Rick Lazio to beat Hillary Rodham Clinton in the New York Senate race. Yet the Lazio campaign is forcing them to rethink two of their most cherished dogmas: Republican faith in soft money, the unrestricted donations to political parties and outside groups that are used to help campaigns, and conservative aversion to campaign finance reform.
Hillary Rodham Clinton's reluctant acceptance Saturday of a soft-money ban was a victory for Mr. Lazio. The New York Senate 2000 Committee, an arm of the Democratic senatorial campaign organization that has been advertising on behalf of Mrs. Clinton, was spending more than $1 million a week of soft money on TV "issue ads," with a lot more to come. Mr. Lazio has raised more hard money (that is, individual contributions directly to the campaign, which are limited to $1,000 a person) than Mrs. Clinton. If the agreement to ban soft money more or less holds, as pressure from the media may ensure, Mr. Lazio should have an advertising advantage in the last month of the campaign. 
 So much for Republicans' belief that soft money is their friend. Republicans, of course, as a pro-business party, have raised their share of soft money. But the Democrats have now caught up and, in fact, are moving ahead. Soft-money raising, focused on economic interests and unconstrained by any legal limit on contributions, is particularly suited to the Democratic Party of the Bill Clinton era -- a party that is at once pro-business and pro-government and that is willing to approach corporate interests with the prospect that government policies can help or harm their industries. That's why the committees that aid Democratic candidates for Congress are in their best position in decades relative to their Republican counterparts -- despite the Republicans' majorities in Congress.
Most competitive Republican candidates, on the other hand, have an advantage in $1,000 donations and an even bigger advantage in smaller contributions. Isn't it in Republicans' interest to revive the hard money system by increasing individual limits and curbing the soft money system? Rick Lazio thinks so. And others are coming around to this view.
Why did Mrs. Clinton agree to a limit that could hurt her campaign? Because she thought that not agreeing would harm her even more. She was probably right.
Conventional wisdom in Washington, especially conservative conventional wisdom, is that campaign finance reform doesn't matter as an issue. Conservatives resolutely refuse to learn any lesson from John McCain's thumping of George W. Bush in New Hampshire, when he made campaign reform his central theme -- or from Mr. McCain's subsequent defeat after he veered from that message. But campaign reform turns out to have surprising political salience. Conservatives should welcome this fact, since they have no stake in the current system.
The great conservative triumphs of our time came, after all, before the rise of soft money: Ronald Reagan's near-victory, fueled by small-dollar, hard-money donations, in his primary challenge of 1976; the conservative gains in the Congressional elections of 1978 and 1980; and Mr. Reagan's winning presidential campaign of 1980. The full flowering of the soft money system in 1996 has coincided with a period of conservative political and policy retreat.
Is it too much to ask our fellow Republicans and conservatives to rethink their self-defeating defense of the current corrupt campaign finance system, and shape campaign reform legislation that would be good for them and for the country?

HEADLINE: WINE TALK;
A Dream of Zinfandel, Delivered to the Door

BYLINE: By Frank J. Prial 

 BODY:
EVERY year around this time, the mail brings a raft of notices from California's small exclusive wineries offering their latest vintage. The wines are listed with prices, and an address to which I should send my check.
There's only one problem: they won't sell me any wine. There are 29 states in which it is illegal to ship wine from another state directly to a consumer. I live in one of them, New Jersey, and work in another, New York. 
 These laws are strongly supported by the liquor and wine wholesalers and by most retailers. They are terrified that the Internet, through which most of these direct sales are made, is going to put them out of business. The states support these laws for fear that direct shipping will elude their taxes.
I might not be so exercised over all this if, with the mails blocked to me, I could slip around to my local wine merchant and buy the wine there.
But I can't.
In the last 40 years, the number of liquor wholesalers has shrunk by a staggering 97 percent. And while their numbers have dwindled, the number of wineries has exploded. There are more than 800 in California alone, producing some 4,000 different wines. The wholesalers are more interested in volume than variety, and that leaves most people without access to some of best small producers in the country.
As a result, hundreds of small wineries, desperate for an outlet, have turned to the Internet and mail order, but they find themselves largely stymied there as well.
Understandably, the frustrated winemakers and their customers have begun to fight back. And they are finding a sympathetic ear in some courts. In the past year, wineries and consumers have filed lawsuits in six states asserting that to single out wine for special controls is discriminatory, a restraint of trade and a violation of the interstate commerce clause, which grants Congress the exclusive authority to regulate commerce among states.
In two of the suits, in Indiana and Texas, the plaintiffs won. On Sept. 13, the Seventh Circuit Court of Appeals overturned the Indiana decision, a ruling that a spokeswoman for the Wine and Spirits Wholesalers of America, Juanita Duggan, called "a major step forward for the state officials who are trying to prevent out-of-state alcohol shippers from evading taxes and selling wine, liquor and beer without checking ID's."
John DeLuca, the president of the Wine Institute, a trade group that deals with legislation and consumer issues affecting the wine industry, said that the decision seemed to be based mostly on courtroom technicalities. A consumer group, the Informal Coalition to Preserve Consumer Access to Wine, noted that with laws being challenged in New York, Virginia, Texas, Florida and North Carolina, "the Seventh Circuit opinion will certainly not be the last word on this issue."
A week earlier, the United States District Court in the Southern District of New York denied a motion by the wholesalers' lawyers to dismiss the New York suit. New York liquor laws make it illegal to ship or advertise wine in the state without a license. Out-of-state wineries are ineligible for licenses. In allowing the suit challenging the New York laws to go to trial, Judge Richard Berman cited United States Supreme Court cases that declared states' laws invalid if their purpose was "mere economic protectionism," which of course is the fundamental purpose of the laws being challenged.
The wholesalers hotly deny this, claiming they are equally concerned about the effects of the under-age drinking that direct shipping would surely encourage. "The Constitution prevailed over reckless disregard for temperance, tax collection and local custom," Ms. Duggan said.
The United States Senate is expected to consider a new direct shipping bill, probably in the remaining weeks before the end of the current session. Sponsored by Senator Orrin Hatch of Utah and called the 21st Amendment Enforcement Act, the bill would, in essence, allow the states to get federal help in prosecuting out-of-state wineries that ship wine directly to a wine drinker.
By itself, the bill probably wouldn't get anywhere. Tacked on to one appropriations bill or another, it will probably pass, but direct shipping advocates say it will have minimal effect on the growing number of suits in the federal courts aimed at overthrowing the states' shipping laws.
The whole fracas will almost certainly end up in the Supreme Court, not because it's important that some dot-com plutocrat gets his Pahlmeyer Chardonnay from Jason Pahlmeyer himself, but because it affects the future of e-commerce and what many consider outmoded interpretations of the 21st Amendment.
Back in 1933, the idea behind the 21st was to keep gangsters out of the liquor business. The thugs never did get out; they just went legit, as Damon Runyon would say, and with the people who had been locking them up on their side, they prospered.
Now, for the first time, the wholesalers face a serious threat. And not just from a passel of wine geeks. The rise of the Internet has created a new dynamic in the business world, one that a group of outmoded monopolists, with a few prohibitionists along for old times' sake, cannot long withstand. Besides, once the wineries agreed to pay state taxes -- which they have -- it was all over but the shouting.
It may take a while, but one day not too far off, clicking on zinfandel and waiting for the Federal Express delivery will be an essential part of the wine experience.

HEADLINE: Consumer Confidence Index Rose In September Over Job Security

BYLINE: By Bloomberg News 

 BODY:
Consumer confidence rose in September as optimism about job security bolstered the outlook for the rest of the year, the Conference Board said yesterday.
The board's index of consumer confidence increased to 141.9 this month from 140.8 in August. The September reading was close to the record 144.7 set in January and matched in May. 
 "Nothing in this latest survey suggests the economy will run out of steam soon," said Lynn Franco, an economist with the Conference Board, a private research group based in New York.
Unemployment at 4.1 percent, close to a 30-year low, and higher earnings have buoyed confidence, and that should prolong the economy's record nine-year expansion, analysts said. Growth has also pushed the poverty rate to a 20-year low and sent household income to the highest ever in 1999, a separate report from the Census Bureau showed yesterday.
The Conference Board's survey of 5,000 households found the share of consumers expecting more jobs to become available rose to 17.9 percent in September from 17.1 percent in August. The share of consumers seeing jobs as hard to get dropped to 10.7 percent from 11.5 percent.
"Until this rises -- which means until the unemployment rate rises -- confidence will remain strong," said Ian Shepherdson, chief United States economist at High Frequency Economics Ltd. in Valhalla, N.Y.
Still, consumers may be scaling back spending plans, the report suggested. The share of survey participants expecting to buy new homes, cars and major appliances all fell in September.
Oil prices that rose to a 10-year high last week have pushed up the cost of gasoline and heating oil. That leaves less money for other things.
The price of gasoline at the pump was as high as $1.60 a gallon this month, up 22 percent since the start of the year. Home heating oil prices are up 37 percent so far this year.
"The oil price run-up will start to hurt confidence a bit if it continues," said Ethan Harris, senior economist at Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc. "Once people start seeing home heating oil bills, that could do it."
