SECTION: Section C; Page 2; Column 1; Business/Financial Desk

LENGTH: 1021 words

HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
Don't underestimate the government's role in building the economy.

BYLINE: By Jeff Madrick; This column appears here every Thursday. Jeff Madrick is the editor of Challenge Magazine and a new collection of essays, "Unconventional Wisdom: Alternative Perspectives on the New Economy." Mr. Madrick, Virginia Postrel, Alan B. Krueger and Hal R. Varian rotate as contributors.

 BODY:
GEORGE W. BUSH has finally made government the issue of the presidential campaign. After wasting months focusing on Al Gore's character, he returned to what was always the Republicans' meat and potatoes. As president, Vice President Gore would give you big government, Governor Bush now emphasizes. But if voters elect him instead, Mr. Bush says, he would continue to tame Washington.
Mr. Bush is picking up, of course, from the legacy of Ronald Reagan, who was determined to bench government as long as he could. President Reagan helped make entrepreneurialism and profits respectable again, which was no small achievement. And some deflation of government was no doubt deserved. In the 1960's and 1970's, advocates of progressive actions underestimated how difficult such tasks as reducing poverty and revitalizing the inner cities really were, and did not take sufficient care to develop new policies that would be effective. Americans also overestimated the nation's potential affluence. Remember guns and butter? 
 But the oversimplified fear of big government has been damaging and does not accurately reflect American history. On the crucial role of government in the nation's economic success, our past is unambiguous. Beginning with Jefferson's presidency, the federal government may have resisted building a national system of roads, but by the 1820's the states picked up the ball. Led by New York, the states invested millions of dollars, which was big money then, in new canals, and New York even made loans to business. Where would the early economy have been without these canals?
In those same years, federal laws were passed to protect the rights to the ownership of property for millions of Americans, including squatters, even though it violated the sanctity of the contracts the nation's rich landowners held. To Hernando de Soto, a Peruvian development specialist and author of "The Mystery of Capital" (Basic Books), America's far-seeing protection of property rights was a key to economic success.
Before the Civil War, there was a pitched battle over whether primary education should remain private, but public education thankfully prevailed, and America became the best-educated nation in the world. Now, economists believe educational attainment is the foremost economic resource of a nation.
Throughout the 1800's, the federal government aggressively subsidized the building of the railroads. After a couple of decades of a simplistic Social Darwinian ideology of "survival of the fittest" at the end of the 19th century, the progressives established regulations that made competition fairer and protected workers from abusive hours and labor conditions.
In the early 1900's, local governments built much-needed high schools. In the 1930's, Social Security and unemployment insurance were introduced over the vehement objections of conservatives. And after World War II, the federal government was responsible for the highway system, the G.I. Bill of Rights, the Pentagon-sponsored research that resulted in the Internet, and civil rights legislation that helped bring African-Americans into the economic fold.
The record is plain: government has done many things awfully well. And beyond the historical evidence, there is also strong statistical support for the idea that government investment has produced substantial returns. Ishaq Nadiri, an economist at New York University, has developed with colleagues a provocative model of public investment that is widely accepted. Their research shows that the return on federal transportation investment, for example, has been every bit as strong as the returns on private investment in recent years (and even stronger in the past) because such investment benefits society as a whole.
Economic theory is consistent with Professor Nadiri's findings. Private investors will systematically underinvest in areas in which such social returns exceed private returns because the individual companies themselves will not enjoy all the benefits. Good theory, then, tells us that investments in public goods, from roads, sewers and airports to research and education, cannot be completely entrusted to private markets.
Since the Reagan era began, America has largely neglected many of these areas. Of course, government will make mistakes (so does business). But consider that federal investment in transportation, research and development and education and training has fallen to only 1.7 percent of G.D.P., 30 percent lower than it was in 1970. At former levels, it would have been roughly an additional $100 billion of spending a year.
The new economy is also creating new needs that are going unmet. Paying down the nation's debt is apparently our priority, but two-worker families desperately need quality day care. The information age requires even better education, but according to Richard Rothstein and Karen Hawley Miles, both education specialists, schools have increased inflation-adjusted spending per pupil since the late 1960's by only 1 percent a year, much less than the growth of the economy.
What does the low level of teachers' salaries say about our society? Nancy Folbre, an economist at the University of Massachusetts at Amherst, argues that such caring professions, including nursing and caring for the elderly, are themselves public goods that require serious government assistance.
And, although deregulation and privatization have been useful, regulatory vigilance cannot simply be relaxed across the board as the economy changes. What are we to make of deregulation in a time when tire companies neglect safety standards, and the number of product recalls continues to set records? Despite the dangerous collapse of the hedge fund Long-Term Investment Capital two years ago, no new requirements for public disclosure of financial information are in the works.
Both business and government have vital roles in the economy, and always have. But for many people, government has become an us-versus-them issue. For the sake of the nation's economy, the next president should realize that the government is us.

HEADLINE: Danes to Vote on Adopting the Euro

BYLINE: By ROGER COHEN

DATELINE: COPENHAGEN, Sept. 27

 BODY:
Denmark, pursuing an odd calling as the barometer of Europe's mood, holds its fourth referendum in less than a decade on Thursday on what degree of integration with the European Union the country desires.
This time, the question being put to just over four million voters is whether Denmark should follow 12 other states of the 15-member Union and join a shared European currency, the euro. But the real issue may be summed up otherwise: it's the politics, stupid. 
 The Danish currency, the krone, is pegged to the euro and has followed the currency's 25 percent slide against the dollar during the past 21 months. This link will be maintained whatever the outcome of the vote.
But even if this arrangement ties Denmark irrevocably to Europe's economic fate, many Danes are concerned about adopting a currency they see as a political symbol of a push for a European superstate in which the identity of small nations like Denmark would be diminished.
"Countries should have their own borders, their own defense and their own currency, but all of this is being eroded by attempts to create a United States of Europe," said Peter Skaarup, a member of the right-wing Danish People's Party. "If we give up the krone, we won't be masters in our own house any more."
Such fears have bolstered the "No" campaign, and most opinion polls suggest that Danes will reject the euro. The vote, however, is too close to call, with at least 10 percent of the electorate undecided, and the government of Social Democratic Prime Minister Poul Nyrup Rasmussen is placing its faith in a last-minute swing in favor of the euro.
Other European governments will be watching closely. The euro's weakness since its introduction in 1999 has been tied partly to a market perception that the will does not exist in the European Union to pursue the sort of political integration that would bolster the currency.
A Danish rejection would inevitably reinforce such sentiments at a time when the European Central Bank, with support from the United States and Japan, has already felt obliged to intervene in support of the currency. Any prospect of Britain and Sweden adopting the euro would also recede.
A Gallup poll published today showed 47 percent of Danes against the euro and 41 percent in favor. A poll by the National Statistics Office gave the anti-euro camp an even wider victory, with 51.7 percent voting "No" and 41.3 percent "Yes." Only one survey, by the Vilstrup polling center, gave a narrow victory to euro supporters.
Jorn Thulstrup, a pollster who has followed the national mood closely and was predicting a month ago that Denmark would accept the euro, said he had now reluctantly concluded that the country would reject it.
Fears about pensions reflect anxiety among Danes that joining the euro would open the way to outside domination in the form of an insistence that Denmark cut its taxes, scale back its welfare state and deregulate its economy along the lines that markets generally favor.
Many Danish Socialists continue to oppose the euro on the grounds that it would prove incompatible with their welfare state. As a result, the left and right have come to oppose the euro for different reasons.
"We are facing an unholy alliance of right-wing nationalists concerned about our identity and left-wingers worried about economic freedom," said John Iversen, a spokesman for the Social Democratic Party.

HEADLINE: THE 2000 CAMPAIGN: THE TEXAS GOVERNOR;
Bush Welcomes Vouchers, But the Word Eludes Him

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Sept. 27

 BODY:
Gov. George W. Bush concluded three days of education events today with a visit to a parochial school in this city's heavily Hispanic South-Central section, talking about reading, character education and discipline. But there was one issue Mr. Bush did not so much as mention: school vouchers.
That Mr. Bush, the Republican candidate for president, would avoid discussing at a Catholic school a policy that many Catholic parents staunchly support -- and that he has endorsed -- spoke volumes about the way he has been campaigning on the education issue this week. 
 On Monday, Mr. Bush's aides distributed a 16-page pamphlet on his education agenda that does not use the words school vouchers. On Tuesday afternoon, when asked if he supported vouchers, Mr. Bush sidestepped the issue by saying the decision should be left to state and local officials. And on Tuesday evening, when Larry King asked him on his CNN program whether he thought vouchers worked, Mr. Bush replied, "I'm not positive."
From his extraordinarily careful handling of the issue, one might conclude that Mr. Bush harbored deep reservations about vouchers. But in fact, a voucherlike proposal is an integral part of his plan to push public schools to be more accountable for student achievement.
Under his proposal, the federal government could take aid away from chronically failing schools and give it directly to low-income parents. The parents could use the money -- $1,500 per student -- for a variety of educational purposes, including private school tuition.
That is the widely accepted definition of a voucher, yet Mr. Bush declines to use the word, suggesting that he considers it loaded with bad connotations. Instead, he and his running mate, Dick Cheney, emphasize the concept of choice. Their plan, they say, would provide poor children trapped in bad schools a range of options normally available only to the more affluent, such as hiring tutors, transferring to other public schools or attending private schools.
"A voucher connotes that you want everybody to take the money and flee into the private world," said B. Alexander Kress, an education adviser to Mr. Bush. "I think the governor is of the notion that the problems will be fixed by the public schools. But if they aren't, then there are these other options."
But aides to Vice President Al Gore, the Democratic presidential nominee, contend that Mr. Bush is running away from a contentious policy that they say is widely opposed by the moderate swing voters, particularly women, he is assiduously courting.
Mr. Bush "realizes most Americans don't agree with vouchers," asserted Kym Spell, a spokeswoman for the Gore campaign. "That's why they don't use the word vouchers for what is a voucher plan."
Actually, many polls suggest that the public is divided on the matter. A New York Times/CBS News poll conducted in July showed that 47 percent of voters supported the concept of taxpayer-financed vouchers, while 46 percent opposed it.
Mr. Gore strongly opposes taxpayer-financed vouchers, arguing that they drain public aid away from the schools that need it most. That is also the position of the powerful teachers unions that are expected to provide important support in getting out the Democratic vote in November.
Viewed more broadly, Mr. Bush's approach to vouchers is similar to his handling of a number of contentious conservative policies. Mr. Bush has won support from groups like the National Rifle Association and the National Right to Life Committee by opposing most forms of gun control and by vowing to sign legislation outlawing the procedure known by opponents as partial-birth abortion. Yet he rarely discusses those views on the stump.
During a week in which Mr. Bush focused on education, hammering at Mr. Gore's policies and warning of an "education recession," his avoidance of the word, or even the concept, of vouchers was often striking.
The pamphlet his campaign issued on Monday, titled "Ending the Education Recession," says that Mr. Bush of Texas would give parents "real options -- like tutoring or the ability to transfer to another school," avoiding any mention that the money could be used for private or parochial schools.
At a public high school in Redwood City, Calif., on Tuesday, a student asked Mr. Bush how he felt about vouchers. Mr. Bush never acknowledged that he supported the concept.
"If the state of California wants to do that, it's up to the state of California," he said. "I believe in local control of schools."
Mr. Cheney offered a similar answer to voters in a visit to Michigan on Tuesday. Michigan and California have hotly contested ballot initiatives this year calling for the creation of voucher systems.
Later on Tuesday, during his interview on "Larry King Live," Mr. Bush said, "I'm not positive vouchers work." But, he continued, "I think we ought to try them."

SECTION: Section B; Page 1; Column 2; Metropolitan Desk

LENGTH: 1634 words

HEADLINE: In Brooklyn, a Vision Of a Cultural Haven;
Academy Plan Generates Buzz and Backlash

BYLINE: By JULIAN E. BARNES

 BODY:
The three desolate lots that encircle the Brooklyn Academy of Music have gnawed at Harvey Lichtenstein for decades.
Mr. Lichtenstein made his reputation rebuilding the academy of music by discovering how to turn Manhattan's attention to Brooklyn. Under Mr. Lichtenstein, the academy's stately Beaux-Arts opera house was filled with provocative and avant-garde productions. The strategy worked, bringing in the kind of Manhattanite who never before would have admitted to taking the No. 4 train past Bowling Green. 
 But even as the academy thrived, the lots around it remained as scars on the street grid. When Mr. Lichtenstein stepped down in 1999, after 32 years as president and artistic director of the academy, the empty spaces continued to bother him. So he took charge of the academy's local development corporation to see if what clicked for an old opera house might work for vacant lots.
In recent months, Mr. Lichtenstein has been trying to sell artists, community leaders and public officials on his idea of raising up a cultural district on those empty tracts and in the surrounding area. He envisions a visual and performing arts library, a charter school, a new-media center, a mixed-use arts complex, theaters, nonprofit offices, a museum, retail space, loft housing and a hotel.
To match his ambitions, Mr. Lichtenstein dreams of unique buildings for the project and has hired Rem Koolhaas, a Dutch architect known for nontraditional designs. Mr. Lichtenstein says he hopes that Mr. Koolhaas will draft building plans that can lure established cultural institutions and create a great public architecture rarely seen in Brooklyn.
"BAM had made a reputation as a performing arts center, but the context never grew up around it," Mr. Lichtenstein said. "Now it is the right time to develop it as a cultural district."
So far, Mr. Lichtenstein's game plan appears to be working. The city has pledged $25 million to the project and the academy's renovation of the Strand Theater, just north of the academy. Construction has begun on a new headquarters for the Mark Morris Dance Group. The development corporation is negotiating with other dance companies, theater groups, nonprofit organizations and a museum, all of which feel squeezed out of Manhattan. Mr. Lichtenstein has also piqued the interest of a charter school and the Brooklyn Public Library.
In short, he has begun to generate buzz.
But where there is buzz there is backlash. Some community leaders in Fort Greene, the neighborhood next to the academy, are worried that Mr. Koolhaas's ideas will clash with the neighborhood's brownstone architecture. Business owners who have helped usher in an African-American renaissance along Fulton Street worry about being left out of the project. Rents have already shot up in Fort Greene in recent months, and some black residents fear that the project will make the neighborhood whiter.
Mr. Lichtenstein and Jeanne Lutfy, the president of the development corporation, argue vociferously that they are taking care to make sure their development only helps residents and merchants.
But the district, Mr. Lichtenstein has made clear, is not supposed to be artistically safe. It is supposed to be exciting.
Although Mr. Lichtenstein has filled his office at the development corporation with the trappings of an urban planner (zoning rules, aerial photos, land-use maps), he still thinks like the creative director of a performing arts group.
Mr. Lichtenstein's wild eyebrows, tightly packed teeth and wide smile give him an impish look that match his love of attention-getting actions.
"The district will develop like a work of art," he said recently as he and Ms. Lutfy discussed their plans. "It will be a creative process. It could be terrible; it could come out terribly."
As Mr. Lichtenstein said "terrible," Ms. Lutfy shifted uncomfortably in her seat and tried to cut him off. But Mr. Lichtenstein would not stop.
"Anything creative starts out with that possibility, that it could be terrible," he continued. "But we have the commitment, flexibility and nerve to go through with it."
Mr. Lichtenstein hopes his first show of nerve was hiring Mr. Koolhaas. Only the initial stages of research and study have begun, but Mr. Koolhaas has clearly begun to think about what he wants to create.
Mr. Koolhaas argues that Manhattan has begun a process of super-gentrification, becoming an island of shiny buildings and unchecked consumerism.
"In Brooklyn, I want to set up a polarity," Mr. Koolhaas said. "I want something less shiny, less consumerist, more productive."
Mr. Koolhaas first made his reputation not with a building but with a book on architecture, "Delirious New York," first published in 1978. In the book, Mr. Koolhaas argues the city's position as a cultural capital is intertwined with its soaring, dense architecture. And that idea, he said, informs the Brooklyn Academy of Music's project. Packing many cultural institutions close together will produce creative results, for both the programs and the architecture, Mr. Koolhaas said.
Those possibilities clearly excite some of the proposed tenants. The Brooklyn Public Library is studying a proposal to build a $50 million, 100,000-square-foot visual and performing arts branch in the district.
"I look at the library as really being the center of knowledge for a neighborhood," said Martin Gomez, executive director of the library. "Translate that to the BAM cultural district and you see that many of those groups are going to need a place to find information to enable them to pursue their craft."
The initial ideas that have popped forth for the cultural district from Mr. Koolhaas's Office for Metropolitan Architecture bear his distinct stamp. Mr. Koolhaas is known for his preoccupation with horizontal layering, rendering buildings like off-kilter sandwiches. His team's early ideas for the museum and the library incorporate layers, each dedicated to different uses, and each set off from the floor below and above.
Howard Pitsch, the president of the Fort Greene Association, said he was excited by the prospect of creating a district designed with "good, clean, contemporary modern architecture."
"Why should it be bad?" Mr. Pitsch said. "There is nothing but parking lots there now.
"If we can build Fort Greene as a cultural center, couldn't we all celebrate?" he said.
Others, though, are more reserved. Robert Evans, chairman of Community Board 2, said he liked the idea of the district. But he said architectural proposals that resulted in radical design, glass boxes or loft housing made him nervous.
"Everyone in the area is very much interested in protecting quality," Mr. Evans said. "We are not much interested in modernizing. You are going to create problems if the buildings are too outlandishly modern."
Business owners and residents worry that Mr. Lichtenstein will succeed too well, that he will change the character of the area. Some business owners in the Fort Greene shopping area known as the Bogolan District (named after the Mali word for "mud cloth") worry that the development could drive up commercial rents and provide new, subsidized retail space for Manhattan businesses, while shutting out existing merchants.
Jonathan Adewumi, chairman of the Bogolan Merchants Association, said he worried that visitors to the academy of music district would never venture beyond its borders into Fort Greene proper.
"You don't want to look across from your store and see a bright, shiny new retail space and you are not a part of it," Mr. Adewumi said. "I think the current project just looks at luring people from Manhattan and does not look at the people who are already here."
Tracye Gardner, a black Fort Greene resident and founder of the Brooklyn Young Filmmakers Center, said that more white families had moved into the area recently, and that she worried that black families were being pushed out.
"I am very happy about having a beautiful cultural district in my neighborhood, as long as I can stay in my neighborhood," Ms. Gardner said. "Fort Greene is already an amazing community in terms of the black resources and black history, but that could change radically. It could become a culture district for a portion of Manhattan that is relocating."
Backers of the project contend that the cultural district is not the cause of gentrification. Rather, the project is the product of the same forces that are gentrifying Fort Greene.
"Gentrification is happening in Fort Greene already, even though we haven't done anything yet," said Bruce C. Ratner, chairman of the academy of music and the president of Forest City Ratner Companies, a real estate development company. "That proves that what causes gentrification are economic good times."
Ms. Lutfy said that the development corporation had extended the borders of the cultural district into the Fort Greene shopping area, in an effort to integrate those businesses into the project. Both the development corporation officials and architects argue that they are taking pains to ensure that any plans they come up with enhance the brownstone neighborhood.
"We are trying to situate this so that it is not an alien mega-project dropped in the neighborhood," said Charles Renfro, the project manager for Diller & Scofidio, the New York architecture firm working with Mr. Koolhaas.
Mr. Ratner said he thought that in the end, Brooklyn would accept a more avant-garde architectural proposal than parts of Manhattan might.
"Hopefully, we are at a stage in the city where we are more accepting of extraordinary architecture even if it doesn't mesh with what people are used to," Mr. Ratner said. "It's even expected that we at BAM will do something that is cutting edge and contemporary."

HEADLINE: Mrs. Clinton Offers Debt-Reduction Plan; Lazio Questions Its Value

BYLINE: By DEAN E. MURPHY

 BODY:
Supported by Robert E. Rubin, the former secretary of the Treasury Department, and Warren Buffet, the billionaire investor, Hillary Rodham Clinton pledged yesterday to protect the federal budget surplus from Republican "tax schemes" by proposing legislation to limit tax cuts and new spending.
Mrs. Clinton, laying out one of her most detailed plans for the budget surplus since entering the New York Senate race, proposed mandatory "debt-reduction caps" that would make it more difficult for Congress to use expected budget surpluses for cutting taxes or paying for new programs. 
 Her plan, similar in general terms to the one proposed by Vice President Al Gore, goes further than Mr. Gore's by setting aside more money for debt reduction and imposing legislative hurdles for making exceptions.
Under Mrs. Clinton's plan, 20 percent of the budget surplus each year over the next 10 years, apart from money for Social Security and Medicare, would be reserved for reducing federal debt. That would create a pool of about $360 billion, her campaign staff estimated.
If Congress wanted to spend that money or use it to offset tax cuts, it would have to do so with a vote of three-fifths of the members of both houses, she said. In addition, the estimated $2.7 trillion surplus in the Social Security and Medicare accounts would be set aside for debt reduction, she said.
According to her campaign staff's estimates, Mrs. Clinton's proposal would devote about $60 billion more over the 10 years for debt reduction than would Mr. Gore's budget-surplus plan. Mr. Gore's plan also does not require a three-fifths vote for breaking the spending and tax cut limit.
"We have to put first things first and be conservative in how we use this surplus and project our expenditures," Mrs. Clinton said during an economic forum at Columbia University. "I would propose that we set some limits on what we can do with the surplus and just keep everybody on the same page."
But the Republicans said the spending habits of Mrs. Clinton and the Democrats, not their party's proposed tax cuts, are the biggest threat to the surplus. Representative Rick A. Lazio, speaking in Newburgh after touring a riverfront development project, suggested that Mrs. Clinton's policy proposals would leave little of the surplus left to apply toward reducing the debt. He cited a report by the National Taxpayers Union, a conservative group, that said her economic and education plans would nearly wipe out the surplus.
"So, it's fine rhetoric, but when you add up the numbers, it doesn't add up," Mr. Lazio said. "My economic plan would reserve three-quarters of the surplus to protect Social Security and Medicare, pay down the debt and also invest in some important priorities, like education."
Mrs. Clinton used her budget-surplus proposal as an opportunity once again to criticize Mr. Lazio's tax plan, which she says would give back too much of the projected $4.5 trillion surplus to taxpayers, and which Mr. Rubin said would "reject fiscal discipline." Speaking to an audience of students organized by the university's College Democrats club, Mrs. Clinton said they must look beyond their immediate self-interest.
Mr. Lazio spent the day in the Hudson Valley, touring businesses and the Newburgh waterfront and speaking at a business breakfast in Rockland County. The visits were intended to highlight the potential for his plan for improving the economy through an increase in partnerships between the federal government and the private sector, and through lower taxes that Mr. Lazio says will stimulate the economy.
"I think what we're doing here is we're demonstrating that there is one candidate in this race who has got the capacity to build the kind of partnerships that will be necessary to improve the quality of life for New York," Mr. Lazio said.

HEADLINE: Parent Says Chrysler Unit Will Post a Third-Quarter Loss

BYLINE: By EDMUND L. ANDREWS

DATELINE: FRANKFURT, Sept. 27

 BODY:
DaimlerChrysler A.G. said today that its Chrysler car group would report a loss in the third quarter -- the first such loss since a recession in the United States nine years ago -- and that Chrysler's highly respected design chief, Thomas C. Gale, would retire at the end of this year.
Though neither announcement had been entirely unexpected, they provided new evidence that DaimlerChrysler remains deeply divided between its American and German car companies long after their merger. 
 Mr. Gale, 57, is the latest in a long series of top Chrysler executives to bail out of the company since the merger two years ago.
Thomas T. Stallkamp, Chrysler's former president and the top American working on integration of the two companies, quit last year after his relations with German executives soured.
Robert J. Eaton, Chrysler's former chairman, who had been a co-chairman of DaimlerChrysler, retired in March, earlier than expected. Many others below the top ranks have left as well.
DaimlerChrysler attributed Chrysler's anticipated third-quarter loss to extremely stiff price competition in the United States and the costs of bringing new models to the market quickly.
But the company insisted that Chrysler would again turn profitable in the last quarter of the year and asserted that total operating profits this year would match those of 1999.
That would be better than many analysts had been fearing, because rumors have been circulating for weeks that the entire company might report a loss and that full-year profits would decline sharply. Reflecting the relief, shares in DaimlerChrysler rose 2.6 percent in Frankfurt, to 52.8 euros or about $46.62. In New York, shares rose 94 cents, to $45.94.
That said, the loss at Chrysler is extraordinary given the company's reputation for profitability during the mid-1990's. It is the first such loss since an $82 million shortfall in the third quarter of 1991 -- and that was a recession year, in contrast to the flush economic times in the United States now.
Investor concern about Chrysler is reflected in the shares of the parent company, which have still lost more than half their value since they peaked at $108 in January 1999. And the basic problems confronting Chrysler are not over. Though the retro-styled PT Cruiser has been a runaway hit, other models are getting stale and need to be revamped.
In a determined effort to hold its market share, Chrysler has torn into its profit margins by spending vast sums on discount programs and other incentives to consumers.
In outlining its predictions for the rest of the year, DaimlerChrysler said that Chrysler would lose about $530 million in the third quarter of this year but would post an overall profit for the year of about $1.7 billion.
Jurgen Schrempp, the chairman of DaimlerChrysler, has already announced plans for billions of dollars in cost-cutting moves during this year and next.
But the company is looking for additional cuts, while also trying to speed up the introduction of new models and expand production of the PT Cruiser.
In contrast to Chrysler, Mercedes has continued to post healthy gains. Even though the German market for midprice cars shriveled this year, largely because of a flood of nearly new used cars on the market, Mercedes sales increased slightly in Germany and about 4.5 percent worldwide.
Even the much-criticized Smart, the snub-nosed two-seat vehicle that was a flop, has bounced back. Its sales jumped 67 percent through August, to 69,500 cars.
The resignation of Mr. Gale had been widely expected, but it could be an important loss. Having spent 33 years at Chrysler, Mr. Gale oversaw the design of cars ranging from the popular Chrysler Intrepid sedan to the Dodge Viper, an eye-catching muscle car that helped jazz up Dodge's dusty image.
Rumors about Mr. Gale's departure spread as soon as it became clear that Mr. Stallkamp was on his way out as president. Earlier this year, when asked in an interview about his intention to stay with the company, he carefully avoided making any long-term commitments.
Today, Mr. Gale strongly implied he had indeed begun planning his departure about that time.
"When Jim Holden took his position as president, I agreed to stay on and see the future product plan through, while continuing to build the team to succeed me," Mr. Gale said. "Now that both of those processes are complete and we're in the next phase of development, it's time for me to step back."
Mr. Gale will be replaced by Richard O. Schaum, who has been in charge of engineering platforms -- the basic chassis and components that are common to many different car models. Trevor M. Creed, who was recently promoted to senior vice president in charge of design, will be responsible for the more visible aspects of exterior and interior styling.
Mr. Gale also said there are no plans to replace him on the 13-member DaimlerChrysler management board. His departure would leave a 12-member board -- 9 from Daimler and 3 from Chrysler.

Jeep Investment in China
By The New York Times
SHANGHAI, Sept. 27 -- DaimlerChrysler will invest $118 million in cash to revive Beijing Jeep, one of the earliest joint ventures in China, which has been making the same modified Jeep for the last 15 years.
The investment is part of a $226 million revamping plan meant to prepare the joint venture for competition after China enters the World Trade Organization and duties on imported sport utility vehicles fall. DaimlerChrysler plans to produce two new models at the venture in the next three years. China is expected to join the W.T.O. within the next few months.

HEADLINE: Protests Diminish at Conference in Prague on Worldwide Aid

BYLINE: By JOSEPH KAHN

DATELINE: PRAGUE, Sept. 27

 BODY:
Protests diminished sharply today as financial officials and many demonstrators condemned an outbreak of violence and vandalism on Tuesday that turned a conference on aid into a street battle.
The officials ended the conference a half-day early, saying the formal presentations on the agenda had been completed ahead of schedule. 
 The officials said moving up the closing ceremony, originally planned for Thursday, had nothing to do with the protests.
Some bilateral meetings and news conferences remain scheduled for Thursday.
The delegates have been meeting here for a week. Although some began leaving today, the senior officials of the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund, which organize the annual event, stayed.
But shattered glass covered the sidewalks along Wenceslas Square, where hundreds of protesters -- many masked anarchists with clubs -- smashed a Kentucky Fried Chicken, a Dunkin' Donuts and a Mercedes-Benz dealership on Tuesday. Earlier that day they had repeatedly clashed with the police near the hilltop Conference Center that was the site of the finance meeting.
About 100 people were injured, including 63 police officials, a spokesman for the government said. The police arrested 420 people. But many of those taken into custody were quickly released.
The police today sealed the protesters' center of operations in an old factory on the outskirts of Prague.
This afternoon, two cordons of officers surrounded several hundred protesters in a downtown square, holding them in place until they agreed to disperse.
The violence helped the demonstrators generate publicity, as well as highlighting a gulf between them and representatives of poor nations that they often say they represent.
"Violence has no place in a civilized society," Finance Minister Yashwant Sinha of India told delegates this morning. "I stand here on behalf of India, the land of Mahatma Gandhi, who espoused the cause of nonviolence, and I condemn the violence which was unleashed here."
Czech officials said the protesters had lied to them when they promised, at marathon negotiations before the meetings, to conduct peaceful sit-ins.
Protest organizers distanced themselves from what they called hoodlums and extremists who had no ties to the main march. Even some anarchists criticized the violence.
"We agreed to nonviolence, and it all turned out badly," said a 24-year-old Brazilian who gave his name as Ivan and wore a "Punk Not Dead" T-shirt caked with dust. He sat with other protesters on Peace Square today, hemmed in by the officers who would not let them march. "But I should say that I respect the people who committed the violence. They are not my enemy. The enemy is the I.M.F."
Though protesters distracted the news media from the conference, just as they had helped to undermine a similar meeting last year in Seattle, some said the almost ritualistic violence common to several recent antiglobalization protests obscured their main message.
Many protesters argue that the World Bank and the I.M.F. do more harm than good in poor countries and need to be radically overhauled or abolished.
"The media only care about the violence, not about our arguments," said Katarina Bartovicova, 22, of Bratislava, Slovakia. "They are making us pay for yesterday."

HEADLINE: From Europe, Creative Taxation;
Hungry for Revenue and Seeing Web Sales as a Banquet

BYLINE: By JOHN TAGLIABUE

DATELINE: PARIS, Sept. 27

 BODY:
Europe is rushing to tear down borders, but the Internet is toppling some of them even faster, making life distinctly uncomfortable for European policy makers.
A big source of that discomfort is the threat of lost tax revenue, because European nations depend far more on taxes on consumption than the United States does. So in contrast to the United States -- where a federal commission failed to agree on how states should tax basic electronic commerce and mail-order sales like books, consumer electronics and other products -- European officials are pushing hard to adopt a seemingly narrow but potentially far-reaching proposal to ensure that digital products bought in Europe over the Internet do not escape taxation. 
 Essentially, the legislation proposed in June by the European Commission, the Brussels-based executive arm of the 15-nation European Union, defines digital products like software and video programming downloaded by computer as services rather than goods.
The idea is to ensure that such transactions face the value-added tax, an elaborate system begun in Europe in which purchases are taxed at each stage from production to final sale. (However lucrative, it is a system that politicians in the United States have shied away from.)
Normally in Europe, services would be taxed in the country of origin, meaning that European nations would gain no tax revenue from services flowing in from the outside. But this bill, which has stirred controversy not only on the Continent but among high-technology companies in the United States, would require companies outside Europe with sales here of such services exceeding 100,000 euros, or about $88,000, to register in one European country for the purpose of paying value-added taxes.
The stakes are significant. In 1999, European e-commerce sales were $3.4 billion, or less than one-half of 1 percent of total sales. But by some estimates, such sales in Europe may rise as high as 12 percent of the total in the next few years. And while sales of digital products from outside Europe are only a small fraction of that, policy makers -- even more than their counterparts in the United States -- are fretting over possible lost revenue.
In contrast to the United States, where relatively few people pay state sales taxes on Internet purchases, European governments already impose national value-added taxes on goods sold over the Internet within Europe. The tax rates and the list of goods covered vary from country to country. For services sold by European companies in Europe, the V.A.T. is paid by the selling company in its home country.
As a result, European governments already tax e-commerce more than the United States does. The Internet Tax Freedom Act, passed by Congress in 1998, created a moratorium on sales taxes on e-commerce across state lines until 2001, and antitax forces in Congress have tried, so far unsuccessfully, to extend the moratorium until 2006.
Washington has sought to coordinate Internet tax policies with the Europeans through the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development in Paris. At an O.E.C.D. conference in Ottawa in 1998, the Europeans and the United States agreed on a list of principles for treating digital goods as services rather than manufactured products, to avoid customs duties on their delivery, and on jurisdiction in case of eventual taxation.
But here, the issue is considered a pressing one. European governments depend for roughly 40 percent of their total revenue on value-added taxes, while state sales taxes in the United States make up only about 25 percent of total state tax revenue. The budget of the European Commission is financed almost entirely by the V.A.T.
In Europe as in the United States, governments are also coming under pressure from traditional retailers that must collect taxes on all their sales -- taxes that Internet providers can skirt. The American software producer Symantec, for instance, which sells its Norton Utilities software to Europeans in several languages through its Web site, encourages customers to download the software directly, saying that there is "no value-added tax, shipping or export charge."
Still, serious obstacles face the proposed European legislation, and enactment is far from certain. For one thing, the bill will not become effective unless it is approved and enacted into national law by all 15 members of the European Union. It also needs to pass in the European Parliament. So difficult is the debate expected to be that Thomas Tindemans, a tax expert in the Brussels office of the law firm White & Case, said the delays would probably produce "a de facto moratorium."
What makes the debate complex is that the proposed legislation affects not only software but also other electronic services like pay-for-use radio and television broadcasting.
The path the Europeans have taken is not unlike the one Congress chose in July, when it passed legislation to resolve the issue of how to tax cellular phone use in the United States. Under the new rules, cell phones have a "primary service area" in which the telephone user pays state and local user taxes. But unlike the American legislation, the broader European measures face bitter opposition.
The European legislators are treading a thin line. National governments -- at pains to see their industries close the gap with the United States, where Internet use by business and consumers is ahead of Europe's -- do not want to impose extra burdens. Yet officials in Brussels also worry that inaction could encourage individual countries to take unilateral action to prevent tax erosion. Mr. Tindemans of White & Case said the goal appeared to be "to force debate at the European level and try to coordinate it."
Initial criticism has been intense. In the United States, the Software and Information Industry Association, a trade group, assailed the draft as "fatally flawed." Mark Bohannon, the group's general counsel, said the measure would be unenforceable because it is often impossible to tell where a customer is in cyberspace. He called the proposed law a step backward from international agreements not to tax the Internet.
In Europe, just about every interest group seems unhappy. At Eurocommerce, a trade organization for wholesale and retail distributors, officials are preparing a catalog of objections, including ones to the provision setting the threshold at 100,000 euros. They say that would discriminate against small European companies that already pay the tax while their small rivals abroad would not.
Moreover, the trade group hopes to use the issue as a cudgel to force governments to harmonize value-added tax rates at lower levels, arguing that the proposal encourages foreign companies to register in countries with little or no V.A.T. for digital products, like Britain, where the rate on some items is zero, rather than, say, Sweden and Denmark, where the uniform rate is 25 percent.
"You have the theory," said the chief tax adviser at Eurocommerce, Dimitris Kalogeras. "The practice is quite different."
Freddy Tengberg, chief executive of a company called Buyonet, could not agree more. When Mr. Tengberg, a former mechanical engineer with Exxon, set up his company in 1997 to sell software on the Web and to help other companies do likewise, he established headquarters in Sweden, but registered it in Seattle. The company sells all its products to Europe from the United States.
Buyonet's main competitors, Mr. Tengberg explained in an interview, are American companies like Beyond.com that enable European customers to download their products without paying the value-added tax. If Buyonet added Sweden's 25 percent rate to its prices, he said, "people just would not buy."
Mr. Tengberg, one of the most outspoken opponents of the legislation, called it "primitive and impossible to enforce." The authorities in Europe, he argues, would have no power to force American companies to register. And if American authorities cooperated, he said, many companies would simply move the computers used to deliver their products "to Uruguay, Burkina Faso or Brazil."
"In a digital world," he went on, "where you deliver from doesn't matter. A buyer in Bahrain gets the same service as a buyer in Philadelphia. It's a beautiful fact, but for the tax man it's a nightmare."
Therese Torris, a tax specialist at Forrester Research B.V., regards such comments as nonsense. So far, she said, the market for directly downloaded digital products consists mainly of software sales to corporate customers.
"Large companies have no incentive for tax evasion," Ms. Torris said, relying on governments for assistance in matters like antipiracy and copyright protection. Small businesses may seek to evade taxes, "but it's not in the interest of big companies."
"When they have a problem," Ms. Torris added, "they go to the government and the law."
She said European and American governments face a formidable challenge when digital goods are delivered online and paid for directly over the Internet. But she also said that the problem was purely technical and that companies specializing in tax-collection software were already providing solutions. Taxware International, for instance, recently began marketing software to track European value-added taxes.
Stephane Buydens, a tax specialist at the O.E.C.D., said Europe's value-added tax system could more readily be adjusted than what exists in the United States, where 7,000 revenue jurisdictions create a minefield of differing sales tax rates, and there are contrasting definitions of taxable and nontaxable items.
"In the long term, Europe will have to make some changes," he said. "In the United States, the discussion is about the system itself."
But Mr. Tengberg of Buyonet said that he, like many other executives of businesses operating in cyberspace, believes that the Internet ultimately spells the end of the European value-added tax system.
"It works in a physical market," he said. "It doesn't work here."

HEADLINE: Effect of Prison Building on Crime Is Weighed

BYLINE: By FOX BUTTERFIELD

 BODY:
The first studies to look at the link between the enormous growth of incarceration and the significant drop in crime in the 1990's show that the nation's prison-building boom has accounted for 5 percent to 25 percent of the eight-year decline. In light of those findings, some of the researchers questioned whether the benefits from the growth of incarceration were worth the cost to taxpayers.
The studies found that the number of inmates in jails and prisons quadrupled over the past two decades, to two million, and now cost about $40 billion a year. To avert one killing in the 1990's, it required locking up an additional 670 prisoners, at a cost of $13.4 million a year, according to an estimate by Richard Rosenfeld, a professor of criminology at the University of Missouri at St. Louis. 
 Professor Rosenfeld's findings are included in the most comprehensive of the new studies, a book, "The Crime Drop in America," to be published next month by Cambridge University Press. Written by a group of academic experts, the studies found that "no single factor can be invoked as the cause of the crime decline of the 1990's."
Instead, the authors conclude, "the explanation appears to lie with a number of factors," which interacted with each other, making "it difficult to isolate the relative contribution of any one of them."
Among these factors, in addition to the growth in incarceration, the authors said, were the ebbing of the crack epidemic, innovative efforts to keep guns out of the hands of criminals, new police tactics and an improved job market. The authors said it was difficult if not impossible to determine how much these factors contributed to the drop in crime.
In addition, Professor Rosenfeld said, the country may be undergoing a cultural change in which people have become less tolerant of certain forms of violence, particularly domestic violence, which has helped account for a decline in killings by adults every year since 1980.
"It is possible that American adults are becoming, in a word, more civilized," he said.
A similar cultural change beginning in the late Middle Ages in Western Europe, with the spread of courts of law and better manners, helped reduce crime so that homicide rates there today are only about one-tenth of what they were in the 14th century, other scholars have found.
Another study, released today by the Sentencing Project, a research and advocacy group in Washington, found that states that had increased the number of prison inmates the most in the 1990's actually had smaller reductions in crime than those states with below average increases in incarceration.
Texas led the nation with a 144 percent increase in prisoners from 1991 to 1998 and had a decrease in crime of 35 percent, the study said. But two other populous states, California and New York, which had much smaller increases in incarceration -- California of 52 percent and New York of 24 percent -- had even sharper drops in crime. In California, the drop was 36 percent and in New York, 43 percent.
In another recent study, of the impact of the growth in incarceration on the drop in crime, Anne Piehl, an associate professor of public policy at the John F. Kennedy School of Government at Harvard University, drawing on changes in crime and prison data for each state, found that the increase in incarceration from 1989 to 1999 accounted for only about 5 percent of the drop in overall violent and property crime.
Carlisle Moody, a professor of economics at the College of William and Mary who has long studied the link between incarceration and crime, criticized the new estimates of the effect of imprisonment, saying they were far too low because they did not take into account the recent history of prisons.
The rate at which criminals were incarcerated remained remarkably stable from about 1900 to the mid-1960's, as did the crime rate, Professor Moody said, but then, with a sharp increase in crime in the mid-60's, there was not an equivalent buildup in incarceration. The rate of incarceration has only recently begun to catch up with the crime rate.
"I'm pretty sure these other people are underestimating the effects of incarceration on crime because they are ignoring the historical changes," Professor Moody said. "Prison has a much greater effect on crime than anything else, like employment or policing."
The new book contains two studies of the impact of the growth in incarceration on the crime drop in the 1990's. Besides the study by Professor Rosenfeld, who estimated that incarceration reduced homicides by adults by 26 percent, a study by William Spelman, a professor of public policy at the Lyndon B. Johnson School of Public Affairs at the University of Texas, calculated that it reduced violent crime by 25 percent.

HEADLINE: The First Lady Heads West to Raise Money, Both Hard and Soft

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD J. LEVY

 BODY:
Hillary Rodham Clinton began a three-day fund-raising trip outside New York yesterday with a visit to Oklahoma City, where her host was a former state attorney general who came under scrutiny in one of the fund-raising investigations involving her husband's re-election.
Mrs. Clinton then held an event in Las Vegas last night, and after that travels to the Los Angeles area for two days. 
 Her opponent, Representative Rick A. Lazio, has criticized her for relying heavily on contributions from Hollywood celebrities, a traditional fund-raising source for Democrats, and at first, her aides would not disclose the names of sponsors of the California events.
They later acknowledged that one host would be Cher. The others include the financier Marvin Davis and his wife, Barbara.
Most of the money from the fund-raising trip is expected to go to Mrs. Clinton's Senate campaign, but she also plans to collect soft money for the Democratic Senatorial Campaign Committee.
In the New York contest, she and her Democratic allies have tentatively agreed not to spend soft money -- the largely unregulated contributions collected by parties in an effort to circumvent contribution limits -- under an arrangement with Mr. Lazio.
But Mrs. Clinton, who headed West after addressing an economic forum at Columbia University yesterday morning, has said she will continue to raise soft money, which would be used to help other Democrats, not her candidacy, as long as the New York arrangement stands.
Over all, Mrs. Clinton has received about 60 percent of her campaign contributions from out of state, more than Mr. Lazio or most Senate candidates.
"Rick Lazio has a clear financial advantage in this race because he has raised millions out-of-state with his insult-filled direct mail," said Howard Wolfson, a spokesman for the Clinton campaign. "As a result, we have to work harder to be competitive."
In Oklahoma City yesterday, the host for a fund-raising luncheon for Mrs. Clinton was Michael Turpen, the former state attorney general and one of the state's most prominent Democrats.
Mr. Turpen's fund-raising activities were examined by Congressional Republicans in 1997 after he raised a large donation for the Democratic National Committee from an Indian tribe in Oklahoma that has sought to reclaim federal land that the tribe believes was stolen from it.
Mr. Turpen had been hired as a lobbyist by the tribe, the Cheyenne-Arapaho. He suggested to tribal leaders that they could gain access to federal officials if they pledged a $100,000 donation to the Democrats, tribal leaders said.
The leaders were soon invited to a lunch with President Clinton at the White House, where they asked him for help on the land issue. The president, according to the tribal leaders, said he would look into the matter.
The president ultimately took no action, and after the circumstances surrounding the donation were publicized, the Democratic National Committee returned the money. But Republicans in Congress who investigated the affair called it part of a pattern of Democratic fund-raising abuses.
Mr. Wolfson, the spokesman for Mrs. Clinton's campaign, said the fund-raiser sponsored by Mr. Turpen yesterday was "perfectly legal and appropriate."
Mr. Turpen did not respond to two messages left for him.
Also yesterday, Mrs. Clinton's campaign manager, Bill de Blasio, sent a letter to the National Abortion Rights Action League, requesting that it refrain from airing any advertising attacking Mr. Lazio for his position on abortion, in deference to the agreement between the Lazio and Clinton campaigns.
Leaders of the group had initially chafed at the deal, arguing that it interfered with their rights. After meeting yesterday, board members said they would unveil a new advertisement today, but pointedly did not say whether the advertisement would be aired.
Several Democrats said last night it was likely that NARAL would unveil the ad today, expecting that it would be the subject of news reports, but would not buy ad time in deference to the deal.

HEADLINE: A BIG PUSH IS ON FOR NATURAL GAS UNDER THE ARCTIC

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE

DATELINE: INUVIK, Northwest Territories

 BODY:
For three decades, geologists probing beneath the Arctic Ocean near here and offshore Alaska have mapped the largest known untapped reserves of natural gas in North America: 165 trillion cubic feet, seven times what the United States will consume this year.
But, until recently, with gas prices low, indigenous opposition high and environmental issues looming, energy companies never found a way to build pipelines to send the gas south to heat homes and to generate electricity. 
 This is changing as fast as it takes to read a home heating bill.
Wholesale prices for natural gas have doubled over the last year, and industry analysts expect that the 55 million Americans who heat their homes with gas will pay 25 to 40 percent more than they did last winter. And with the Energy Department forecasting that demand for natural gas in the United States will rise 40 percent in the next two decades, prices may go higher still.
At the same time, native groups that once opposed development of the big gas fields near here now spy chances for economic development, and are campaigning for royalties and jobs.
After a winter of planning and politicking, energy companies, pipeline operators and Indian tribes expect to announce next spring plans to build one, and possibly two, subterranean pipelines to carry Arctic gas to North America's pipeline grid. Building two pipelines, one from Alaska along the Trans-Alaska oil pipeline and the Alaska-Canada Highway, and the other from Canada's Northwest Territories, along the Mackenzie River Valley, would cost $10 billion and take seven years.
While pipeline company executives say they are confident they can finance and engineer the projects, environmental problems remain unresolved and some conservation groups vow opposition. Development also portends radical change for isolated places like this one-stoplight town, where the barks of sled dogs herald the long Arctic night and the northernmost road in North America trails off into tundra.
Arguments in favor of tapping the gas fields echo those heard three decades ago, when the United States debated building the Trans-Alaska pipeline to move crude oil south from Prudhoe Bay. The theme is self-reliance in energy in a world buffeted by higher oil prices orchestrated by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries.
"We aren't dependent on OPEC for gas," said K. Terry Koonce, president of the Exxon Mobil Production Company. "Gas is up to us Americans to bring about."
Often called a continental commodity, natural gas consumed in the United States comes from North America, delivered by pipelines. Canada, which doubled its exports to the United States in the 1990's, now supplies 16 percent of American consumption, and Canadian executives expect exports to the United States to grow 50 percent this decade.
Last summer, as brownouts hit California and electricity bills jumped in New York, consumers and politicians started demanding new power plants. But with natural gas the fuel of choice for 90 percent of power plants now built in the United States, few people asked the follow-up question: With demand already outstripping supply, where is this gas for these plants going to come from?
The Energy Department predicts that consumption will increase to 31.5 trillion cubic feet a year by 2020, largely because it is a relatively clean fuel. Gas, when burned, produces only one-third the carbon dioxide for each unit of energy as coal and two-thirds as much as oil.
While high prices led energy companies to double their exploration efforts in the United States over the last year, industry executives do not expect production in the lower 48 states to pace consumption. Energy companies and consumer groups see Arctic reserves as crucial to meet future needs.
Environmentalists, who bitterly opposed the Trans-Alaska oil pipeline in the 1970's, are split over building gas pipelines in the Arctic. Greenpeace opposes them, saying Americans should wean themselves off fossil fuels and invest the billions of dollars it would take to build pipelines in alternative energy sources like solar and wind. Other groups, recognizing that gas pollutes less than oil, plan to focus on reducing the environmental impact of the lines by pushing to have them follow the existing Alaska oil pipeline and the Alaska-Canada Highway.
In Canada and the United States, federal and local authorities will have to approve drilling plans and pipeline construction, but industry executives said the rising price of gas made their case for the project.
"At the end of the day, consumers are going to make a lot of pressure to have this gas flow to the lower 48," said Michael E. Phelps, president of WestCoast Energy Inc. WestCoast is working with TransCanada PipeLines Ltd. to build a gas pipeline from Alaska to Alberta, where it would tie into existing lines south to the United States.
Some development has already begun, particularly in Alaska, where companies have been pumping oil from under Prudhoe Bay for more than two decades and sending it south through the Trans-Alaska pipeline.
Canada, too, has moved ahead. Chasing what Canadians call frontier gas, the first pipeline nosed "north of 60" in May. This small link, connecting gas fields in Fort Liard, Northwest Territories, to the continent's pipeline grid, crossed a psychological barrier, the 60th parallel, a line on maps that separates the settled southern provinces from frontier northern territories.
In Canada's first commercial gas development north of the Arctic Circle, Inuvik Gas Ltd. started pumping gas a year ago to houses in this town of 3,500 people on the Mackenzie River. The company is jointly owned by Enbridge Inc. and AltaGas Services Inc. of Calgary, Alberta, and the Inuvialuit Petroleum Corporation. The Inuvialuit company is wholly owned by the Inuvialuit people, one of two main native groups in this corner of the Canadian Arctic. Their participation represents an about-face in native attitudes toward energy development here.
In the late 1970's, tribal chiefs up and down the Mackenzie River Valley spoke out against any gas or oil development and obtained a court-imposed 10-year moratorium that stopped work during the 1980's.
"We had no offices for our chiefs, no telephones," recalled Stephen Kakfwi, a member of the Dene tribe who fought the pipelines 25 years ago. "Of our people, 75 percent were trappers. They weren't involved in the process. We had no understanding of the way that the government of Canada worked. We had no business experience."
In the intervening years, the federal and territorial governments settled virtually all native land claims in the valley, giving tribes legal standing to negotiate rents and royalties. Today, tribes own drilling companies and negotiate exploration royalty payments with multinational companies. Mr. Kakfwi is now premier of the Northwest Territories.
In January, chiefs of all 30 tribes in the region unanimously approved a joint statement "to build a business partnership to maximize ownership and benefit of a Mackenzie Valley pipeline." Their representative, the Aboriginal Working Group, which is seeking a majority stake in a pipeline, has hired the aboriginal banking unit of the Bank of Montreal to secure job guarantees, job training, service contracts and rent payments from pipeline companies. In May, Mr. Kakfwi flew to Calgary, where he pitched the pipeline to the Canadian Energy Pipeline Association.
"What has changed? We are now in control, the majority of the territorial legislature is aboriginal," Mr. Kakfwi said in Yellowknife, the territorial capital. "The nations have got their own land back. They have businesses. They have money."
With energy company engineers studying three possible routes from the Arctic Ocean to pipelines in Alberta, some environmentalists question spending billions of dollars to perpetuate North America's dependency on fossil fuels. They stress that Arctic gas would only feed United States needs for less than one decade.
"Drilling for new oil, or putting in natural gas pipelines, is not how we are going to get energy security," said Melanie Duchin, a member of Greenpeace. "Rather than opening up a fossil fuel frontier, such as trying to drill for these immense natural gas reserves, the money should go for developing renewable energy."
In the Canadian Arctic, however, Greenpeace has little credibility with aboriginal leaders. They say the group's anti-fur campaigns destroyed their traditional hunting and trapping economies.
"My people were Stone Age people 30 years ago," Mr. Kakfwi said. "We have embarked on a journey and we can't turn back. To say, 'You should be a happy little tree hugger, choking rabbits, while everyone else exploits your diamonds and gas' -- no, I don't think so."
In her office a block from Inuvik's stoplight, Nellie J. Cournoyea, chairman of the Inuvialuit Regional Corporation, dismissed environmentalists' objections. "If these people want to be useful, they should start cleaning up the south, stop the pollution that is damaging to the Arctic wildlife and environment," she said, referring to windborne soot and smog.
With northern native support solid and southern consumer support expected to solidify after this winter's home heating bills go out, the race is now on to to see who builds an Arctic gas pipeline first.
On Sept. 14, rival companies presented proposals in Washington for a pipeline through Alaska to the Senate Energy and Natural Resources Committee, which is run by Senator Frank H. Murkowski, a Republican of Alaska. Forrest Hoglund, chairman of the Arctic Resources Company of Houston, said the multibillion-dollar race to build a gas line out of the Arctic was the 21st-century "equivalent of the first railroad to California."
Many Alaskans are eager to see a gas pipeline built because oil production on the North Slope near Prudhoe Bay dropped by half in the 1990's. As royalties dwindle, Alaskans face big tax increases in five years if there is no gas development.
"Alaska is extremely interested in getting the gas out," Mr. Koonce, the Exxon Mobil executive, said after testifying in Washington. "Frankly, we are, too."
Spokesmen for Vice President Al Gore and Gov. George W. Bush of Texas said their respective candidates did not have positions on the pipeline proposals.
Proponents of an Alaskan route said they could start pumping gas south in six years. They said they could work so quickly because permits and an international regulatory framework have existed since the late 1970's to place a gas pipeline parallel to the Trans-Alaska oil pipeline, and then east, along the Alaska-Canada Highway.
At the same time, improved telecommunications, high-speed mechanized welding and the use of rock saws to cut permafrost have combined to make pipeline building faster and cheaper than ever, said Mr. Phelps, the executive at WestCoast, one of the companies planning to build a line.
"The route using the Alaska pipeline and highway makes sense, because you are not dividing the wilderness any further; you are using existing corridors," said Charles M. Clusen, an Alaska policy analyst for the Natural Resources Defense Council, a New York-based environmental group.
Executives at BP Amoco, one of the largest gas owners in Alaska, have said they expect to make a decision on building a gas pipeline by the middle of next year.
But the 1,700-mile Alaska-Yukon route, with a price tag around $8 billion, would leave the Canadian Arctic gas reserves stranded.
To link the two reserves, other companies have proposed a second, $3 billion pipeline up the Mackenzie River Valley and 300 miles west to Prudhoe, running 15 feet below the floor of the Arctic Ocean. In March, four major companies that own gas in the Canadian Arctic plan to release results of a feasibility study for a Mackenzie Valley pipeline.
Although this route could provide cheaper gas to consumers, environmentalists oppose a submerged line, saying it could endanger caribou and polar bear populations. Many Alaskans oppose a submarine pipeline because it would deprive their towns and cities of piped natural gas.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Correction of Data Shows Slightly Higher Inflation
The government said that a software error caused it to slightly underestimate inflation during the first eight months of the year. Revision of the data means somewhat higher payments for recipients of Social Security and other federal benefits.
And while economists said that the error, one-tenth of a percentage point, was not large enough to significantly alter their view of the nation's economy, it was nonetheless in the direction of faster inflation. Page C6. 

Northern Gas Pipelines Proposed
Energy companies, pipeline operators and Indian tribes expect to announce next spring plans to build one, and possibly two, subterranean pipelines to carry Arctic natural gas to North America's pipeline grid. A1.
 
Priceline Drops on Sales Forecast
Shares in the online retailer Priceline.com fell more than 40 percent after the company said its sales in the third quarter would be as much as 11 percent below forecasts. The company attributed the shortfall to slow sales of airline tickets -- its signature product, which still account for 87 percent of its sales. Shares of Priceline fell $7.89, to $10.75. C10.
 
Stocks Slump on Earnings Concern
Stock prices fell as investors continued to be concerned about third-quarter results. The Dow Jones industrial average declined 2.96 points, to 10,628.36. The Nasdaq composite index dropped 32.80 points, or 0.9 percent, to 3,656.30. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.64 of a point, to 1,426.57. C13.
 
Audit Firm's Inspections Criticized
In a report to be issued today, a professor contends that PricewaterhouseCoopers, the world's largest factory-monitoring firm, does a shoddy job and overlooks many safety and wage violations in plants overseas. Pricewaterhouse disputed the conclusions. A12.
 
FleetBoston in Talks to Buy Summit
Summit Bancorp, the largest bank in New Jersey, is in talks to be acquired by FleetBoston Financial, executives close to the discussions said. Analysts estimated the bank could be sold for $7 billion. C6.
 
Sale of Global Crossing Unit Is Seen
In a move that would advance its lead in the business of storage and maintenance facilities for Web sites, Exodus Communications is close to acquiring the Web-hosting division of Global Crossing for about $6.5 billion in stock, people close to the companies said. C10.
 
Interpublic Names a Coordinator
Bruce S. Nelson, an executive with experience in the account, creative and strategic realms of advertising, is being named to a new top post at Interpublic Group. Mr. Nelson will coordinate efforts to manage the increasingly diversified Interpublic units specializing in interactive and direct marketing, public relations, media services, research, programming and consulting as well as traditional advertising. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. C12.
 
Judge Favors Release of Tire Data
A federal judge ruled that the public should have access to extensive information on tire technology that Firestone had to turn over during a lawsuit over a fatal accident. The company said releasing the documents would amount to giving up trade secrets. C2.
 
Air France Sues in Concorde Crash
Air France said that it had sued Continental Airlines because it believed a metal strip from a Continental DC-10 that fell onto a runway led to the crash of its Concorde jet in July. A5.
 
Government Still Has Economic Role
George W. Bush has made government a campaign issue, arguing that as president he would continue to tame Washington. But the oversimplified fear of big government has been damaging. The next president should realize that both business and government have vital roles. Jeff Madrick: Economic Scene. C2.
