HEADLINE: Parents Say Censoring Films Is Their Job, Not Politicians'

BYLINE: By JACQUES STEINBERG

DATELINE: WESTLAND, Mich., Sept. 27

 BODY:
Overwhelmed by the competing proposals in a tight presidential race, Michael Horst grabbed a sheet of paper during the evening news the other night and drew a line down the middle. He wrote "Gore" on the left, "Bush" on the right, and then rated the candidates' positions, drawing a plus sign in the Bush column next to capital punishment and a minus sign next to the Texas governor's education record.
But nowhere on his cheat sheet did Mr. Horst mention an issue that has been the focus of much moralizing on the campaign trail: sex and violence in movies and popular music, and on the Internet. 
 Though Mr. Horst, 39, said he had agonized over how his 11-year-old daughter and 8-year-old son were being affected by the seepage of nudity and mayhem into what they watch and hear, he said he was not looking to politicians for answers. It was, he said, something he could best grapple with on his own, however imperfectly.
"I really don't feel anything they say on this is going to influence me one way or the other," said Mr. Horst, a theft prevention manager for the Rite Aid chain of drugstores. "What decision they make is not going to affect what my children see."
Mr. Horst's sentiments echoed through more than a dozen other interviews with parents of adolescents in Westland, a middle-class suburb of Detroit that, like the rest of Michigan, is considered to be a close call in the presidential race. Neither Republicans nor Democrats dominate here.
Westlanders supported President Clinton, a Democrat, in 1992 and 1996, and Gov. John Engler, a Republican, in 1994 and 1998, and nearly everyone interviewed said they had not decided whom they would vote for in November.
But as they shopped for milk at Kroger, ate ice cream at the Westland Mall, sat in the bleachers at the Westland Sports Arena or even waited for an adjustment at Shunkwiler Chiropractic, most parents agreed that it was their responsibility -- and not that of the president, Congress or even Hollywood -- to filter what reaches their children. The candidates, they suggested, should save their breath for the economy and health care.
Yet all the parents conceded that they sometimes blurred the borders they had drawn between acceptable and unacceptable content.
Jenny Palise, 44, a homemaker whose husband is a tool-and-die maker at the Ford plant in Rawnsonville, said she tried to shield her son Anthony, 12, from nudity and bad language in movies. But the other day, after skimming a review of "The Watcher" to gauge its appropriateness for her son, she decided to take him to see the film, which was rated R.
"I didn't know it was about a serial killer," Ms. Palise said, loading groceries into her customized van. "I thought it was about a stalker."
Though Ms. Palise regretted asking Anthony to come along, he was unfazed. "I fell asleep," he said.
That even the sturdiest dams erected by parents can occasionally spring holes was obvious as Phyllis Blackman shopped for gospel records at Record Town in the Westland Mall with her 16-year-old daughter, La Toya Hamilton. Ms. Blackman said she had a zero-tolerance policy against rap -- unless it was the feel-good rap of the Hollywood star Will Smith.
"She doesn't play the stupid stuff in the house," Ms. Blackman said.
But as La Toya sought out a compact disc by the gospel singer Yolanda Adams, an artist adored by both mother and daughter, La Toya said she also enjoyed the music of the slain rapper Tupac Shakur, which, she admitted, is layered with references to "sex, bad-mouthing of some females, violence and killing."
"I knew she had it," Ms. Blackman said. "It's not something she continues to listen to on a daily basis."
Similarly, Mr. Horst, the Rite Aid manager, said he "blew my cork" when he discovered that his 8-year-old son had seen an Austin Powers video, rated PG-13, at a friend's house.
"Everything with Austin Powers has a sexual connotation, and I got hit with a lot of questions an 8-year-old shouldn't be asking," Mr. Horst said, watching from a bench at the arena as the boy scrimmaged with his ice hockey team, the Westland Eagles.
And yet Mr. Horst said that he did not hesitate to let his son watch the explosion-laden "Terminator" movies starring Arnold Schwarzenegger, and that he took the boy and his 11-year-old sister to see "Titanic."
"That had a moderately explicit scene," Mr. Horst said. "My daughter asked me, 'Dad, what are they doing?' I said, 'He's an artist; he's painting her; they're not actually doing any intimate acts.' "
Mr. Horst, a self-described conservative who is a registered Republican, and Ms. Palise and Ms. Blackman, both Democrats, said they considered themselves strict.
Almost none of those interviewed said they wanted Washington to censor Hollywood, or even Hollywood to censor itself.
The one pledge that they hoped Hollywood would make would be to swear off marketing violent fare directly to teenagers, the subject of a Congressional hearing today, and a Federal Trade Commission report earlier this month.
In speaking against more regulation of Hollywood, the parents repeatedly cited their belief in freedom of speech, and said they often enjoyed material that they would prefer their children not see.
Mark Obsniuk, 48, a mail carrier whose children are 12, 14 and 16, said that any effort by Washington to rein in Hollywood would be "a waste of time." As his youngest child, Jenna, twirled on the ice during a skating lesson, Mr. Obsniuk described how he and his wife, Debbie, an elementary school secretary, enjoyed watching programs like "The Sopranos," the bloody and often sexually charged mob drama on HBO. But he said that they were careful to change the channel whenever their children entered the room.
Having voted for George Bush in 1988 and Bill Clinton in 1992 and 1996, Mr. Obsniuk said he was undecided between Vice President Al Gore and Gov. George W. Bush of Texas. But he said that his assessment of the economy, and of which candidate presented the best chance for continued prosperity, would drive his thinking.
Sitting in the bleachers nearby, Kevin Beahon, 47, a quality control inspector at the Ford national parts depot in Livonia, said he was eminently qualified to screen what reaches the eyes and ears of his sons, 13 and 16, and his daughter, 7.
Mr. Beahon, a likely Bush voter who has barred his two younger children from watching adult-oriented cartoons like "The Simpsons" and "South Park," said he had gotten pretty good at navigating the music Web site Napster. When someone left a note on the front door for his 13-year-old son, Stephen, imploring him to "Get Your Roll On," Mr. Beahon was puzzled. Stephen told his dad that his friend was using the title of a song by a rap act called Big Tymers to urge him to get going to a party.
Mr. Beahon went straight to the Internet to retrieve the song, and after listening to its rapid-fire references to oral sex, male genitalia, adultery and prostitution, he concluded: "This is the crudest thing I've ever heard." Though he admits he cannot control what his son hears outside the home, he told the boy, "I don't want to ever hear that again."
Nonetheless, he added, "I don't think a president should be delving into that."
In a community where many residents work in the skilled trades or middle management at auto plants in surrounding communities, few of those interviewed said they recognized the name of Senator Joseph I. Lieberman of Connecticut, Mr. Gore's running mate.
And fewer still were aware that, among the candidates on the two national tickets, Mr. Lieberman has waged the loudest campaign against what he describes as Hollywood's shrinking mores.
"Tell me the name again, because I'm good with names and I'll be sure to remember it," said one undecided voter and parent, a saleswoman at a furniture store.
Another parent, Tracy Hart, 34, whose presidential vote see-sawed from Michael S. Dukakis, a Democrat, in 1988, to George Bush in 1992 and then to President Clinton in 1996, was one of the few people interviewed here who lamented that the issue of sex and violence in Hollywood was "not one of the major issues on the forefront, in terms of the campaign."
As they loaded their car outside Kroger, her daughter, Hillary, 11, quickly corrected her mother, raising the name of Mr. Lieberman.
"Who's that?" asked Ms. Hart, a single mother who is an administrator at a physical therapy and rehabilitation clinic.
Her daughter, citing an article in the Time magazine spin-off "Time for Kids," responded: "He doesn't think kids should be able to go to R-rated movies and he thinks bad music is bad. He wants to do something about it."
Ms. Hart said, "Well, maybe we know who we're voting for now."

HEADLINE: In America;
Focus on Women

BYLINE: By BOB HERBERT

 BODY:
The most extensive analysis of women's views in this campaign season has shown that as the economy has improved, American women have been taking a much closer look at the quality of their lives, and not liking a great deal that they see.
The new economy has bolstered the incomes of millions of families, but it has also exacerbated the sense of estrangement between the haves and have-nots, and has left many women with the feeling that they have less and less time to spend with their families and properly care for their children. 
 The analysis was drawn from a series of focus groups and a bipartisan national poll conducted for Lifetime Television and the Center for Policy Alternatives. The joint project was called Women's Voices 2000.
The findings suggest it won't be easy for politicians of either party to fashion a "women's message" because the women's vote is far from monolithic, and some of the issues of concern to large numbers of women are so tough and so complex they will require very creative solutions.
For example, two of the major issues of concern to poll respondents were the ever-tightening "time crunch" (more time at work, less time with family) and what was perceived by many respondents as a decline in the nation's moral values. The best way to reverse the decline in moral values, according to the poll, was for parents to spend more time with their children. But the ever-increasing demands of work have made that virtually impossible for most families.
According to the poll, 59 percent of women with children under 6 said they were finding it harder to balance the demands of work and family now than they did four years ago, when the last Women's Voices analysis was conducted. And 30 percent said it was "much harder."
The poll also found:
That a large majority of women -- 72 percent -- would like their health insurance to be independent of their employment. This was not even one of the issues the pollsters had intended to ask about, but it came up repeatedly in the focus groups that preceded the polling. Politicians should take notice.
That 93 percent of African-American women, 91 percent of Latinas, 90 percent of Asian-American women and 87 percent of white women said equal pay and benefits for women should be one of the top policy priorities in the United States.
That retirement is a major worry for many women. Some 85 percent of Democratic women and 77 percent of Republican women want pension benefits that can be transferred from one job to another.
That nearly half of all women -- 48 percent -- feel they will eventually be responsible for the care of a parent or other elderly person.
The polling appeared to show that, more than ever, class distinctions among women are being shaped by whether a woman is college-educated and married. Women who are neither are frequently in a woeful economic state.
"Unmarried women feel so much more economically marginal than married women," said Celinda Lake, who conducted the polling with Linda DiVall, Linda Williams and Anna Rivera.
At the same time, like men, women are finding a big economic divide between those who have a college education and those who do not. And the awareness of these distinctions is increasing, Ms. Lake said.
"Women are now more likely to say they are concerned about the growing divide between the rich and the poor," she said. "The new economy, the new prosperity, has made the class differences more dramatic, because while everyone agrees the economy is better and offers more opportunity, one side of America tends to feel it has benefited much more than the other half of America."
While these concerns do not all lend themselves to government solutions -- and some, like the time crunch, offer difficulties that approach the level of paradox -- there is a noticeable sense among women that government should try to do what it can.
The good times are seen as pretty good, not great. And many of the poll's respondents, with little in the way of savings and troubling memories of the job crunch of a few years ago, look to the future warily.

HEADLINE: New Jersey Man, 82, Is First Known West Nile Fatality in U.S. This Year

BYLINE: By JENNIFER STEINHAUER

 BODY:
New Jersey officials announced yesterday that an 82-year-old Passaic County man died of the West Nile virus two weeks ago, the first person known to die of the disease in the United States this year.
Officials believe it is most likely that the man was bitten in August. He fell ill on Sept. 3, was admitted three days later to Mountainside Hospital in Montclair, and died there on Sept. 14. 
 The diagnosis of West Nile was delayed while blood and tissue studies were conducted. The man had chronic illnesses as well, officials said.
At a news conference, Gov. Christine Todd Whitman and other state officials did not name the man but said he had lived in Little Falls. He was identified as Anthony Opalka by the Herald News of West Paterson, The Associated Press reported last night.
In all, there have been four cases of West Nile in New Jersey this year.
"I don't know if I would call this an epidemic," Dr. Eddy A. Bresnitz, the deputy state commissioner of health, said yesterday at the news conference in Hackensack. "I think what we do have is an outbreak."
A 72-year-old woman from Bayonne also contracted the disease in the last few months, but she is already home recovering, officials said. The other human cases previously confirmed in New Jersey involved a 43-year-old man from Jersey City and a 54-year-old man who had homes in both Cliffside Park and Brooklyn, N.Y. Both men were hospitalized for treatment and are recovering.
Blood tests were performed on 18 other people in the state whose illnesses resembled West Nile, but the results are still pending.
In New York City, West Nile has been diagnosed this year in 13 people, including a 73-year-old woman from Queens who the city announced yesterday was hospitalized but recovering. Among the other cases, eight were treated and released and four remain hospitalized.
Scores of birds and other animals have contracted the disease this year, so many that many health care officials have been surprised that more human cases did not emerge. Explanations of why that could be range from crediting state prevention campaigns to musing about the weather: Did cold air keep people inside? Did the wet summer keep birds around so mosquitoes were drawn to them instead of to humans?
While state officials never like to have any cases in their areas, the New York region has fared far better than Israel, where 14 people have died of West Nile this year.
Last year, West Nile killed 7 people in the New York region and severely sickened 55 others.
Governor Whitman said yesterday that New Jersey would re-evaluate its West Nile control plan, which has included spraying insecticide to kill adult mosquitoes in every county. In Bergen County tomorrow, the state will spray between 3 and 6 a.m. in the Carlstadt, Wood-Ridge, Franklin Lakes and Mahwah areas.
"Obviously, this is a disease that's going to be with us for some time," Mrs. Whitman said. "And I intend after this season is over to ask the relevant cabinet members, including the secretary of commerce and economic growth, the secretary of agriculture, the commissioner of health and senior services, to get together and to reassess everything that we have done."
Insecticides have been sprayed this year in all five New York City boroughs and in Westchester and Rockland Counties, on Long Island and in Connecticut and New Jersey, though not always to the satisfaction of New York City officials. Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani had complained earlier this year that northern New Jersey and Nassau County were endangering public health by not spraying aggressively enough.

HEADLINE: Making It in This New Economy

 BODY:

To the Editor:
As an underpaid holder of a Ph.D. in philosophy, I empathize with Ann L. Berrios's discontent ("No Wealth of Opportunity," Op-Ed, Sept. 25). Our common plight points up the downside of America's free-market economy. 
 Those who are either unable or unwilling to enhance a company's bottom line, even if highly educated, are bound to find themselves less affluent than those with marketable skills.
The academic institutions at which someone with a liberal arts education used to be able to earn a good living are now themselves driven by the profit motive, hiring more and more part-time teachers and financially rewarding only those "scholars" whose work has market value. Is it any wonder, then, that intellectuals tend to favor socialism?
 
ROBERT F. ALLEN
Redford, Mich., Sept. 25, 2000

HEADLINE: Making It in This New Economy

 BODY:

To the Editor:
Re "No Wealth of Opportunity," by Ann L. Berrios (Op-Ed, Sept. 25): 
 I can commiserate with Ms. Berrios blow by blow. I live in Evanston, on the exclusive North Shore of Lake Michigan. My husband and I own a portion of a modest four-room, two-bedroom condo (the bank owns the rest, of course). Because of the decision we made that I stay home with our small children, we do not have any of the "extras" that our children's schoolmates seem to take for granted.
My husband and I are generally happy with our life, and we realize that our standard of living is high compared with the worldwide average. But now that our children are becoming more aware of peer and societal pressure and values, we are pressured, and more and more squeezed into the North Shore mold. We, too, sometimes ask, If the wealth of opportunity is so evident to everyone else, why aren't we feeling it?
 
ELIZABETH RECHT JONES
Evanston, Ill., Sept. 25, 2000

NEW YORK
 
MANHATTAN: SCOOTER SAFETY PROPOSAL
A City Council bill introduced yesterday would require all children under 14 to wear helmets while using scooters in New York City. The bill was drafted in response to the recent death of a 6-year-old New Jersey boy struck by a car while using a scooter. (Proposals have been made in New Jersey for a similar law.) Violators would pay a $50 fine. The bill would also require the distribution of pamphlets on scooter safety. Thomas J. Lueck (NYT) 

COMMACK: STATE TO BE SUED IN KILLINGS
The family of a victim of Michael J. Swango, left, the former doctor who pleaded guilty to killing three Long Island men while at the Veterans Affairs Medical Center in Northport, has taken the first step toward suing New York State. This month, Mr. Swango said he killed Thomas Sammarco, 73; George Siano, 60; and Aldo Serini, 62, while a resident at the medical center. Mr. Siano's family filed a notice of claim against New York, and the families of the other victims said they intended to join the suit. All three families plan to sue Ohio as well, for what they charge was a lax investigation of Mr. Swango when he was under suspicion of killing two patients in 1984 as an intern at Ohio State University Hospital. Tina Kelley (NYT)
 
MANHATTAN: McCALL BACKS DEBT PLAN
State Comptroller H. Carl McCall said yesterday that he had decided to ask voters to support a $3.8 billion transportation borrowing act, which will be the largest sale of state bonds in American history if it is approved in November. Mr. McCall, a critic of the state's reliance on borrowing, said the projects the bond act would help finance, including engineering work for the Second Avenue subway line, were needed to ensure that New York remained "economically competitive." But he said he was less than enthusiastic because New York was continuing to borrow "with no concern for our mounting debt load." Randy Kennedy (NYT)1
 
MANHATTAN: TAXI PASSENGER KILLED
A taxi passenger was killed around 12:30 a.m. yesterday when a car struck the cab on the Upper East Side, the police said. The passenger, Paul Sheridan, 29, died at the scene. Michael Glaubman, 42, was charged with manslaughter and operating a motor vehicle while intoxicated. The police said Mr. Glaubman's car was headed north on Madison Avenue and the cab, driven by Joseph Brecevs, 37, was headed east on 82nd Street. Mr. Brecevs was in stable condition at New York Weill Cornell Medical Center. Elissa Gootman (NYT)
 
THE BRONX: WOMAN ATTACKED
A 24-year-old woman was raped at gunpoint yesterday morning in Bronx Park, near the New York Botanical Garden, by a man who was later arrested, the police said. The woman was in the sliver of park that lies east of the Bronx River Parkway at 9:30 a.m. when the man, Miguel Camacho, 24, attacked her, the authorities said. Mr. Camacho, of Morris Avenue in the Bronx, was arrested last night after a daylong search. He was charged with rape and weapons possession, said Detective John Giammarino, a police spokesman. Andy Newman (NYT)
 
MANHATTAN: GARAGE LACKED BARRIERS
There were no protective concrete wheel blocks at the Virginia Beach, Va., parking deck where four New York nurses were killed when their car crashed through a rooftop cinderblock wall and fell 41 feet. The Sandcastle Resort Motel garage was built in 1985, and officials were investigating whether codes that applied to the building required wheel guards. (AP)

The Suffolk County police said yesterday that they had arrested 64 men over three weeks and charged them with public lewdness. Most of the men had been engaging in sexual activities at the Vietnam Memorial here. Several veterans and other visitors to the park, on County Road 83, complained to the police, who conducted an undercover operation from Sept. 2 to 22. Those arrested included a New York City schoolteacher and an assistant football coach at Bellport High School, the police said.

BROOKLYN: CONEY ISLAND GANG ARRESTS
The authorities announced yesterday that 14 members of a Coney Island street gang had been arrested on charges of attempted murder, kidnapping, assault, robbery and drug dealing in and around the Marlboro Houses, a public housing project in southern Brooklyn. Known as "the Cream Team," the gang name is an acronym for Cash Rules Everything Around Me. Prosecutors said the gang was particularly violent, typically beating their victims, robbing them and stripping them of their clothes. Alan Feuer (NYT)
 
NEW JERSEY
 
PARSIPPANY: FEDERAL CUTOFF THREATENED
The federal government has threatened to cut off funds to New Jersey for the operation of Greystone Psychiatric Hospital by Oct. 12 because of what the government calls inadequate staffing. The funds, which are reimbursements to the state for the treatment of Medicare and Medicaid patients and the uninsured, totaled $42 million last year, or much of the hospital's $50 million budget. The State Department of Human Services, which runs Greystone, said it had hired 99 new nurses, technicians and other staff members, and had moved 43 of Greystone's 615 patients. Steve Strunsky (NYT)

Brian C. Peterson, who served 18 months of a two-year prison sentence after he and his girlfriend pleaded guilty to negligent manslaughter in the death of their newborn son in 1996 in Delaware, may be sent back to jail after being arrested Tuesday night at Shea Stadium. As he and a friend, Raymond Maniaci, watched a Mets game, according to the police, Mr. Maniaci tossed a partly filled bottle at John Rocker, the outspoken relief pitcher for the Atlanta Braves. As officers led Mr. Maniaci away, Mr. Peterson interfered, the police said, yelling, swearing and flailing his arms. Probation officials in Delaware, where the infant's death occurred, said that Mr. Peterson's future would depend on the outcome of the new case. Robert Hanley (NYT)

NORWICH: UKRAINE SHIP FINDS HOME
Local officials are preparing to welcome the Ukrainian tall ship Bat'kivshchyna as it docks for the winter. The ship's captain, Dmytro Biriukovich, met with officials on Tuesday to cement a deal that will give the boat a free berth and utilities until it departs for tall ship festivities in the Great Lakes next spring. The ship, which persevered through numerous adversities on the way to the OpSail 2000 celebration in the summer, is in New London awaiting dry-dock space for repairs. David M. Herszenhorn (NYT)

HEADLINE: ANTIQUES;
A Devotion To Japanese Art Is Requited

BYLINE: By Suzanne Charle

 BODY:
Rene Scholten came to love Japanese art through a circuitous route. On a trip to Japan while working as a software consultant, Mr. Scholten, who is Dutch, purchased an inexpensive woodblock print. Then he saw a fine 1929 print by Torii Kotondo of a beautiful woman applying powder. Completely seduced, he bought it.
When an art dealer friend said he wanted to quit, Mr. Scholten took over his shop in The Hague. Business expanded, and so did the gallery; Mr. Scholten left his high-tech life and became a partner in Hotei Publishing, expanding its list of books on Japanese art. 
 His most recent venture is Scholten Japanese Art in Manhattan, which he opened two weeks ago with the help of two respected experts, Katherine Martin and Rosemary Bandini. Its first show focuses on the art of the Edo period in Japan (1603 to 1867). Intricate netsuke, the fanciful toggles that delighted the Japanese gentry for whom they were carved, are suspended in illuminated plastic cubes, as are shimmering lacquer boxes, better suited to holding admiration and wonder than more substantial stuff. On the walls are prints by some of the great masters: Suzuki Harunobu, Katsukawa Shuncho, Kitagawa Utamaro and Utagawa Hiroshige. Mr. Scholten spent $10 million to convert three floors of the town house at 63 East 66th Street, and it shows.
Woodblock prints known as ukiyo-e have been the portal into Japanese art for many Westerners, including Monet, whose collection can be seen at his home-museum in Giverny, and van Gogh, who copied two of Hiroshige's prints, most famously "Ohashi Bridge." The fragile colored prints were also favorites of Frank Lloyd Wright; an exhibition of his collection and his architecture in Japan will be at the Japan Society in March. The allure has not faded. A recent woodblock show at the Asian Art Museum in San Francisco drew more than 80,000 visitors. "They have an immense following," said its director, Emily Sano.
"Not everyone can collect Monet, but they can collect prints," said Ms. Martin, the Scholten gallery's managing director. "Here are artists at a turning point. They are creating totally new abstract images, taking proportion and perspective and color and playing with them."
The prints lead the viewer into the floating world ukiyo, a Buddhist concept for the sad and transitory nature of the material world. Edo artists transformed the idea into the transitory world of pleasure, a demimonde of geisha and parties, actors and theater.
The high-flying Edo era was a boom period. Like present-day New York, Edo (now Tokyo) was the center of commerce and trade. Merchants flush with money from a new economy sought to spend it on pleasure and more pleasure. Ukiyo-e put the spotlight on this self-indulgent, glamorous world, immortalizing the day's most sought-after courtesans, the season's most popular Kabuki actors. "The beauties were Edo's supermodels, the actors its rock stars," Ms. Martin said. "The prints were the Edo period's color TV."
The prints were major productions. Publishers hired artists who drew images that were then carved into wood blocks (sometimes a dozen or more for one print) by a master engraver. The blocks were given to a printer who worked under the publisher and the artist.
Although publicly scorned by the higher caste samurai, the burgeoning merchant class collected the prints as passionately as later generations would collect posters and baseball cards. Some artists became almost as famous as their subjects. Utamaro, who worked at the end of the 18th century, was the Richard Avedon of Edo. Known for his innovative close-ups of the beauties of the day, he at once idealized them and captured, through simple elegant lines, their emotions and personalities. (His "Mirror of Courtesans of the Green Houses" is for sale at Scholten for $25,000.)
"There were thousands of images and varying levels of prints, ranging from cheap ephemera to mica-ground prints for the wealthy," said Sebastian Izzard, a dealer who has just opened a new space at 42 East 76th Street, Manhattan. Many were lost to fire or ruined by water. In the Meiji period, when the Japanese turned their eyes to the West, some were actually used for packing."We have no real idea of how many have survived," said Mr. Izzard, who worked at Christie's for 17 years. "What we do know is that there are many 'sleeping prints' stored away. Whenever a fine 'unique' print is sold for a high price at auction, another surfaces."
One of Scholten's aims is to make the gallery a focus for Japanese art: a library on the third floor will be open to students, scholars and collectors, and Ms. Martin plans a series of lectures next year. (Information and appointments: 212-585-0474.) "We want to develop collectors," Mr. Scholten said. "And you can only really do that through a gallery."
George Mann, a collector from Chicago, agreed: "It's one thing to see a print on a wall at a museum, and quite another to hold it and examine it. An even larger barrier is psychological. You can't own a museum print. A print in a gallery is much more seductive."
The novice has much to learn. A print of the famed Ohashi bridge by Hiroshige can sell for anywhere from several hundred dollars to tens of thousands. In a recent auction at Christie's an early impression went for $52,875; at Scholten, another fine impression costs $75,000. At issue is the quality and condition of the print. Often an early edition will command the highest price: the artist was there supervising the printer, making sure the proper colors were used. In later editions, when the artist was off creating new images, a printer might take shortcuts, using less expensive pigments.
But the first impression does not always command the highest price. Sometimes a coterie of patrons commissioned a deluxe edition printed on special paper with special pigments. Or the publisher or artist decided to edit the print to make it more salable or attractive. The first prints of Hiroshige's "Ohashi," for example, are sometimes known as "Two Boats"; in later editions two boats in the background were etched out to make the print more aesthetically appealing. Popular works could be printed and reprinted until the block became so worn that the impressions were virtually unreadable. The title of Hiroshige's series "100 Views of Edo" was actually changed to "100 Views of Tokyo." "The name of the city changed in 1868, 10 years after the print was made," Mr. Izzard said. "The condition of the blocks had to be pretty rough."
The prints are as ephemeral as their subjects, soon fading in direct light. Improperly stored, they become food for worms and insects. Today most serious collectors keep prints in acid-free paper in boxes, taking them out to look at or to hang on a wall, for only a brief time, behind protective glass.
Prices for ukiyo-e are driven by fashion, just as they were in the Edo period. Although a popular image in excellent condition would be out of reach for most (an actor print by Sharaku just sold at Christie's for $280,000), there are still many prints in fine condition available for as little as $3,000 or $4,000. "You might not be able to afford a fine print of 'Ohashi,' " Ms. Martin said. "But there are excellent prints from the same series that you could buy for $3,000. The market, today as in the past, is driven by changing tastes."

HEADLINE: SYDNEY 2000: Sports of The Times;
Two 'Enemies' Who Make Music By Playing Each Other So Hard

BYLINE: By GEORGE VECSEY

DATELINE: SYDNEY, Australia

 BODY:
BACK in Norway, they heard about the victory tour, the commercials, the sponsorships, when the United States won the Women's World Cup in 1999.
"The United States has more money," said Silje Joergensen, who plays defense for Norway. "They are rich." 
 The Norwegian players did not sulk about losing to the Americans as they went back to their jobs, playing for local clubs for a few extra kroner. The countries are different. The cultures are different. The economies are different.
And besides, the Norwegians have been known to beat the United States more than occasionally -- and even ticked off the Yanks with a choreographed choo-choo train celebration after winning in their semifinal of the 1995 World Cup in Sweden.
"Archenemies" is the word Tiffeny Milbrett once used to describe the Norwegians, although last night the effervescent Milbrett was saying, "Gosh, you've got to give them credit."

 
 Hearing word of the way corporate and hometown America fell for the women last summer, the Norwegians could only remind themselves that their time would come around again.
It arrived in the final of the Summer Games last night when Norway outlasted the United States, 3-2, in sudden death. Norway is the only nation to hold an advantage over the Americans, 15 victories to 13 losses with 2 ties.
"Money is not everything," Joergensen said. "We showed that tonight."
The two old rivals, who have been storing up bruises and cuts, grudges and resentments, in the decade and a half of this sport in the Olympics, staged a classic game, as good as sport gets. The United States tied the game in the dying seconds of regulation time, and then Norway clutched the victory in the 12th minute of overtime.
The leaping header by the 5-foot-2-inch Milbrett was as stunning as Mookie Wilson's eccentric at-bat in the 1986 World Series. And the stunning overtime goal was as dramatic as Bill Mazeroski's home run that ended the seventh game of the 1960 World Series -- and more controversial because it involved a potential hand ball by a Norwegian forward.
Fans will debate how Hege Riise's perfect lofting pass was touched by Joy Fawcett and then bounced onto the chest/shoulder/upper arm of Norway's Dagny Mellgren, who lashed a grass-cutter under the fingers of Siri Mullinix.
The referee, Sonia Denoncourt of Canada, showed common sense in deciding that Mellgren had not blatantly positioned her upper arm to control the ball.
Would the call have gone that way in the jingoistic confines of the Rose Bowl in 1999? The Yanks got the benefit of the doubt when goalkeeper Briana Scurry leaped forward -- in violation of the rule -- to smother a Chinese penalty kick. Last night, Norway won on neutral turf, with many Norwegians sprouting Viking horns and painting their faces red.
These Americans and the Norwegians have become the Fraziers and the Alis of women's sport. When they become grumpy old women, they will still remember the mutual testiness.
In 1991 the Yanks beat Norway, 2-1, in China to win the first World Cup. In 1995 the Yanks were offended when the Norwegians crawled around the field clutching the ankles of the players in front of them. And the Norwegians were not thrilled when the Yanks beat them, 2-1, in overtime in the semifinals of the 1996 Summer Games in Athens, Ga. But they usually come back.
The two nations have produced the greatest offensive and defensive players in the history of the sport, Michelle Akers and Linda Medalen, the latter a detective who treated the Americans like suspected perps.
Without them, the legend grows. Gro Espeseth, Norway's defender, who missed the 1999 World Cup because of knee surgery, was back last night, scoring on a header off the desperate flying foot of Shannon MacMillan. That goal gave the Norwegians a reprieve at 1-1.
"I think we wanted it more," said Marianne Pettersen of Norway.
Milbrett conceded that the Yanks have a problem when they play Norway.
"With every other team, we play the way we want, but they know how to beat us," Milbrett said. "We just played the best game of the tournament, and we're going home with a silver medal."
The Americans are also going home to a professional league that begins next April. And, yes, the Norwegians have heard about the chunk of change being spent.
"I play soccer because I love it," said Joergensen, who is attending college to learn to teach retarded children. "I would love to play soccer for money. I would go to the United States to play in that league."
She would be welcome. And she would be gracious enough to not mention the status of the Norwegian-American rivalry, at least not very often.

HEADLINE: Residential Real Estate;
A Community Created By a Builder's Tenacity

BYLINE: By NADINE BROZAN

 BODY:
John Ciardullo is planning to build himself a new home on Rock Shelter Road in the northern Westchester hamlet of Waccabuc, and why not? He carved the road itself out of rocky wooded terrain, designed, engineered and built all the 7 houses on it, and will do the 14 yet to come. 
 "I'm creating a community," he said during a recent visit, "so why not live in it?"
The community, set amid the rolling countryside, horse trails and estates of Waccabuc, an affluent hamlet in the town of Lewisboro, might have never been built had it not been for the tenacity of Mr. Ciardullo, whose projects in the New York City area have included public schools and parks, luxury high-rise apartments, low-income housing in the South Bronx and a refurbishing of the Algonquin Hotel. The story of his development and his singlemindedness shows the difficulties that developers can face in some areas even with comparatively small projects.
In 1985, Mr. Ciardullo bought a 100-acre parcel of land for $900,000 and an adjoining 7-acre lot for $120,000, and then spent the next 11 years filing, revising and refiling plans to prove that he would meet the requirements of the State Environmental Quality Review Act and other state and local regulations.
Among the agencies with jurisdiction over his project were the Lewisboro Planning and Town Boards; the Westchester County Health Department; the New York City Department of Environmental Protection, because the property is part of the city's watershed; and the State Department of Environmental Conservation. The process was complicated because his acreage contained wetlands.
"I had to submit documents on the effect of the development on the topography, on the vegetation, on the wetlands, on endangered wildlife," Mr. Ciardullo said. "We had to do an archaeological study to see if anyone had ever lived there before, and we found a cave on the property that dates back 4,000 years -- which gave us the idea for the name of the street. I also had to put in a salamander crossing -- and salamanders aren't even endangered."
Though getting approval for a subdivision is usually a lengthy procedure, "the amount of time it took is extraordinary," said Adam Wekstein, a land-use lawyer who represented Mr. Ciardullo during the last three years of the process.
Once the hurdles were cleared, and Mr. Ciardullo had sunk $5 million more into the venture, he began to put in the road and build a model house.
Though he intended to offer only custom homes tailored to the individual preferences of the buyers, he has built several houses on speculation as models and then sold them. "People have no imagination," Mr. Ciardullo said, "no ability to visualize what it will look like unless they can see something."
The first spec house was sold two years ago for $250,000 less than it had cost him to put up, he said. "We gave it away for $1.3 million because though people were interested, no one wanted to be the pioneer in a new subdivision," he said. "People didn't know if we would survive. And no one knew the economy was about to boom."
Today, with six more houses completed and sold, two under construction and three on the drawing board, he is not giving anything away. Prices, which are agreed upon before any job begins, have risen with each house, to $2.4 million in the latest agreement.
"The buyers, generally people in their 20's and 30's, have been mostly people in the financial industries," he said. "Doctors can't afford these houses anymore."
Three of the buyers have been content to buy spec houses rather than ones in which they had any input.
"My husband wanted a new house, I wanted something older and this made us both happy," said Amanda Brown, who now lives in one of the houses that was built on speculation. "And," she added, "it had so many things we wanted we didn't feel the need to personalize."
Though he considers himself a modernist, Mr. Ciardullo has limited the Rock Shelter Road houses, replete with such details as Palladian windows, elaborate door casings and porticos, to classic Colonial style (albeit with the most current luxury appliances). "Modern houses don't sell today," he said. "Everyone wants Colonial so we give them an exact replica with all the modern accouterments."
Rambling in size from 6,700 to 7,400 square feet, they all combine formality (in living and dining rooms) with informality (large kitchens that flow into family rooms) and include vaulted ceilings, colonnades, expensive tile work and thick moldings. One of the spec houses had closets that were so large that the buyers turned two of them into children's bedrooms.
Set on four-acre lots, the houses are built into terrain that is sloping, densely wooded and clearly a construction challenge. "I like to figure out how to enhance land, not destroy it," Mr. Ciardullo said. He has put in stone walls along all the road and in other places within the property and incorporated the numerous outcroppings into the landscaping. For one of the houses under construction, he said, he had to blast 3,700 cubic yards of rock.
Because he is a one-man band, doing everything from laying out septic systems to helping clients decide on the depth of their Jacuzzis, Mr. Ciardullo gets to know his clients well. And when the job is done, he said, "you get me as a friend for all time."

HEADLINE: Morgan Stanley Creates Fund Tied to Internet Index

BYLINE: By DANNY HAKIM

 BODY:
Beginning today, investors will be able to tie their money to picks made in part by one of the most influential Wall Street analysts following the Internet.
Morgan Stanley Dean Witter is introducing an investment fund that will track the Morgan Stanley Internet index, whose 29 stocks are chosen by Mary Meeker and other analysts at Morgan Stanley. 
 Ms. Meeker has been called Queen of the Net by Barron's and was the subject of a long profile in The New Yorker last year. Her firm was the lead underwriter for such pioneering initial public offerings as Netscape.
Now Morgan Stanley is using Ms. Meeker to promote its new Internet fund -- she is scheduled to appear at a news conference today promoting the product, which will be the first investment fund for retail investors that has a tie to her research.
This year, of course, being Queen of the Net is not the most exalted perch. Internet stocks took a drubbing in April and have yet to recover. Since its introduction on March 24, the Morgan Internet index has fallen 48.4 percent. Ms. Meeker declined to comment, and Morgan officials refused to speak on the record. But they say that the index is a better gauge of the Internet industry compared with the dozens of other Internet indexes that have preceded them.
Ironically, as the market for Internet stocks has slumped this year, Internet stock indexes continue to proliferate, as do investment funds created to buy only Internet stocks. Because of regulatory bureaucracy and the investment industry's sometimes sluggish pace, new funds created to invest in hot market trends often arrive just in time to find that the hot investment trend has grown ice cold.
Two years ago, three mutual funds invested exclusively in Internet stocks. Today, there are more than 40 of various types.
"Is there room for more? My inclination is probably not," said Donald Cassidy, senior research analyst at the fund tracking firm Lipper. "The Morgan Stanley name will help. The fact that she'd talk it up will help. But, gee, this isn't the first one."
The StreetTracks Morgan Stanley Internet Index Fund, as the new fund is called, is an exchange-traded fund, a mutual-fund-like product that trades like a stock. The index is run in conjunction with the American Stock Exchange, which uses Morgan Stanley's research and its lists of recommended stocks to compile and alter the index, in which each stock is rebalanced with an equal weighting every quarter. The fund will be run by State Street Global Advisers, an investment firm in Boston that is introducing six other exchange-traded funds today. The Internet fund will have a 0.50 percent expense ratio -- the annual fee deducted in daily increments from mutual and exchange-traded funds.
"There might be interest because it's Mary Meeker, but there's no shortage of Internet investing options," said James Atkinson, president of an Internet investment company called Maxfunds.com. "This is a very late entrant into this field."
Mr. Atkinson helped create the first Internet index fund last July, when he was the top domestic mutual fund executive at Investec Asset Management, based in London. Investec's fund tracks an index maintained by Internet.com, a Web publishing company. Since then, funds have been introduced that track Internet indexes created by Dow Jones; Goldman, Sachs; and several firms with less familiar names.
"It's interesting how new funds are created," Mr. Cassidy said. "Somebody makes a marketing decision that's usually made in a favorable time. Then you take two, three, four months, whatever the process is in the particular house, before it finally trades. By then the market might be quite different."
Compared with last year, the market is quite different. The average Internet mutual fund has fallen 22.9 percent this year, according to the fund tracking firm Morningstar. Some have lost 40 percent to 50 percent. The optimist would call it a buying opportunity; the pessimist would call it a dark hole to jump into.
Diane Garnick, a Merrill Lynch analyst who follows exchange-traded funds, said the Morgan fund would be a breed apart from existing Internet funds, a hybrid falling in between traditional index funds that try to represent the market or different sectors, and actively managed mutual funds, where stock pickers try to beat the market.
"You could say it is a giant leap forward toward actively managed E.T.F.'s," Ms. Garnick said. Because of the regulatory restrictions of a fund that trades throughout the day like a stock, all exchange-traded funds are index funds. Most prominent stock indexes, like those run by Standard & Poor's or Dow Jones, are compiled by a committee of economists trying to represent a market sector. Ms. Garnick thinks Ms. Meeker and the other Morgan analysts would add a different kind of selectivity to the process.
"Analysts know better the prospects of the companies," she said, and would go beyond merely trying to represent the Net industry and try to pick "the best companies."

HEADLINE: Mexican Chief to Promote Trade in Europe

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: MEXICO CITY, Sept. 28

 BODY:
Bolstering his image as Mexico's free trade salesman, President-elect Vicente Fox Quesada promised today to carry his economic vision to Europe, where, he said, a new accord has the potential to be another Nafta.
Mr. Fox, who is to leave on Saturday for a five-nation tour of the European Union, called the free-trade agreement signed in July with the European Union a huge opportunity. The weeklong trip will take Mr. Fox to France, England, Spain, Germany and Belgium. 
 Mr. Fox compared the European accord to the adoption of Nafta in 1994, which he said has created thousands of jobs and generated $10 billion in direct foreign investment in Mexico. "With these figures," he said, "we can only imagine what we can do with Europe. Perhaps, it's not even too much to think that this could lower the trade balance between European countries and Mexico and become something as big and powerful as the North American Free Trade Agreement."
The new pact could make Mexico less dependent on the United States, the destination for 88 percent of its exports last year. Trade between Mexico and the United States has soared under Nafta, to $225.9 billion last year from $88.1 billion in 1993.
Mr. Fox said he would also present proposals to resolve the conflict with Zapatista rebels in southern Mexico, another priority for European leaders.

HEADLINE: THE 2000 CAMPAIGN: THE ENERGY ISSUE;
Supply vs. Demand Ideas Separate Gore and Bush

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 28

 BODY:
With oil prices up, consumers agitated and the winter heating season looming, Vice President Al Gore and Gov. George W. Bush are going at it on energy policy, seeking to draw sharp distinctions over an issue on which both candidates have political vulnerabilities.
It is a topic that the two parties see as touching consumers every time they fill up at the pump or adjust their thermostats, and one that is inextricably linked to broader environmental, foreign policy and economic issues. 
 Mr. Gore forced the topic back onto the campaign agenda last week with his call, acceded to by President Clinton, for the government to release oil from the Strategic Petroleum Reserve to head off shortages of home heating oil -- an action that both the president and the vice president had opposed earlier in the year. Mr. Bush, who criticized the decision as a political ploy to drive down prices just ahead of Election Day, is scheduled to discuss energy policy in a speech on Friday.
The most basic question facing the country on energy is how to keep supply and demand in line. Mr. Bush, like most Republicans, emphasized finding new oil and ensuring a flow from other oil-producing nations. Mr. Gore, like many Democrats, focuses on trying to use less energy, or at least on using existing energy supplies as efficiently as possible.
"In very simple terms, the Republicans would approach an energy problem from the supply side: 'What can we do to increase supply?' " said Robert E. Ebel, director of the energy program at the Center for Strategic and International Studies. "The Democrats would say: "What can we do to make better use of what we've got?' "
There are a number of other clearly defined differences between them. Mr. Gore sees global warming as a serious threat, and strongly backs an international agreement reached three years ago in Kyoto, Japan, which calls for substantial reductions in emissions of industrial gases from burning coal, oil, wood and natural gas. Mr. Bush acknowledges that global warming is a potential problem, but says the Kyoto agreement would be too costly to the United States and would not ensure that developing countries do their part.
Mr. Bush, who worked for years in the oil industry and whose running mate, Dick Cheney, ran an oil field services company, supports opening some environmentally sensitive areas, including the Arctic National Wildlife Refuge in Alaska, to oil exploration and production. Mr. Gore opposes such steps.
Mr. Gore, who has long said he is willing to pay a political price for his environmental principles, has suggested that the United States needs to take drastic steps, like eliminating the internal combustion engine, a goal that is widely mocked by Republicans.
In an effort to encourage conservation, Mr. Gore has also supported higher taxes on energy consumption, including a broad-based energy tax proposed by the Clinton administration in 1993 but never adopted, and a 4.3-cents-a-gallon increase in the federal gasoline tax, which became law in 1993 over the objection of every Republican in Congress.
In some ways, the two candidates are not as far apart as they seem. For all his focus on reducing demand, Mr. Gore has supported limited expansion of domestic exploration and production, including in the Gulf of Mexico and on federal land. Mr. Bush backs policies that promote conservation and energy efficiency.
Although Mr. Gore would rely more heavily on a government role than Mr. Bush, both candidates see the market as the most powerful force for addressing energy problems. Both generally support the deregulation of the electric power industry to allow power producers to compete in serving consumers.
In their own way, both of them have had to manage balancing acts.
Mr. Gore's general philosophy, and some of the specific positions he has taken over the years, are based in part on the idea that higher oil prices could be a good thing in holding down consumption and encouraging conservation and use of alternative energy sources.
Yet Mr. Gore has also sought to portray himself as defender of the consumer against higher gasoline and heating costs. During the summer, he called for a federal investigation into whether oil companies were unfairly jacking up gasoline prices. And with winter looming and crude oil prices rising, he tried to pre-empt a possible spike in home heating oil prices with his call last week for release of supplies from the reserve.
"Gore is right that there is vast potential for alternative technologies and fuels," said Pietro S. Nivola, a senior fellow at the Brookings Institution, a liberal-leaning research organization. "But every time we try to manipulate or depress the price of oil, we put off the day when some of these innovations come online."
Mr. Bush has said the surge in oil prices this year has resulted from what he says is the Clinton-Gore administration's ineptitude in dealing with the oil-producing nations of the Middle East and on its failure to promote new oil production and modernization of power plants, pipelines and refineries at home.
But his own close ties to the oil industry and problems with air quality in Texas have sometimes left him on the defensive, and his choice of Mr. Cheney as a running mate only exacerbated the problem. And while Mr. Bush's advisers say his criticism of Mr. Gore for tapping the nation's oil reserves makes the vice president look like a crass panderer, it is unclear whether voters will appreciate that kind of principle more than immediate action to help them deal with surging prices.
Lawrence B. Lindsey, Mr. Bush's chief economic adviser, told reporters this week that energy was the "best issue" for the Texas governor in the campaign right now.
Mr. Lindsey said Mr. Bush's policy prescriptions would center on promoting more domestic exploration and production of oil, speeding the process of granting permits for new refineries, power plants and pipelines, more active diplomatic efforts to persuade oil producing nations to keep oil prices stable and at reasonable levels and a greater emphasis on working with Western Hemisphere oil producers, especially Mexico.
Mr. Bush would also work with industry to promote conservation and efficiency through innovations like time-of-day pricing, under which energy users would pay more at peak hours of demand, Mr. Lindsey said.
"Eight years of neglect cannot be made up overnight," Mr. Lindsey said. "It's going to take a lot of years of work on our part to rectify the problems that have accumulated."
In a series of speeches this year, Mr. Gore set out a list of energy initiatives. He relies heavily on tax credits -- for the purchase of fuel-efficient vehicles and homes, use of low-polluting fuels and solar power, investment by business in energy efficient equipment and for production of electricity from renewable and alternative fuel sources.
But the core of his message is that developing efficiency-enhancing products and cleaner sources of energy is not an economic burden but an opportunity that can create jobs and profits for the United States.
His plan, Mr. Gore said at a campaign stop in Pennsylvania last week, would "create millions of new jobs by getting out in front of the market for these new boilers and new furnaces and new cars and trucks and buses and revamped power plants and new technologies that help us to conserve more energy."
What neither candidate is saying explicitly is that the best policy in the long run, according to some analysts, may be to promote prices that are substantially below the nearly $38 a barrel level that crude oil hit briefly last week before declining to around $30 today, but well above the $12 level that made gasoline so cheap two years ago.
Mr. Clinton acknowledged as much this week. He told reporters on Wednesday that the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries' target range of $22 to $28 a barrel was "reasonable," but that he did not want to see prices "go back down to $13 or $12 or $10 again because that was also disruptive."

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: COMMODITIES;
OPEC's Unity Is Undercut By the Saudis

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER

DATELINE: CARACAS, Venezuela, Sept. 28

 BODY:
A display of unity by OPEC heads of state at the conclusion of their summit meeting today was undercut by the leading oil producer Saudi Arabia, which caused a sell-off in the petroleum market with a unilateral pledge to pump more crude to weaken prices that have recently hit 10-year highs.
The price of light crude oil, the benchmark grade in the United States, tumbled nearly 4 percent, to $30.34 a barrel and is now nearly 20 percent below the peak of $37.80 a barrel reached last week. 
 "The kingdom is willing and ready to offer the amount necessary to stabilize the world oil market," the Saudi crown prince, Abdullah bin Abdulaziz al-Saud, declared to other members of the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries at their meeting here. "We are worried today by the increase in oil prices, which if continued permanently could lead to a negative impact on the world economy."
Whether the Saudi pledge has a longer-term impact on the price of oil remains unclear. But Saudi Arabia holds the biggest reserves among the 11 OPEC members -- and the greatest ability to influence the direction of prices.
An underlying cause of the run-up in oil prices, which have tripled during the last 18 months, was a production cutback agreement that Ali Rodriguez Araque, Venezuela's minister of energy and now the president of OPEC, negotiated early last year with Saudi Arabia and Mexico. But the Saudi statement suggested that the three nation's singleness of purpose may now be fraying.
There were other indications today that the solidarity was weakening. Mexico's energy minister, Luis Tellez, whose country is a major oil exporter but not an OPEC member, called for world producers to cooperate to keep prices at what he called acceptable levels. Speaking in Mexico City, Mr. Tellez said he expected prices to stabilize eventually around $30 a barrel.
Saudi Arabia's pledge contrasted with the tone of the OPEC summit meeting's host, President Hugo Chavez of Venezuela, who has said the cartel is not to blame for the oil inflation that has caused alarm and protests in the main industrialized consumer countries.
The Saudi pledge also overshadowed a call by the OPEC heads of state for a "constructive dialogue" with the industrialized countries to stabilize oil prices.
"OPEC is willing to dialogue at all levels" of government and "with all the countries and regions of the world," Mr. Chavez said in his closing speech this afternoon. But oil prices, he added later at a news conference, are "only a small part of an infinite globality of themes that we need to discuss."
Oil producers and consumer nations are already scheduled to meet in Riyadh, Saudi Arabia, in mid-November to discuss ways to stabilize oil prices. But Mr. Chavez, citing a telephone conversation Wednesday with the prime minister of France, Lionel Jospin, said that an earlier meeting between OPEC heads of state and their counterparts from the rich industrialized nations or the European Union would be useful "if they desire it."
The 11 members of OPEC, who account for 75 percent of world oil reserves and 40 percent of total output, have agreed to increase production three times this year by a total of 3.2 million barrels a day in an effort to halt spiraling prices. But their efforts have met with little success, and President Clinton last week ordered the release of 30 million barrels from the United States strategic oil reserve in an attempt to drive prices down.
The Saudis have traditionally been closer to the United States and more cautious than OPEC members like Iraq and Libya, which are now closely aligned with Venezuela. Mexico must balance its interest in high oil revenue with its fears of an economic slowdown in the United States, its most important trading partner.
The OPEC heads of state attending the meeting here spoke in one voice, however, regarding what they regard as onerous taxes that the governments of consuming countries impose on oil products. "We are unfairly blamed for all the problems of the world economy" stemming from high oil prices, Prince Abdulla complained, adding that consuming countries should "share the sacrifice through the taxes that they impose on oil."
Consuming countries, though, are sure to resist any linkage between a price drop and a reduction of taxes. To ease taxes would not only increase gasoline consumption, and therefore OPEC's revenue, but extend the dependence on fossil fuels that industrialized countries are trying to reduce through the development of alternative sources of energy.
"According to our calculations, a tax cut of only 10 percent by consuming countries would increase OPEC revenues by 100 percent, of course benefiting consumers who are suffering from those high taxes," Mr. Rodriguez said at a news conference this morning. He and other OPEC officials also said it was necessary for industrialized countries, the United States in particular, to increase their oil refining capacity to help brake oil prices.
In addition, a joint communique read at the conclusion of the summit meeting by OPEC heads of state, the first such meeting in 25 years, contained a reference to the group's alarm at international negotiations intended to shape a treaty on global warming. At the latest round of talks, OPEC members insisted that such an accord must include a commitment to compensate them for any decline in the use of oil.
"The dominant attitude in the international negotiations on climate change is bent on driving a stake into the heart of oil production, especially in member countries," Olusegun Obasanjo, the president of Nigeria, complained. Policies "explicitly intended to dramatically reduce the consumption of fossil fuels" mean that "oil exporting countries would stand to lose more than $20 billion of revenue by 2010," he added.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS;
Fidelity Sells Newspapers To Boston Herald Owner

BYLINE: By FELICITY BARRINGER

 BODY:
The owner of The Boston Herald is purchasing the Community Newspaper Company, a group of four daily and 88 weekly newspapers in the Boston suburbs, from Fidelity Capital, the new business and venture capital arm of Fidelity Investments.
The purchase price was not disclosed. 
 The deal gives Patrick J. Purcell, the owner of Herald Media Inc., a regional platform from which to compete with The Boston Globe, particularly in the wealthy suburbs west of the city. The Globe is owned by The New York Times Company, which earlier this year completed its purchase of The Worcester Telegram & Gazette, Massachusetts's third-largest newspaper, for $295 million.
The Telegram & Gazette circulates in many of the same western suburbs where Community Newspapers owns weeklies. The biggest of the four daily newspapers, The MetroWest Daily News, is published in Framingham.
In a conference call yesterday, Mr. Purcell declared, "Let the battle begin."
In the six and a half years since he bought The Herald -- which has a daily circulation of 266,000 -- from his old employer, Rupert Murdoch's News Corporation, Mr. Purcell has impressed industry analysts and New England newspaper executives with his ability to keep The Herald profitable.
The same observers say that Fidelity struggled to get consistent returns on its investment in the newspapers and niche publications that serve 140 communities in eastern Massachusetts.
"In my view, Fidelity has not operated these newspapers well," said Steven Barlow, an analyst with Credit Suisse First Boston.
William B. Ketter, the former editor of The Patriot Ledger in Quincy, Mass., who is now chairman of Boston University's journalism department, said: "I think Fidelity has found that the newspaper business is a very difficult business. They tried the concept of 100 little mice trying to be an elephant, and they realized that it didn't work."
But Mr. Purcell, operating from a strong base in Boston, may be able to find economies and combinations that enhance The Herald's reach and profitability, while increasing the margins of the community newspapers, which have a combined circulation of about 900,000. "I think the purchase of the Fidelity papers by The Herald moves them up a significant notch in competition with The Globe," Mr. Ketter added.
Mr. Purcell said yesterday that "this is an extraordinary revenue-generating opportunity." He added that "the ability to generate ad revenue in this combination is enormous." He also noted that his was by far the biggest community newspaper operation in a single region.
Two other companies have pursued a similar strategy. Pulitzer Inc., the owner of The St. Louis Post-Dispatch, paid $165 million in the summer for Suburban Journals of Greater St. Louis, including 38 newspapers and some niche publications. Hollinger Inc., the parent of Hollinger International, owns Chicago's No. 2 newspaper, The Chicago Sun-Times, as well as the suburban Pioneer Press newspapers.
The announcement of the Community Newspaper sale came on the same day The Globe started its new Thursday GlobeWest section, serving 35 communities in Boston's western suburbs. The Globe has also just finished a redesign, and has recently added new editorial initiatives, including focused weekly business coverage of the biotechnology industry -- a growing part of the economy of the same western suburbs.

HEADLINE: THE 2000 CAMPAIGN: THE TEXAS GOVERNOR;
Giving Praise to Clinton, Bush Says Gore Is Flawed

BYLINE: By FRANK BRUNI

DATELINE: GREEN BAY, Wis., Sept. 28

 BODY:
After more than a year of trying to hang President Clinton around Vice President Al Gore's neck, Gov. George W. Bush took the opposite tack today, saying the main problem with Mr. Gore was that he was not enough like his boss.
In a speech to workers at a manufacturing plant here, Mr. Bush tacitly praised Mr. Clinton's past calls for an end to "big government" and said Mr. Gore was not heeding them. 
 Instead, the vice president had "cast his lot with the old Democratic Party" in proposing a new level of federal spending and regulation that Mr. Bush said would endanger the country's current prosperity.
"The vice president was seated right behind Bill Clinton at the State of the Union when the president declared, 'The era of big government is over,' " Mr. Bush said, referring to Mr. Clinton's address in January 1996. "Apparently, the message never took."
"My opponent seemed interested in reinventing government," Mr. Bush said, alluding to one of Mr. Gore's signature phrases. "Now he seems interested only in expanding it. For him, big government has never really been dead. It's simply been biding its time, waiting for its next chance."
Those comments made up some of the sharpened language that Mr. Bush unveiled today to make the case that electing Mr. Gore might mean a return to "the old ways of tax and spend."
Pressing the point, Mr. Bush called on Mr. Gore to promise unequivocally that he would never raise the payroll taxes for Social Security to keep the entitlement program financially solvent. Mr. Bush has made that pledge.
The references to Mr. Clinton revealed a fresh tactic in Mr. Bush's intrinsically difficult quest to persuade Americans that they should unseat an incumbent administration in a period of unparalleled prosperity.
Mr. Bush was essentially saying that electing Mr. Gore would not be like re-electing Mr. Clinton, because Mr. Gore was less protege than dissident.
"He is proposing the largest increase in federal spending in 35 years, since the presidency of Lyndon Baines Johnson," Mr. Bush said. "The vice president's spending plan proposes three times more in new spending than Bill Clinton proposed in 1992. If the vice president gets elected, the era of big government being over is over. And so, too, I fear, could be our prosperity."
In casting Mr. Gore as financially profligate and trying to plant fears that he might raise taxes, Mr. Bush sounded much like his father did in 1988, when then-Vice President George Bush waged a successful campaign for the presidency against Michael S. Dukakis, then the governor of Massachusetts.
Mr. Bush did not use the word "liberal," which was the tag his father placed on Mr. Dukakis at the time. But that was clearly what the Texas governor meant. And what he intended was to persuade voters that prosperity was a potentially fragile thing and that Mr. Gore was not its surest protector but its direst threat, someone who would not use the projected federal surpluses in the right way.
Mr. Bush began an effort to wrest the issue of prosperity at least partly away from Mr. Gore this week when he warned that the vice president was not taking the necessary steps to end an "education recession" that imperiled the American economy down the line.
Today, he warned that too much new government spending could have the same effect. He said that his own proposal for a sweeping tax cut of $1.3 trillion over 10 years was, in fact, "an insurance policy against an economic slowdown or a recession."
Douglas Hattaway, a spokesman for the Gore campaign, called Mr. Bush's assessments "absolutely laughable."
Mr. Hattaway acknowledged that Mr. Gore's 10-year budget plan called for higher expenditures in "education, health care, Medicare and the environment." But he said Mr. Gore's proposal for an estimated $500 billion in tax cuts over 10 years -- less than half the size of Mr. Bush's -- made that spending affordable.
By contrast, Mr. Hattaway said, Mr. Bush's budget "wastes most of the surplus on a massive tax cut and keeps the nation in debt."
Asked about Mr. Bush's pledge not to raise payroll taxes for Social Security, Mr. Hattaway said, "Did he say, 'Read my lips'? The last time a Bush made a silly pledge like that, he broke it."
Mr. Hattaway said Mr. Gore would not be making a similar vow, because the vice president had already outlined a plan for safeguarding Social Security that did not involve a tax increase.
Mr. Bush mentioned Mr. Clinton twice -- and twice flattered him in comparison with Mr. Gore.
Previously, Mr. Bush had loudly rued the possibility of "four more years of Clinton-Gore" and implied that the vice president was a participant in an administration that, in Mr. Bush's view, was guilty of serious ethical lapses, divisive partisanship and failed leadership.
But today, Mr. Bush described Mr. Gore as an entirely different kind of politician from Mr. Clinton, one who had "left the vital center of American politics" and headed left, leaving behind a moderate group of Democrats that he championed.
"He has claimed to invent many things," Mr. Bush said, referring to that organization, "but he did help invent the Democratic Leadership Council."
 
IN HIS OWN WORDS: GEORGE W. BUSH
Remarks yesterday in Green Bay, Wis., on Vice President Al Gore's spending proposals:
"I'll treat people with respect. I trust them to know their own interests and chart their own course.
My opponent once had some sympathy for these ideas. He has claimed to invent many things, but he did help invent the Democratic Leadership Council. Senator Lieberman is also a member of that council.
 This is an organization of more moderate Democrats who want to modernize Medicare and Social Security. They want to shrink the federal work force and control spending. They want to use market-oriented ideas to help people in education and in health care. I don't always agree with this group, and they certainly don't always agree with me. But they are leaders, and the kind of leaders I will work with should I become the president.
Vice President Gore has cast his lot with the old Democratic Party. His promises throw the budget out of balance. He offers a big federal spending program to every -- to nearly every -- single voting bloc in America. He expands entitlements without reforms to sustain them. And when others, Republican or Democrat, propose needed reforms in Social Security or Medicare or education, he opposes them vigorously, and he attacks them. Just when progress on important issues seemed within reach, my opponent has left the vital center of American politics."

HEADLINE: THE 2000 CAMPAIGN: THE VICE PRESIDENT;
Gore Says Bush Tax Plan Threatens Prosperity

BYLINE: By KEVIN SACK

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 28

 BODY:
Vice President Al Gore said today that "prosperity itself will be on the ballot" on Nov. 7 and then repeatedly portrayed Gov. George W. Bush's proposed income tax cut as a serious threat to the country's economic health.
"That's not tax relief," Mr. Gore said of his Republican rival's $1.3 trillion tax reduction plan. "That's a massive tax on America's potential." 
 Mr. Gore made his comments in a notably brief speech to a group of scholars and staff members at the Brookings Institution, one of Washington's most prominent research organizations. It is a nonpartisan group that conducts research on a broad range of domestic and foreign policy topics.
The vice president made no new proposals during his address, which he read somewhat impassively from a teleprompter while standing at a lectern. But he did introduce new language, and aides said the speech, like other Gore addresses this week, was designed as something of a test drive for Mr. Gore's first debate with Mr. Bush, which is set for Tuesday night in Boston.
If that is the case, viewers can expect to hear a vigorous assault on Mr. Bush's tax cut plan. Mr. Gore attacked the Bush plan five times in his 13-minute address. The candidates' disparate approaches to tax cuts provide perhaps the clearest distinction in this year's campaign, and Mr. Gore has long maintained that the Republican plan risks a return to heavy deficits.
The Bush proposal would reduce all income tax rates, double the child credit, provide a break for married couples, repeal the federal estate tax and expand the deductibility of charitable contributions.
It would reduce federal revenue by $1.3 trillion in the nine years starting 2002 and would use $1.6 trillion of the federal surplus because of increased interest costs that the government would incur if the surplus is used for tax cuts.
The surplus is projected by the Congressional Budget Office to be $4.3 trillion during the same period.
Mr. Gore, by contrast, opposes any across-the-board reduction in tax rates. He has proposed a $500 billion array of targeted tax cuts and credits aimed at providing relief for the middle class. They would include financial breaks intended to encourage retirement savings and to make college tuition, health insurance and child care more affordable.
Mr. Gore would spend more than Mr. Bush on education and health care, and he would devote significant sums to paying down the national debt.
"Consider this fact," Mr. Gore said of Mr. Bush's proposal. "The other side's plan gives more in tax cuts to the wealthiest 1 percent -- $667 billion of the surplus -- than all the new money they would invest in education, health care, prescription drugs and the national defense combined."
He added, "I believe in smart investments for the future, not massive tax cuts for the few that could bring us back into deficit."

HEADLINE: Senators Doubt Progress on Global Warming Plan

BYLINE: By ANDREW C. REVKIN

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 28

 BODY:
Several Republican senators today expressed strong concerns that talks to flesh out details of a global-warming treaty signed by the administration in 1998 would never produce a document that the Senate would approve.
The comments came as Frank E. Loy, undersecretary of state for global affairs, spoke at a joint hearing of the Senate Foreign Relations and Energy and Natural Resources Committees in which he defended efforts to transform the treaty, the Kyoto Protocol, from a broad commitment to stem global emissions of heat-trapping greenhouse gases into a detailed, enforceable plan. 
 The protocol calls for the United States and other industrialized countries to start by 2008 reducing emissions of greenhouse gases like carbon dioxide, which comes mainly from burning coal and oil. More than 150 countries have signed the protocol, but it has not been ratified by any of the world's industrial powers.
Negotiations will resume in November in The Hague on a variety of points, including defining ways for wealthy countries to get credit toward emissions targets by paying for projects in developing countries that help prevent releases of greenhouse gases as those economies grow.
Senator Frank H. Murkowski, the Alaska Republican who is chairman of the energy committee, said important aspects of the treaty were far from the goals the Senate set in a resolution 1997, before the accord was adopted at a Kyoto meeting.
It would still be an unfair burden on the American economy, Mr. Murkowski said, by forcing cuts in the use of coal and oil. And, he added, there appeared to be no requirement for developing countries to limit future emissions. That was a specific demand of in the Senate resolution, which passed on a 95-to-0 vote.
Mr. Loy, the chief negotiator for the United States in recent rounds of climate talks, said the agreement was not, and would not -- even after further talks -- be a complete or perfect solution to global warming. But, he said, it is an essential start, providing relatively cheap insurance against harm if scientists' predictions of the consequences of a warming climate prove correct.
Senator Richard G. Lugar, Republican of Indiana, said there were signs in his state that farmers would support a treaty if they got financial credits for sopping up carbon dioxide through certain farming methods.
